The Red Page
Nostalgia Versos by Will Ogiivie
SYDNEY.
1 Ac green bush mantles your shoulder,
The blue wave washes your feet •
There be greater cities and older,
Hut never a city so sweet.
By gardens sloped to the water,
By clean towers built for pride,
You were born for an Empire's daughter.
And bred for an ocean's bride!
By every sun-browned maiden
1 hat laughs on your low sea-beach,
By your tanned Apollos laden
With all that your long waves teach,
By each clasp in your diamond splendor,
By each kiss in your diamond cove,
You were made for the passionate tender
Embrace and avowal of love! ]
By the banks of your rippling river,
And the eamping-ground in the trees,
You were made for ever and ever
For love and laughter and ease.
For sunlit hours on the water,
For soft hands trailed in the foam,
7? moon on the heeling quarter,
When the white sea-wings come home!
Not a ship that rocks in your fairway,
Not a liner lashed to your quays,
a war-gig chained to your stairway
But has brought from the outmost seas
borne heart that will soon grow tender
lo your charm of beach and grove,
And go sadly forth from your splendor
would leave his love!
BLACK WINGS.
Sextons of the Overland! Buriers of the
dead.
Whose graves are lone and shallow and
winding sheets are red!
Wardens of the waggon track. watchers by
the creek,
Loiterers in the lignum, where the blacksoil
traps the weak!
Feasters at the wayside, guests at the
lagoon,
Gloating over dead sheep rotting in the
noon !
Robbers on the red roads, highwaymen of
Brought,
Settlers of the issue that the dawn has left
m doubt!
Was there ever team-horse from the chains
let go,
Was there ever lean steer lightened of the
bow ,
But your hungry vanguard drifting from the
sky
Croaked beside his shoulder, glad to watch
him die?
Ever tramped our cattle knee-deep in the
grass,
But you soared above them praying Death to
pass?
Ever went our sheep-mobs starvedly and
slow,
But you marked their weaklings stumbling
to and fro?
Ever trod a bushman, tramp or pioneer,
U er the plains of Famine, through the scrubs
of Fear,
But darker than his danger, closer than his
dread,
Shadows on his pathway, flapped ye over-
head?
Call to mind the stock routes north and west
and east! —
Every heap of white bones fashioned you a
feast!
Call to mind the sandhills!—every wrinkled
hide
Made your perch at banquet the day a dumb
beast died!
Surely, at God's muster, when our mobs
again
Trample through the star-grass up the
purple plain,
When from creek and sandhill crowd our
western dead,
He shall suffer only white wings overhead!
CICADAS.
TT hen the Bush is still as death,
And the night wind whispers under her
breath,
And the white stars beckon without a word
And not one leaf of the box is stirred—
Sudden, as though at the baton-fall
Of a hidden leader, the oak-trees break
Into sibilant music, one and all;
And every pine has a harp to wake,
And every gidyea a tune to call.
Then the bridle-path that was dumb and
drear
Rings with an elfin music clear,
And the shimmering starlight wraps us
round
With a cloak of passion, a robe of sound.
\\e pierce the shadows with watchful eyes;
But, however the moon shine bold and
bright,
—owever the weird notes fall and rise,
There is never a sweet-throat singer in
sight
I wix't the sombre earth and the silver skies.
1 is a song of love and a song of pride,
And it swells in a rolling splendid tide,
Till the night with its rosy warmth is lit
And the cold, grey Bush is a-throb with it.
2 hen suddenly swift, as it were a sword
Had flashed from some jealous angel’s
hand,
And severed the music’s silver cord,
Cloaked Silence stoops on the listening
land.
With the broken bowl of her peace restored.
From “The Overlander, and Other
Verses, ’ by Will Ogilvie (Fraser, Asher,
Glasgow).
Haroy
A new volume by Thomas Hardy 1 This
would be in literature an event comparable
only to the discovery of a hitherto unpub-
lished novel by Meredith. But A Changed
Man and Other 'Tales (Macmillan) is not
a new .piece of work. It is but a reprint of
a dozen minor novels that have seen light
in various periodicals. Hardy’s last novel,
Jude the Obscure, appeared in 1895, for The
Well-Beloved, which was published in book
form in 1897, had appeared serially in 1892.
After that Hardy was silent in prose. Of
these minor novels only three were published
since Jude—A Changed Man, The Grave by
the Handpost, and Enter a Dragoon: the rest
represent earlier work, and the best of
them, The Romantic Adventures of a Milk-
maid (1883), was written before The Mayor
of Casterbridge. From such chronological
considerations we ard enabled to discover that
Hardy ceased writing prose simply because
he could no longer write fiction. A Changed
Man is a narrative of matter-of-fact dulness;
The Grave by the Handpost is one of “Life’s
Little Ironies” in which the irony seems un-
necessarily perverse; and in Enter a Dra-
goon the mechanism of the tragi-comedy is too
obviously contrived. In these three late efforts
Hardy’s invention is seen running thin. He
was wise to make his retirement from the
fiction stage.
There is in all these stories a conception
of . I ate that is outrageously pessimistic.
“Life’s Big Ironies” they might have been
aptly entitled. The Waiting Supper, con-
temporaneous with The Woodlanders, tells
of two parted lovers, throughout whose lives
Fate intervenes again and again at the very
moment when their happiness seems assured.
While the Afroman is waiting for the arrival
of her lover, with the supper prepared for
their wedding, news comes that her husband,
supposed to be dead, is on his way to claim
her. But though he has sent his luggage on,
he does not arrive. Nothing is heard of him
thereafter, yet the two lovers are afraid to
carry out the twice-postponed marriage. They
grow old waiting; and then the skeleton of
her husband’s body is found beneath a water-
fall. the trysting-place of the two unhappy
lovers.
“To think,” said Nicholas, “how we visited
him I How we sat over him, hours and
hours, gazing at him, bewailing our fate,
when all the while he was ironically hissing
at us from the spot, in an unknown tongue,
that we could marry if we chose! You
might have married me on the day we had
fixed, and there would have been no impedi-
ment. You would now have been seventeen
years my wife, and we might have had tall
sons and daughters Well—is it still bet-
ter late than never?”
But they are too old to rnarry. “Is it
worth while, after so many years?” she said
to him.
But occasionally he ventured to urge her
to reconsider the case, though he spoke not
with the fervor of his earlier years.
J*
It is clear from these stories that Hardy
could never have been a master of the modern
short story. Nearly all the tales in this
resurrected collection begin cumbrously.
They are mere skeleton plots of novels that
he might have written. Thus one story be-
gins :
We had been talking of the Georgian
glories of our old-fashioned watering-place,
which now, etc The writer, quite a
youth, was present merely as a listener.
±fie conversation proceeded from general
subjects to particular, until old Mrs. H—
whose memory was as perfect at 80 as it had
ever been in her life, interested us all by the
obvious fidelity with which she repeated a
storv many times related to her by her
mother when our aged friend was a girl—a
domestic drama much affecting the life of an
acquaintance of her said parent, one Madem-
oiselle V , a teacher of French, etc.
Lut there is one riovelette, occupying one-
fourth of the book, which, written in 1883,
gives us Hardy at his best. This is The
Romantic Adventures of a Milkmaid, a de-
lightful story in Hardy’s finest vein of tragi-
comedy, colorful, romantic, ironically humor-
ous. The matter-of-fact milkmaid intrudes
upon a mysterious Baron at a moment when
he had determined to take his own life. Her
mere presence lifts the cloud of melancholy
from his mind, and gratefully he offers to
give her any “treat” she wishes. The one
thing she asks is to go to a public ball. The
Baron, by an elaborate scheme, arranges to
take her, gives her lessons in the then new
dance, the polka, provides for her the proper
dress, and escorts her to the ball. On their
return, she changes into her old clothes, and
the Baron thereupon calmly builds a fire and
destroys the gorgeous garments in which he
had arrayed her. But the glimpse into an-
other life that had been thus miraculously
afforded the milkmaid changes her whole out-
look ; and just here the Baron learns with
dismay that the girl has all the while been
engaged to marry an honest lime-burner.
Also, the milkmaid has fallen in love with
himself. Upon these pegs Hardy hangs a pretty
comedy of sentiment. The Baron is the
unwitting cause of the girl failing to go to
the church cm her wedding day. lie does
his best to put things right; but fails—for
the milkmaid now refuses to wed her swain ;
and onlv by a pretty bit of "plotting does the
Baron make her marry the But
this solution, too, is equally futile, for the
milkmaid refuses to live with her husband.
Once again the Baron intervenes, and this
time the romance comes to its predestined
happy ending. Altogether a delightful piece
of sentimental comedy.
The collection will hot reveal Hardy in
any new light, but indubitably it contains a
faithful exposition of Hardy’s conception of
Fate, and here and there one finds examples
of character-drawing and of landscape that
could be done by no hand but that of our
last surviving master of prose fiction.
We should like to see more of More Aus-
tralian Songs for Young and Old (George
Robertson, Melb.), words by Annie R. Ren-
toul, music by Georgette Peterson, illustra-
tions by Ida S. Rentoul-Outhwaite. The
words are occasionally too difficult for little
singers, but frequently, as in “The Boom-
erang, they allow for the action necessary
for laddies. The first verse runs
/ wonder if you've ever seen the boomerang fly
Like a curly caterpillar all across the sky '
O it swoops and out it swoops, and back it
always comes
Right to where it started looping loops
among the gums.
Whoo-oomp !
Back it always comes,
L nless it hits a cockatoo or parrot in the
gums!
The music is pleasantly humorous and
properly simple; and the illustrations are
works of art, full of sprightlv- fancy.
The series of genteel triflings with litera-
ture, entitled Bays with the Great Novelists,
Bays with- the Victorian Poets, also. With
the English Poets, and With the Lyric Poets
(Idodder and Stoughton, per Angus and
Robertson, Sydney) are fully illustrated in
color and nicely printed. The scope of the
works is indicated by the fact that the
great novelists” include only George Eliot,
Dickens and Thackeray, while the “English
poets comprise merely Tennyson, Byron
rpwniog. As gift books, however, they
lulfu their innocuous purpose.
R-e George Lambert’s work in the recent
exhibition of the International Society, Lon-
*-G Athenaeum regards his portrait
or Mr. Cunninghame Graham as “a triumph
ot compact and self-sufficient statement. Its
completeness suggests finality, as though
along that particular road the painter could
hardly progress further.” Iu another por-
trait the critic finds the head “really badly
painted, emerging harsh and flat, without
the subtlety of silhouette which alone could
excuse such a contrast from the elaborate
background. Yet that background is splendidlv
painted in itself. There is no more beauti-
ful pigment in the exhibition.”
Two Humorists
Stephen Leacock, one of our few new
humorists, betrays signs of weariness. Be-
hind the Beyond, and Other Contributions
to Human Knowledge (Lane, per Sydney
Book Club), is brilliant in patches. The title
piece, a burlesque of the modern problem
play, is perfectly done. This sort of thing:
“In this expectant hush, a man in a check
tweed suit walks on the stage ; only one man.
one single man. Because if he had been
accompanied by a chorus, that would have
been a burlesque; if four citizens in toeas
had been with him, that would have been
Shakespeare; if two Russian soldiers had
walked after him, that would have been
melodrama. But this is none of these. This
is a problem play. So he steps in alone, all
alone, and with that absolute finish of step,
that ability to walk as if—how can one ex-
press it? —as if he were walking, that betrays
the finished actor.”
In the other sketches the humor is rather
hectic.
Jt
A great humorist was lost to the world
when circumstances made of James Edmond
a great editor. The mere fact that he
became the wittiest of living editors hardly
makes up for the loss suffered by the thin
ranks of world-humorists. From time
to time, however, James Edmond has escaped
from the leading strings of his editorial
columns, and given us stories and verse
that are unadulterated humor. These the
Platypus Press, Bathurst-street, Sydney, has
collected into a fat volume bearing the title
A Journalist and Two Bears, llis humor
almost escapes classification, §o unexpected
it is, so unhampered by rules and regula-
tions. But it may be defined as the quintes-
sence of pure fun. There is no wit in this
collection, for wit is hard and brilliant and
cold ; this is clean and pellucid humor, genial
and unconfined, the product of a pen that
Hots and rambles at his grotesque will, and
without formulae or method finds humor in
places where no other writer has ever
thought of seeking it before. Yet it would
be wrong to say that Edmond seeks his
humor ; it bubbles up inexliaustively from a
perennial spring; and, as we are mixing our
metaphors without the happy results that
accrue when some of Edmond’s characters
mix their drinks, we may add that his humor
captures his pen and carries it along iiresist-
ibly until the ink is exhausted with laughter-
Some of these stories are tales, others have
no room for any plot, the whole available
space having been pre-empted by humor.
Even his dreams have a grim, purposeless
humor that never comes to other writers.
Here is a slab from one of his dreams. He
found himself descending to the bottom of
Infinity, and he was going down for cen-
turies :
“I walked steadily on at the same pace.
In all the millions of years I had been walk-
ing I had never met anybody, and I knew
somehow that in all Futurity I never would
meet anybody. I never heard anything, either,
and I wms perfectly well aware that I never
would hear anything. I knew that I was
going nowhere except to more passages, each
one just the same as the last, and just the
same as the next. I was aware, somehow or
other, that the ghost who had died before
me was walking along at exactly the same
steady pace as myself in some other passage
countless miles ahead, and that the ghost
who had died just after me was doing his
ceaseless tramp countless miles behind, and
that the ghost who had died after him was
on the spiral staircase, and would get to the
first Passage by-and-bve. I knew, also, that
the ghost behind would never catch up to
me so that we might be two ghosts together
and keep each other company, and I knew
equally well that I would never catch up to
the ghost in front, but neither fact troubled
me to any extent. We would all walk apart
like this for all Eternity, and that fact
whollv failed to interest me. So I went on
walking, and the silence continued in full
blast, and the light at the'end of the passage
was always ahead, and whether there was a
similar lieht behind me I did not know, for
T never looked back. I could have looked back
if it had Struck me to do so, but I never did.”
The trouble is that J-ames Edmond n o s
sesses an imagination. No one would object
to that if Edmond’s imagination was a tame
and grocer-like imagination ; but the imagin-
ation that got loose in these stories is a sort
of super-imagination that isn’t safe with the
ordinary.citizen. The book should be allowed
out only to people with well-oiled risible
faculties. The effect of “The ltow in Our
Boarding House” upon a cprate or a member
of the Stock Exchange would be catastro-
phic. Quite respectable persons in congrega-
tions would be seriously injured by the flying
fragments of an exploded curate who hap-
pened to think of “The City of Eree Drinks”
during matins, while the popping noise of ex-
ploding members of Stock Exchanges, who
glanced at “The Girl in the Turkish Bath”—
the first thing a member of the Stock Ex-
change would glance at —would seriously in-
terfere with the business of the country. By
the way, “The Baseless Fabric of the City
of Free Drinks,” begins in the maddest way
that any respectable story ever began. There
are of places and people in these
stories that remain unforgettable. There was
Bildad, where “The Curse of the Lost
Soul Mine” vegetated :— ■'
“Nothing ever happens at Bildad’s Creek,
or if it does, nobody ever wakes in time to
see it. Now and then, perhaps, a slate is
blown off the roof of the little church —‘the
only building in sight which seems to have
been roofed at all except by accident—but
though the inhabitants as a whole are well-
disposed towards the Christian faith, they
invariably let it lie. Possibly they indulge
a faint hope that it may one day be blown
on again, but this seldom takes place.”
But there is more than humor in this book.
One turns from the brilliance of the riotous
"City of Free Drinks,” the “shriek” that is
entitled ‘“The Wrongs of the Girl in the
Bath,” the delightful account of
Ihe Unspeakable Turk,” and the heroic
story of ‘Barrios, Consul for Galatia”: one
even turns from the “The Autobiography of
a Cannibal,” in which a murderer disposes
of the body by the haopy expedient of eating
him-;—•
“I lived on him for the next fortnight till
he was all gone. I hunted up an old gridiron
and a saucepan, and cooked him in all sorts
° • ) va^s ’. he was best when I fried him
with onions. Roasted he was insipid, and
lie made the worst stew I ever tasted, but
grilled he was not at all bad. I got tired of
him at last, but I kept looking forward to
the time when he would be finished, and
then I would go back to civilisation and
enjoy a club dinner once more, and that kept
me up.”
Apd the subsequent confusion in the mind
of the condemned murderer wdiether it was
himself or the man he had iacorpoi’ated in
luniselt who was going to be executed —we
turn from these outbursts of sheer humor to
the underlying idea in “The Fool and his
Inheritance,” the humorous grimness of “The
Great Experiment,” and that feat of imagi-
nation connected with “The Three Skele-
tons.” Wells, even, could not have worked
out these remarkable hypotheses with more
convincing detail.
A. H. A.

Two Popular Models ®
'here are two popular Winchester .22 Caliber
Repeating Rifles, either one of which gives
first class satisfaction for target shooting or hunting
small game. They are the Model 1903 .22 Automatic
and the Model 1906. The first, as its name implies, is
reloaded by recoil. After its 10-shot magazine is filled
and the rifle once loaded, the only effort required to
shoot it is to aim and pull the trigger. On account of the
ease and novelty of its operation, there is no end of fun
shooting this rifle. It’s the peer of all .22s. The Model 1906
is a handsome, well-made, fine-shooting, smooth-working gun.
It is a handy little piece which has no equal in gun value. It
has a 20-inch, round barrel, fitted with sporting front and ad-
justable rear sights. It shoots .22 Short, .22 Long and .22
Long Rifle Cartridges, which permits the use of light ammu-
nition for target work and heavier cartridges for game shoot-
ing. These rifles are Take-Downs, and come apart easily
and quickly and can be packed in a small compass.
WINCHESTER CARTRIDGES. Whichever one of these
rifles you select, use Winchester make of cartridges in it.
They develop the possibilities of Winchester and other
makes of rifles and revolvers to the fullest degree. Get
the kind with the big W on the box.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
A Sea Picture.
I wandered on the lonely beach to-day.
The sea was grey, the sky not wholly blue.
Above my head the clamorous sea-gulls flew,
And sleekly hovered watching for their prey,
Then outward wheeled, the sun upon their
wings.
I vainly searched the wrack for garnerings,
Yet nought found I than bitter, dead sea
fruit,
Hope’s wreckage washed upon the shifting
sand..
A vague, chill mist ascended from the land,
The sea wind dropped and suddenly was
mute,
And faintly gold, the last pale ray of light
Was flung across the melancholy sea,
Where one ship trailed its black smoke list-
lessly,
And onward crept into the darkening night.
VICTORIA.
G. J.
Sunset on Mount Arapiles.
Arapiies ! Arapiles !
Old mountain of the years
When surges swung with hammer-blows
Against thy basalt tiers:
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
Upon thy bannered brow
The sunset’s glory helio's
God’s Manhood-Message now!
Upon thy crest at sunset-time
Is flashed in lines of fire
This Word of God’s, heart-magical:
“Be strong! Be true! Aspire!”
Be not cast down in bitterness —
80, on these western tiers
God writes Australia’s horoscope
Through all the coming years !
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
Thou mountain of the storm-
When on thy seaward bastions
The breakers swung enorme ;
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
Before Australia came
Up from the depths, thy mountain-head
Was veiled with bannered flame!
When all the golden Wimmera
Lay deep beneath the sea,
God set thee here, inscrutable
In sunset-panoply!
Thou wert an Isle mysterious—
Last of these mountain-tiers
That ring this White Man’s Continent
In these war-sliadowed years !
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
From Kosciusko’s snow
Thou wert the last Lone Island in
Your Archipelago;
Arapiles! Arapiles!—-
’Neath what dim moon and star,
Wert thou the sea-girt resting-place
For storm-birds blown afar?
Ere Athens and Lacedaemon
Had heard the trumpets blow.
Or Greek had drained exultantly
The cup of Yaphio;
Ere Christ came up from Bethlehem
Or Rome owned slave or thrall,
Thou wert the last sea-citadel —
The first Lone Land of all!
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
Whilst Carthage rose, and fell
God flung His sunset radiance
On thy lone pinnacle ;
Arapiles! Arapiles!—
When Suffetes in their pride
Rowed forth of yore to Sicily.
Thy crest was splendor-dyed !
Dodona’s oaks were whispering,
And Delphi’s murmured low,
When thou wert veiled, all-consecrate
With God’s own sunset-glow!
All silver-limbed and beautiful,
Allured by cloud and tree,.
Lone Altar-Isle, what Paphian
Came forth of yore to thee?
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
The sea-depths leap no more;
Beneath thy sunset-panoply
The drums of threshers roar!
Arapiles! Arapiles!
These strong Australian arms
Have filled the golden Wimmera
With villages and farms !
The sleepless mills at Natimuk
And Horsham take their fill,
The ploughshare tames the wilderness
From Dooen unto Nliill!
This is Australia’s heritage—
This golden sea of grain ;
Alas! upon thy mountain-head
I see no thankful fane!
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
They walk in formal shrouds,
And none behold thy mystery—
Thy pageant of the clouds !
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
The wheat rolls wave on wave ,
Who reads thy story cosmical—
Sea-carved on cliff and cave?
When sunset lights thy battlements
With burnished shields of fire.
None see the mighty Over-God — •
Men’s thoughts are in the mire!
Like blind things in thine ocean-depths
. Of yore, they grope in slime ;
I see thine Archipelago
Rise starkly out of Time!
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
Old mountain of the years.
Thou art my pagan altar-place.
With thy sea-sculptured tiers !
Arapiles ! Arapiles !
Upon thy bannered brow
God’s sunset-glory helios
True mankind's message now !
This Word of His, most magical—
“Be strong! Be true! Aspire!”—
Upon thy crest at sunset-time
Is flashed in pillared fire:
A rapiles ! Arapiles !
Lo, on thy western tiers , .
I see Australia's Destiny
Blaze down the coming years!
victoria.
GRANT HERVEY.

How to Address Letters;
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by talcing some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisemenls, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It- is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for ‘•copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 8, 1914.
THE KEY NIGGER.
On a recent day Governor-General Denman,
usually regarded as a rather level-headed
person, despite his cocked hat of office,
offered Australia the proposition that “India
is really the key of the Empire.” It is an
elusive suggestion, and he who pursues it
with a view to putting the salt of definition
on its tail is likely to have a long chase.
If it is meant that Australia and other
white sections of the Empire depend on their
brown brother for land defence, and must
go to irrecoverable pot without him, past
history shows no evidence to that effect. No
Hindu army ever saved London of any other
important white centre, and though the gar-
ish Benjamin Disraeli once called a
handful of turbaned troops to Europe by
way of bringing pressure to bear on Russia,
they didn’t look sufficiently formidable to
terrify anybody. The effect produced by
that handful of warriors was that Britain
was denounced as a Power which had re-
vived the idea of letting colored barbar-
ians loose on a white population—a thing
that hadn’t been seriously attempted since
the day when a depraved Sultan sent the
hordes of Egypt into Greece. If it is pre-
sumed that, should Britain lose India and
some other Power acquire it, that other
Power will thereby get so much money and
such overwhelming fleets that all will be
finished with the Bull Empire—well, the
world has waited a long time for India to
start its career of marine conquest, and it
is still waiting. Furthermore, it has seen
the Lascar at sea, and hasn’t found him
very serviceable. India isn’t much of a
place to borrow money from. It isn’t a key
of empire in the sense that its loss would
seriously interrupt communication, for the
big liners that run from Canada, South
Africa, Australia and Maoriland —the white
dependencies which really count —to Eng-
land have no use for the old place at all.
The site has some value as a source of
Bull’s food supply, and it returns fair in-
terest on a certain amount of Bull capital,
though in this latter respect its future im-
portance is doubtful. As a usurer the mild
Hindu is beginning to learn too much.
* * * *
In the garish early days of John Com-
pany England made itself rich out of India
by plain robbery. Nowadays there isn’t
much robbery. India uses up a large pro-
portion of the scanty white army which may
yet be badly needed for the defence of Eng-
land itself. It is fruitful in border scraps,
and on its frontier was raised the great
Russian invasion bogey which helped to
make many complications in Europe, and to
muddle Britain into disastrous alliance with
the Unspeakable Turk. It has furnished,
incidentally, many costly interior wars, also
the great Mutiny. It supports less than
100,000 British people, apart from soldiers,
and the British population is declining. It
isn’t in the same street, nor even in the
same town, with Australia or Canada, as a
shipowner. If India is a key it is a badly
discolored one, and considering how small
a hold Bull has on the motley tinted Em-
pire the key is in dubious hands. The day
when foreign complications necessitate the
withdrawal of the bulk of the scanty white
force Hindustan promises to be the burial-
place of a heap of British prestige. There
is glamor and pomp and legend about the
country of the Moguls and the Mahrattas.
It is a big place, and contains much scenery
and many curious faiths and quaint
old temples and mysteries and gods and
snakes and fakirs and materials for Kip-
lingesoue romance, and many notable battle-
fields lie within its borders. But these things
don’t of necessity make it pay as a White
Man’s Burden.
* * * *
The alleged key has become more than
usually troublesome of late. It stands for
the biggest, noisiest and most dissatisfied
colored element in an empire that is mostly
colored. It even stands for more than half
the population of the Empire. It has been
taught a lot of British history and many
British theories of government, and as
some of it has a superficial capacity for
European knowledge it thinks this know-
ledge gives it the right to snread its people
over all other parts of the Empire. It begins
to talk likewise of its right to self-govern-
ment, which can hardly be refused for all
time unless John Bull burns “Mill on
Liberty,” and cuts the history of Charles
I. out of the Indian school books. _ And it
begins to hint very broadly that it is absurd
to suppose that the biggest half of the Em-
pire’s population will lje content for ever
without a share in the control of the Em-
pire, which control would signify the end of
Bull as a great white imperial proposition.
Under British training the land of Hind
threatens to become too big to be held down,
and it is certainly too big to be let up.
A man with an ordinary elephant may be
boss, but a man with an elephant to which
he has imnarted the secrets of how to be
boss is in danger of being trodden upon. At
present Bull shows a tendency to compro-
mise. He isn’t sure that he is strong enough
to sit down hard on his too-much educated
and too multitudinous nigger, nor will his
Nonconformist conscience quite allow him
to do so. He isn’t prepared to let his nigger
sit on him. But he is half-inclined to split
the difference by proposing that the nigger
shall be permitted to sit on dependencies
like South Africa and Fiji and Mauritius
and perhaps Australia, as something to go
on with. This is rough on these countries
which have never gained anything by the
Indian connection, but then Bull is up
against a difficult situation.
Our colored brother of the Ganges is com-
plicated He owns one of the richest coun-
tries on earth, and it is so thinly peopled
that it can not only feed itself but also
show a great surplus of food for export.
Still the Hindu, by reason of his primitive
hand-power methods, remains deadly poor.
So he yearns to swarm away from his own
country and become rich abroad, as his
ideas of riches go. When he strikes a coun-
try like Fiji, or Natal, or Mauritius, where
a fairly numerous white community has in-
troduced improved methods, he is capable
of assimilating these methods —so long, at
least, as he has the white man to copy from
—to a certain extent. He becomes a bloated
capitalist, as he understands bloated capital,
and multiplies like a rabbit. A't the same
time, though he earns large wages, as com-
pared with the standards of his own coun-
try, while acting as a parasite on the white
man’s civilisation, he is so cheap according
to European standards that he immediately
begins to elbow the white man out. He is
doing that in India, though there, of course,
the European settler never had any real
hold. He is doing it in Fiji and Mauritius,
where the European thought himself fairly
’■'-'ll anchored. He is beginning to do it in
Natal, where the European had reckoned
himself for very many years lord of the
soil. Presumably when he has completely
eHen up the white man’s industrial system
in these places he will sink to his old Hindu
level. He will be like the white ant which
has finished eating its tree. There is no
reason why, left to his own efforts and with-
out a higher order of things to prey upon,
he should do any better in Natal or Fiji or
Mauritius than in his rich old peninsula,
especially considering that many of his best
achievements there have been due to Euro-
pean influences. The complaint against the
Indian immigrant is that, wherever he has
gone, he has introduced a lower, dirtier,
worse ventilated end worse drained type of
civilisation, and has begun to elbow out a
better civilisation by sheer cheapness. He
has an infernal capacity for always moving
to countries where there is a higher culture
which he can devour. He never goes where
there is only a meaner race to be elevated.
Aboriginal Africa or Australia or Polynesia
had no attractions for him. Pioneering was
never in his line.
India to-day is in a special ferment be-
cause it isn’t allowed to consume such white
progress as has been built up in South
Africa. Its clamor about its alleged right
to spread itself wherever it feels inclined,
and the wobbling attitude of British politi-
cians who are afraid to say one thing or
another, threatens to seriously undermine
the loyalty of the South African white popu-
lation. India thus becomes a conundrum.
If the gentle brown man gets even half
what he demands in the one matter of his
claim to pervade the Empire, there will pro-
bably be very little empire left. If he
doesn’t get it he will be too dissatisfied to
live with, and if he is temporised with he
will be too inflated to be manage-
able. Meanwhile much of the worry of
British foreign policy consists in the
struggle to protect the sea route that leads
to this disgruntled dependency. Gibraltar
and Cyprus and Egypt and Aden all repre-
sent cne costly phase or another of the effort
to prevent Ram Dass’s postal communica-
tion with the land he so greatly reviles be-
ing cut off. Taking all things into account,
India might make a good Christmas present
for an enemy.
STATE RIGHTS IN DISEASE.
Tiie chief obstacle in the way of dealing
promptly and effectively with preventable
diseases in Australia is the identical old
obstacle which gets in the road of uniform
railway gauges, monorails, the drastic con-
trol of food trusts, decentralisation of ports,
immigration, and most of the other things
which the country needs urgently. It is
an obstacle called State Rights by persons
with plenty of time to be polite; but those
who butt up against it in the course of a
rush of work designed for the public good
call it by other and mostly unprintable
names. With one important exception, the
work of protecting Australia against dis-
ease is in the hands of the States. The
exception is the small group of diseases
which are dealt with by the Federal Quar-
antine Acts, and which comprise cholera,
plague, small-pox, typhus fever, and, to a
limited extent, leprosy. It is an important
exception, although all these diseases put
together have not slain in the whole history
of Australia nearly as many persons as con-
sumption and syphilis kill in a single year.
Prior to 1909 each State had its own
Quarantine Act, just as it now has its own
Health Act, and it ran its quarantine in
any old way it pleased, with any sort of
ramshackle staff and buildings. When an
outbreak of, say, small-pox, occurred in one
State, all the others proceeded to enforce
varying degrees of isolation against that
State as a whole. During the Tasmanian
outbreak of small-pox in 1903, for example,
N. S. Wales was admitting passengers from
Hobart, whilst Victoria was jambing them
into quarantine if they could not show re-
cent vaccination marks. As Victoria did
not trouble itself about train traffic at
Albury, this only necessitated a roundabout
trip for those who wanted to avoid vaccina-
tion, and did not protect Victoria. State
administration of quarantine was also apt
to be marked by a tendency to release dis-
tinguished but unvaccinated travellers of
the uppah-suckle variety, before they had
finished —or even begun—their spell in quar-
antine. To do the State Health Depart-
ments justice, this was not all their fault.
Public health has no definite political
value such as a pleased distinguished visitor
may have ; State public health officials hold
their jobs on very uncertain tenures; and
the average Minister of Health is not the
most brilliant of the bunch. He has plenty
of power to interfere if he wants to, and
about the best that can be expected from
him is that he will have sufficient sense to
refrain from exercising the power of inter-
ference in a technical job such as this. Since
1909 the Federal Quarantine Service has
run matters of sea quarantine on a uniform
and systematic basis, and it is getting to be
high time that some similar efficiency of
control and direction should be secured for
other varieties of poverty-making and death-
dealing disease.
The various State Health Acts are
mostly inadequate, and very uneven at best.
Those of Victoria and New South Wales
are hopelessly out of date. The administra-
tion is just as uneven, and from the prac-
tical working standpoint, also out of date.
Only one State has made any honest at-
tempt to deal with venereal diseases, and
even there the work has been hampered by
the political interference of legislative sen-
ility on the one hand and of wowserism on
the other. Consumption kills over 3000
Australians every year. Its practical con-
trol is mostly left to the municipalities. Now
and then the State Health Departments
deliver a fresh set of platitudes on the sub-
ject of street spitting or tuberculous milk ;
but, with the possible exception of Victoria,
they do little or nothing of any real value
in checking the direct spread- of infection.
Typhoid fever accounts for some 700 Aus-
tralians annually, besides disabling a vastly
greater number for many weeks at a time.
Both it and consumption are diseases
especially of the working periods of life, a
feature which increases enormously their
powers of mischief in a working country
such as ours. Despite the recent discovery
of a vaccine which protects for years against
typhoid, with little or no unpleasant result
at the time of inoculation, no organised
effort has been made by any State to place
it within the easy reach of those who live
or work in the numerous typhoid centres of
the back country. Even such a simple and
straightforward business detail as the uni-
form labelling of food products under the
Pure Food Acts has taken two conferences
of Federal and State officials, and miles of
correspondence, to settle. At this rate of
progress inter-State uniformity of effort
against, say, syphilis, may be expected about
the time that the Little Boy from Manly
grows white whiskers and rheumatics.
Without some approach at uniformity of
direction, operations against disease are
about as effective as military operations
against an organised invader would have
been in the good old days of State volun-
teers and softgoods political colonels. This
does not imply a dark Socialistic intention
to Federalise back yards and dead animals
and choked drains. Consumption, typhoid
and venereal diseases are national questions,
and State control is evidently a failure so
far as they are concerned. It is a failure
for the simnle reason that it brings in no
revenue, and is difficult to use for purposes
of keeping a party in office. In fact, public
health matters are so closely and adversely
related to the vested-interest question that
the average politician of the State brand
would sooner run a mile—and he is seldom
in good training for that sort of exercise—-
than get himself mixed up with them. The
United States have been faced with the same
problem of local inefficiency and apathy
in respect of diseases which are capable of
causing national disaster, or at best of
working untold harm in the way of poverty-
production. They have met it by the estab-
lishment of a United States Public Health
Service, controlled only by the National
Government; but this organisation can only
onerate in a State if it is invited to do so.
The Australian Constitution includes Quay-
antine as a specified field of Federal acti-
vity, and the definition of quarantine
is a very wide one indeed. Some day, when
the noise of scrapping for office has died
down sufficiently to allow a little horse-sense
to be heard, there will be a fine opportunity
for some Government to establish an Aus-
tralian Health Service, and to deal effec-
tively with some of the larger and more
important features of national lmalth-pro-
tection. The machinery is already there,
and the States are not likely to howl very
Ludly if they are asked to give up some of
the health duties which they do not perform
and certainly do not want.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Importing Workers While Exporting
Work.
When the very deliberate inter-State Coin-
mission lias finished —if it ever does finish
—inquiring into the “import” side of Pro-
tection, it will find gallons of work before it
in looking into the “export” side of the
matter. Fellmongering is one item of this
“export” business. In 1884 Geelong had
28 fellmongeries, employing over 500 per-
sons, paying goo_d wages and yielding satis-
factory profits. To-day most of the 28 are
closed, and those that still struggle on do so
with difficulty. About 1884, French, Ger-
man and Belgian skin-buyers cairie nosing
round and bought a few skins, and no one
took much notice; but in course of time
they perceived that they werd' on a good
wicket. By 1904 they took away from the
Commonwealth 4,338,962 sheepskins, besides
the hides of many bulkier animals. By
1909 they had increased their purchases of
sheepskins to 10,021,081; by 1910 to
11,323,959. They paid the market price for
them, of course, and so did not directly im-
poverish the Commonwealth; but the Aus-
tralian fellmonger —manufacturer as well as
employee—found himself up against trouble.
The foreigner took his skins home at no
greater cost for freight than the Geelong
man paid to send his fellmongered goods to
Melbourne or Sydney; and having taken the
raw goods to his own country, the foreigner
manufactured them there for about a third
as much as it cost the Geelong fellmonger,
and sent the manufactured goods back, or
sold them to his own people or their neigh-
bors. As the Geelong man was paying far
higher wages than the foreigner, and work-
ing his employees shorter hours, he simply
couldn’t outbid the foreigner for his raw
material, and he couldn’t get enough skins
at the ruling rates to fill his orders. So
he mostly shut up shop. To-day he is asking
for an export duty of Id. per lb. on raw
sheepskins, and his moderation is almost
amazing. To accede to his request would
hurt no living soul in the Commonwealth,
except the very occasional foreign skin-
bnyer. But it would do an immense amount
of good : it would ensure to Australia what
it is fully entitled to —the job of partly
manufacturing some of its own raw pro-
ducts. The foreigner must buy, and if he
is compelled to buy the finished leather in-
stead of the raw sheepskins, so much the
more work and the more wages and the more
population for the country whose interests
are the inter-State Commission’s first and
last and only consideration.

An Old-World Anomaly on Australian
Soil.
It was significant that the papers which
fought the Australian Defence movement to
the last ditch should have been the ones
to acc’aim most loudly Prime Minister
Cook’s decision to retain the kilt in the
Australian army. On a similar principle
Cook might agree, later, to the institution
of an Ulster regiment for militant anti-
liomanists ; or to a corps composed entirely
of members of the Sydney Pioneers’ Club;
or to any other tragic folly of the kind. By
encouraging Australian soldiers to garb them-
selves in kilts, sporrans, feathers and such-
like the Government is foisting on the
Commonwealth abuses that the mili-
tary authorities in the United Kingdom are
never done deploring. For every ounce of
good that these distinctions between regi-
ment and regiment do in Britain, the very
home of them, they accomplish about a ton
of harm. That is an axiom amongst
those experts who aim at homogeneity in
the service. The Rifle Brigade has the
privilege of being allowed to march at a
ouicker pace than any other regiment;
the Buffs may traverse London with their
arms held at a peculiar angle; the Welsh
Fusiliers are permitted to wear at the back
of their necks the cloths which were in
vogue in the tie-wig period—one might go
on indefinitely citing the queer, purposeless
privileges which have been granted to vari-
ous British regiments. One. effect is to
produce acute class distinctions between
the bodies of men concerned. In a country


where class distinctions prevail overwhelm-
ingly in every walk of life, and in no class
more than the military one, such things are
less disastrous than they are bound to be
if they get a footing in the Commonwealth.
It is the desire of every patriotic Australian
that the local army shall be completely
democratic, inasmuch as the moment it be-
comes otherwise it must inevitably fall into
public disfavor. At present the different
regiments are distinguished by their numbers
alone. The son of a millionaire squatter or
merchant is liable to find himself in the
ranks side by side with the son of a bottle-
gatherer or other humble citizen ; and their
officer may be the bottle-gatherer’s son’s elder
brother; which, under such a training system
as ours, is as it should be. Within a very
short time of the introduction of Scotch,
Irish or other bogus overseas regiments the
human-nature proposition will begin to assert
itself, and the snobs of the community will
rush the gaudier corps. They will do it not only
because of the latter’s shadowy association
with the United Kingdom, but because when
guards of honor and so forth are required,
the garish uniform of the imitation overseas
regiment will be bound to be preferred to. the
drab garments of the frankly local warriors.
A surer way of engendering ill-feeling in the
service could not well be devised. The idea,
in this country, is that a soldier should take
pride not in the fact that he is permitted to
wear green trousers, or none at all, but rather
in the circumstance that he wears the num-
ber of the smartest and most efficient regi-
ment in the land. There is no “loyalty in-
volved in hanging on to the kilt, as Sydney
Telegraph —which bracketed the 1 isher
Government’s banishment of that rather ab-
surd garment with its eradication of the
King’s head from the postage stamp—tried
hard to make out. It is as idiotic to associate
loyalty and kilts as it is to connect loyalty
and top hats. By reintroducing the ancient
Scottish covering the futile Cook Government
has done something that will not edify the
Heart of Empire worth 2d., but. will
certainly produce a lot of most undesirable
derision, annoyance, contempt, anger and the
like amongst the local forces.. In brief, it has
acted as a congenitally Anti-Defence Admin-
istration might have been expected to act.

The Law and a Blind Eye.
Tiie arrest at Omeo (Vic.) of a man
alleged to be John H. Sparks, who escaped
from Pentridge gaol in 1901, lets a large
problem loose. Sparks was sentenced in
1899 to 10 years’ hard labor, along with
one Negus, his mate, in a case of robbery
under arms; but, finding prison life weari-
some, he, with one O’Connor, scaled the
walls on a morning in July and faded away
into a heavy fog. O’Connor was recap-
tured a few weeks later in Sydney; but
Sparks was a bushman, and apparently got
right out of the cities. Now, it happened
that the constable largely instrumental in
Sparks’s arrest was transferred lately to
Omeo, and there he recognised him, or at
any rate thought he did. Of course the
Omeo man averred he knew nothing about
Sparks or his doings, and could give an ac-
count of himself and his goings and comings
for the last 10 years—and he turned out not
to be Sparks at all. But supposing he had been
that enterprising person, what then? The
lately-accused is a married man with four
young children; he has lived at Omeo for
about 10 years, and is well and favorably
known throughout the district. If he had
been Sparks, his brief taste of gaol had
obviously achieved its intent; he had become
an honest, useful citizen, with responsibili-
ties which he is fulfilling satisfactorily.
Why, then, if no mistake had been made,
send him back to gaol? He was doing
well; and would more gaol be at all
likely to make him do any better?
The difficulty is that our system hasn’t
yet made up its mind as to the pur-
pose of gaol. What is it —punishment or
reformation? If it is punishment, and if the
punishment was well fitted to the crime in
the first place, then plainly John H. Sparks
hasn’t had enough. But if it is reformation,
then it seems quite plain that he had had
just enough (that is if the two men had
been identical), and any more might have
spoiled the whole effect. But getting back to
punishment, what is that for? There is clearly
no sense in it except for two things—(l)
it may be a means of reforming the offender
or at any rate of keeping him from offending
again; (2) it may deter others from offend-
ing. As to its first purpose, the punish-
ment Sparks took seems to have had its
proper effect, for he has not fallen into the
hands of the police in 12 or 13 years, and
the further punishment which he escaped
might quite easily have made him a much
worse man; while, if punishment is to be
viewed as a deterrent, who is going to be
deterred from robbery-under-arms by the
arrest of a man who escaped from gaol 12
years ago and had been forgotten by every-
body but one or two officials? Admittedly,
apart from this case altogether, it is a
difficult problem. It won’t do either to
encourage prisoners to attempt escape, or
to foster the idea that when they have
escaped they will not be re-arrested even
if they have meantime behaved themselves.
But when men have escaped so long ago
that their exploit has been forgotten, and
when their present conduct is so exemplary
that they are plainly in no need of further
correction, the Law that lays hands on
them and drags them back to confinement
doesn’t seem to this paper to know the good
use that can sometimes be made of a blind
eye.

The Pot Reviles the Kettle Which is Not
Quite so Black.
In railing at the transitory Victorian Labor
Ministry which was rushed into a false posi-
tion by the intrigues of its Tory foes. Mel-
bourne’s one-eyed Tory journal practically
offered an excuse for the tactics it con-
demned. Said the Argus :
A Cabinet less desperately anxious to hang on to
office until the last possible moment would have
resigned forthwith. These political adventurers make
great professions of disinterestedness, but the truth
is too obvious to be disguised They would dearly
like to be left in office during the many weeks which,
at this season, must intervene before the electors
could bid them begone.
As a matter of fact, the anxiety of Labor-
Premier Elmslie to draw Ministerial salary
during the weeks when political business
would be suspended was no more glaring than
the eagerness of ex-Premier Watt to do the
same thing. Both parties were playing the
game of party politics for the sweets of
office. The tactics of a third party had sud-
denly put the Elmslie lot in by putting the
Watt lot out, and when that third party as
suddenly put Watt back it did so on condi-
tions which Watt subsequently rejected. But
that made no difference to Watt —he went
ahead just the same as if he had got the
Tory party’s support by including McLeod
and his friend. On the other hand, Elmslie
did at least make an attempt to hasten the
dissolution of a highly unsatisfactory Assem-
bly, which dissolution a vast majority of the
electors would welcome as a possible means
of putting one or other well-defined party
in absolute power. Under present conditions
no well-defined party can be in power. Elms-
lie can do nothing, and Watt is likely to be
forced to resign again as soon as he tries
to do anything. He has, in short, merely
deprived Elmslie of the privilege of getting
paid for doing nothing of political import
which is the privilege that the Cook Govern-
ment has also enjoyed in the Federal Parlia-
ment for the past eight months, and is the
same privilege that the Watt Government
is likewise going to enjoy for as long as it
can stave off the re-assembling of Parlia-
ment.
Now that the Referenda are safely out of
the way the truth may he told. This is
from the S. M. Herald of a week ago:—
Few people other than those actually engaged in
the trade have any idea how wholesale prices for
dairy produce are fixed. Perhaps it is just as well
that this is so.
At the beginning of the year the produce selling
agents of Sussex-street decided that the grocer should
pay the cost of the butter box, and also incidentally
that he should pay cartage on all dairy produce. An
agreement was accordingly drawn up which set form
that from a certain date butter boxes would be
charged for at the rate of lOd. each or Is. Bd. per
cwt. if the produce' was taken delivery of in bussex-
street, but that if it had to be forwarded to the
suburbs cartage at the rate of 4d. per cwt., or 2d.
per box, would be charged. ,
In due course the boxes were debited to the grocer,
who in turn passed the extra cost on to the con-
sumer, with, it is said, a little extra added for
himself. Taking the Sydney consumption at about
8000 boxes per week, this extra charge works out
at something like £20,000 per annum.... The price
of butter in Sydney is fixed by the general manager
of one of the big houses, acting in conjunction with
two other gentlemen....
They are monopolists,' and monopolists are aan-
Although the butter trade is by far the largest and
most important in the dairying industry, the position
of other lines at times tends towards the conditions
obtaining in the butter world.
It just shows how these Rings and Combines
grow. As we all know, there were none
when the Referenda were taken a few
months ago.

Fitzpatrick, Chairman of the Victorian
Railway Commissioners, has lately been to
Europe and America, collecting evidence of
the wrong things that are done and the
right things that are left undone by the
railways of this easy-going country. But
no rapid reform of obsolete railway methods
is exnected of Fitzpatrick. He is not
built that way. Moreover, the majority of
the State’s customers don’t know how badly
they are treated in some respects, and are
therefore meek and uncomplaining. They do
not know, for instance, how much trouble
in regard to personal luggage could be
spared them if the Department were not in
league with Private Enterprise. Bad old
Private Enterprise in Victoria has a mono-
poly of all the luggage-carrying business
which in Britain is conducted by the rail-
ways themselves. Trunk, bag and parcel
handling that ought to be well and cheaply
done by the Department for the conveni-
ence of travelling citizens and the good of
the State is badly and expensively done by
private-enterprise firms whose names are
household curses in every suburban home.
The extortionate carrier, never calling for
the dress-baskets when he is most wanted,
is a horrible consideration whenever holiday
trips are talked about. Not only does the
Railway management permit Private En-
terprise to undertake the conveyance of
luggage; it even penalises anybodv who
sends a nortmanteau hv train instead of hv
earrier from one metropolitan suburb to
another. A man travelling from, say, Mel-
bourne to South Yarra can take a con ole
of bags with him in the carriage. The
Railway Department carries him and his
luggage for 3d. But if he wants to send the
same luggage to South Yarra station to be
called for. the charge is not less than a
shilling. What excuse could Chief Railway
Commissioner Fitzpatrick offer for this
policy?
“Winika’S” view of the earlier closing of
pubs:—
One E. L. Slade Mallen lately announced him-
self to the public through the Herald as “a keen
student of the liquor problem,” and then proceeded
to eat up more or less valuable space with a refuta-
tion of the flattering statement. In urging the
earlier closing of hotels in Australia, E. L. Slade,
etc., pointed to the 10 o’clock closing of the bars in
Maoriland as an example to be followed in the
Commonwealth in order to reduce dr in Jcinff. Any
one conversant with the operation of the scheme in
the Shickery Isles knows that, instead of the 10
o’clock rule lessening the consumption of liquor,
it has increased it. I know. I was there when it
was commenced; I have studied it on the spot for
years, with special opportunities for doing so. In
Wellington, for instance, when the No-license crowd,
with the powerful vote of the moderate drinker
(known then and since as the “protest” vote —pro-
test against Bung’s greed and other things), swamped
the Licensing Committee with its nominees (who
were pledged to 10 p.m. closing), great was the
lamentation of the Man Behind the Pump. But he
wailed out of his turn. The whole thing worked
something like the daylight-saving device. Instead
of the pubs’ customers commencing to beat round
town at about the usual hour of 8 p.m., they got a
move on at 7 or 7.30, and, reckoning that 10 p.m.
would be round “before a Bloke could get a few in,”
they hustled their drinking. Result: More business.
You can nearly always tell a visiting Maorilander
in Sydney or Melbourne by the increased pace of
his drinking (provided he is drinking) about 9 p.m.
He’s beating the clock! Mallen is at liberty to
doubt my word that the consumption of liquor has
increased in M.L. since 10 a m. became the fashion
in bar-closing, but if he doubts the word of the
official compilers of the drink bill I’ll know exactly
what to think of him. Here’s another phase of the
scheme. Men, particularly young men, get far more
liquor in the clubs and in private after 10 o’clock
than they would if the public bars were open till 11.
No; don’t all yell at once —I know that the clubs
iu M.L. come under the Licensing Act, and that,
therefore, their bars close at the same hour as the
hotel bars. (Even Parliament House refreshment
bar is no exception.) But it is here that the per-
nicious evil of keeping your own bottle starts to get
in its fine work. I’ve stood up many times to half
a dozen young bloods, each with his particular bottle
nice and handy (and it was almost always spirits)
in the main cities and lesser towns, and
the simpletons who theorise about reducing
the retail selling hours can take it from a
somewhat case-hardened citizen that it re-
quired some strength of character and more dip-
lomacy to keep sober on those occasions. I’m
convinced that the more restrictions you place upon
the liquor business the more will drinkers take their
supplies beyond the reach of harassing laws and
uncomfortable conditions. That’s where the anti-
public bar flock make the mistake. If they were to
barrack for ideal State refreshment places—-gardens
with promenades, bars, restaurants and music, open
to all, where there would be no feeling of sneaking
in and no repugnance to taking women and children
—they might get somewhere near that point which
they’ll never reach the other way. Meanwhile, re-
duction of liquor-selling places in a few suburbs,
or the reduction of hours of trading, or such-like
tinkering with a question that can be finally solved
only by education, is futility on its last legs.
The wickedness of being found out lias
lately been discoursed upon by Archbishop
Clarke at Melbourne Anglican Cathedral.
His remarks were incidental to the question
whether the Church ought to consent to
marry divorced persons :
No person who had passed through the Divorce
Court would receive the Church’s blessing on a second
marriage except that person were wholly innocent.
He wished to speak of his own personal experience
in this matter. In some cases he had consented to
the remarriage of divorced persons, but he had
learned the sad lesson that Divorce Court evidence
was never to be trusted. In the future he would
have to exercise greater care, and he felt he would
rarely be able to sanction the marriage of persons
who had passed through the Divorce Court.
The Church, on Archbishop Clarke’s own
showing, is always prepared to bestow its
blessings and sacraments on any social sin-
ner who hasn’t been found out. The crime
before Heaven is in the exposure. The widows
and widowers who might have passed through
the Court had they been less careful are in
a position to undertake further matrimony
under blessing of the Church. Also, the
spinsters and bachelors who studied for the
co-re. degree, but did not get it. Any of
these can be joined together in a first or
second marriage. The Church, indeed, in-
vites them cordially to come along and be
blessed. But if they had not contrived to
avoid being found out, the Church would de-
clare them disqualified from getting married
at all. Thus sin is not really a thing that
the Devil tempts people to; it is something
that the neighbors and a private detective
bring to light. And when it is brought to
light and the sinners ask permission from
Archbishop Clarke to get married and sin
no more —well, the scandalised prelate up-
lifts his fat white hands and exclaims,
“Heaven forbid !”

From “J.D.5.,” an English leather expert,
at present in Australia :
When Australia does not produce the best it is
important to know why. Now, the best Australian
leather is not, and never has been, equal to the
best English leather. The proof is that a higher
price is paid for the latter by the large boot manu-
facturers and the large users of driving belts, who do
not pay for sentiment.
There are only three possible reasons: (1) The
quality of the raw hides; (2) the preparation or
tanning of the hides, and (3) the question of drying.
Let us look at them in a row. (1) It cannot be
this, because some of the very best leather imported
from England is made from Australian hides. (2)
I do not believe it is this either, because in every
tanner’s yard of importance in this country, either
the proprietor or the manager, or both, were trained
in England, and know all that is known of the
chemistry and practice of tanning. (3) There re-
mains, then, only the question of drying; and it is
here that the cause of failure will be found.
It is not infrequent to find in Australia the tem-
perature by the dry-bulb thermometer anywhere
between 85° and 100°, while the wet-bulb ranges
between 60° and 75°. These great differences in the
wet- and dry-bulb readings mean that the air is very
dry, containing only (say) 27 per cent, to 30 per
cent, of moisture. Now, in England, the percentage
of moisture rarely falls below 65, and for months
together is in the neighborhood of 90. This greater-
moisture gives better drying; hence the tanning has
a chance to do its work, the color is more
even, and the leather is stronger and more pliable.
In Australia the great dryness of the atmosphere
brings out the tanning to the surface too quickly,
discolors the leather, particularly at the edges, and
renders it brittle and for many purposes almost
useless.
When I was a lad the old tanner at home used
to say that “leather should not be dried at a
higher temperature than 70°.” He was neither scien-
tific nor observant, which is much the same thing,
or he would not have blamed the temperature. Most
of his leather was spoilt or discolored when the
cold dry east winds of March were blowing, proving
conclusively that the dryness of the air caused the
trouble.
The modern tanner in England has thus an advan-
tage over the Australian in the greater natural
moisture in the air. But to-day he is not satisfied
with this advantage, mainly because it is slow and
uncertain; hence he resorts to scientific drying. He
dries all kinds of leather in a temperature round
about 100° ; but he puts no trust in the old weather-
boards and the “varying winds of Heaven.” He
scientifically controls the quantity of air he requires,
its temperature and its humidity, and these are the
reasons for the superiority of his leather.
Tf the tanners in this country mean to get ahead,
and do the work I think they ought to do, they must
go and do likewise. Indeed, the natural weather
conditions being less favorable than those of Eng-
land, there is far more reason why they should pro-
duce’ the requisite artificial conditions. I need
hardly add that the requisite artificial conditions are
as easily produced here in Australia as anywhere
else.
From a coastal skipper :
Among the various things shrieking to be made
uniform —from the railway gauge downwards —is one
whose standardising would cost practically nothing.
That is the tide and dredge signals. We have the
system of buoyage that all civilised countries have
adopted ; but in the matter of tide and dredge sig-
nals each little State has its own code, and some-
times more than one. The inter-State skipper has to
keep in his head half a dozen different sets of signals,
with the chance of getting them mixed at a most
inopportune moment. For instance, a Queensland
dredge shows a red flag on the side you’ve to pass
it. South Aus. has a complicated system by which
a red flag means pass on one side and a blue flag
pass on the other going into port. Of course, it’s
all turned round coming out. The Victorian sticks
up a thing like a Soccer football. There’s the same
diversity in the lights they show at night. As for
tide signals, a red light means flood tide in one State
and ebb tide in another, while a third prefers
green lights. To indicate the depths of water, each
Marine Board —Marine Department, Navigation De-
partment, or whatever the local authority happens
to call itself—follows its own inclination. It would
be a simple matter to have one system for all Aus-
tralia, to the saving of confusion ; and confusion
means danger. The inter-Stater who pokes in and
out anywhere from Cairns round to Geraldton has
enough to do remembering the 20 or so ports he s
“exempt” for, without piling on to him unnecessary
differences in this matter of signals; and, some day,
when he forgets one or two of the unnecessary things
he ought to remember, a few people will be drowned.
Thus “Sparker” :
I want to hail “Shipmate” (B. 25/12/T3) and
ask him how much faith he reposes in the wireless
apparatus for which he clamors. A little general
knowledge of the subject would quickly dispel the
illusion from which the public suffers that the
spark is sure to bring assistance when the time comes
to get the women and children into the boats.
Although the 700 survivors of the Titanic undoubt-
edly owe their lives to wireless, the 1600 non-survi-
vors owe their fate to the mismanagement of the
wireless organisation. The nearest ship to the scene,
although fitted with Marconi’s apparatus, was only
fitted with one operator, who was asleep at the
time —even wireless operators cannot keep awake
day and night for 255. a week —and while he slept
the sleep of the underpaid, the Titanic passengers
perished. This happened in the North Atlantic,
where wireless-fitted ships are thicker than State
Governors in Australia. Apply the lesson to our
own coasts, where a ship may be for days out of
touch with anyone; add to this the fact that the
operator on the ship just the other side of the
horizon is asleep, and you have all the ingredients
for a first-class newspaper scoop when the übiquitous
uncharted rock pokes its nose through the ship s
bottom. Ships’ operators keep watch at stated hours,
but the peculiarity of ocean disasters is that they
decline to confine their operations within stated
hours. Personally, I have spent as much as two
days screaming to the heavens in an endeavor to get
into communication with a ship within easy range,
and, when you come to think of it, quite a lot or
people can drown in two days. The remedy is
obvious: Continuous watch, day and night. Of course,
Private Fmterprise, which controls the matter, w|ll
see no beautv in the idea; but there is no reason
why all ship stations should not be acquired by the
Government, and run as postal telegraph offices in the
same manner as the shore stations are. Until the
Government takes them over, or L compels Private
Enterprise to keep continuous watch, the b.O.b. call
will be shrieked forth into an unheeding atmosphere,
and passengers will be left to drown Meanwhile,
the gullible traveller, fascinated by the company s
announcement, “All steamers fitted with wireless
telegraphy,” journeys on, confidently imagining that
the operator has but to press his key and assistance
will materialise.
Somebody in authority should rise to
officially contradict the person who
writes on tropical agriculture in Mel-
■ bourne Age ; otherwise an indignant
tropical agriculturist, who follows the Ag®
and fails, will probably return to Collins-
street to dynamite the office that sent him
to his undoing. The Age recommends copra
production, as practised in Timor, as an
ideal Northern Territory industry—which it
might be, on a few islands off the coast and
in the jungle fringe of the mainland itself.
But the figures put forward are stark
lunacy. Forty acres of young palms will, it
is alleged, net the holder £IOOO a year. That
is, “six palms to the acre, and a net return
of £4 to £5 per palm ; or on well-established
plantations, from £7 to £8 per tree per an-
num.” Wonderful! But, unhappily, only a
wonderful invention. Anybody who knows
anything about copra-growing in (say) the
Solomons will know that the plantation with
six trees to the acre is as rare as the palm
that nets £7 a year. Put it at 7s. and you
are nearer the mark, though still over it in
very many cases. The Age expert, however,
goes further and blares worse: —
No energetic man need fear taking up copra grow-
ing, thinking the labor too hard for the climate.
After the planting there is practically no manual
labor required. The planter must go round the
trees each morning and evening when the nuts are
falling, play a game of bowls with them under the
shade of the palms, rolling them into heaps, and his
work is done.
Nothing about the tropical undergrowth
which makes continuous cultivation a neces-
sity if you are to keep the plantation from
reverting to jungle. All you do is to play
bowls with the cocoanuts and be boob
enough to wait for “a net return of' £7 to £8
a tree.” Before that happens another cocoa-
nut falls on your own ; and you wake up.

A much-travelled man speaks up for Aus-
tralia’s State-owned railways:—
It is the fashion of daily newspapers and people
who vote for Cuttlefish Joseph Cook to intermit-
tently throw mud at any State-owned public utility
because it is State-owned. The railways are always
fair game to these people; hence the Argus loudly
objects to the statement by Fitzpatrick, Chairman
of Victorian Railway Commissioners, that “he had
seen in his travels no better steam suburban railway
system than that of Melbourne.” But, as usual, the
Argus didn’t produce any evidence in support of
itself. The big mistakes and of Australian
railways—for a terrible example, that \of Sunshine —
have been mostly caused bv know-all railway experts
from afar, their heads swollen by the adulation given
to anything foreign by these slavish dailies, attempt-
ing to graft on to the Australian system peculiar
conditions which became ridiculous outside of the
country of their origin. I have travelled a few
miles on railroads, and I say that there is no road
in Australia with the insanitary coaches I paid first-
class fares for or, the London, Chatham and Dover ;
no main lines in Australia as bad as the best lines
in Italy; and no attempt has ever been made in
Australia to come nigh the general beastliness and
inefficiency of the London and South-Eastern. The
American railways are held up as the last word in
transport; but their high-speed trains are so costly
that the average Australian would faint at the
price. The American lines, moreover, hold the
record for killing people; and because the Pullman
concern has a pull over most of the big lines, the
old. uncomfortable longitudinal sleepers are exclu-
sively used, and the passenger is charged_ £6 for six-
nig'hts Sleeper New York to ’Frisco or New York to
Vancouver for a highly intimate car wherein a lady
mav be an upper to a blushful man’s lower, or the
modest bachelor may sleeo on the second storey over
a married couple in the lower berth. I am charged
in Australia half the American fee for a place in a
2-berth cabin from Melbourne to Adelaide —one of
the finest runs in the world; and a similar sum gives
me a berth in a two-berth cabin on the Melhourne-
Sydnev express in a car which has been evolved from
Australian conditions, and yet challenges the world

as a ear-de-luxe. Kalgoorlie was only a pup born in

In relation to large questions nothing better
showed the slackness of the Watt Govern-
ment than its burlesque policy with regard
to the “red plague”—syphilis, etc. Instead
of making this a notifiable and quarantin-
able disease and providing adequate accom-

scared and rushed the public vaccinators.

dangerous; the disease extended from gene-
ration to generation, with most terrible
results. The Government was but paltering
with the matter, and as the hospital staff
expenses, for the six beds the Government
was walling to provide would be the same as
for the 25 beds they needed, they would
have to close the ward.” And they'did so.
Deak Bulletin. —While Sydney’s two gas
cos. are diligently seeking a means to again
raise the price of their commodity to the
long-suffering consumer, I desire your co-
operation to place before Premier Holman a
few pertinent and sensible remarks made by
a prominent politician in the Centenary
Hall. Speaking as the accredited repre-
sentative of the Labor party, he declared :—-
In Manchester some years ago they had a gas co.
giving them the blessings of Private Enterprise and
of competition. What this co. made I cannot tell
you ; we can possibly imagine it. But what I can
tell you is this, that some 20 or 25 years ago the
municipality of Manchester took that" over. They
borrowed £2,000,000 to do it They immedi-
ately raised the wages of the employees, they
immediately lowered the price of gas to the con-
sumer, they immediately shortened the hours of the
employees, they immediately commenced paying a
sinking fund, the result of which is that they have
now practically paid off one-half of that £2,000,000.
In addition to that, they have made during the 20
years they have held it two and a half million pounds
of absolute profit which has gone in Manchester to
the reduction of the rates. I sat' where one mono-
poly has been brought under collective control in
that way one hundred others can be with equal
readiness. That is all we propose.
Of course, the politician who so pledged him-
self and his party had then no power to
carry out the pledge ; but now that the party
has full and uncAiallengeable power in the
requisite Assembly, I would be glad to
learn what Mr. Holman proposes to do. —-
Yours truly, Billy B. P.S. —The politician
who made the above declaration was W. A.
Holman, during a debate with George Reid
on “Socialism as defined in the Australian
labor Party’s Objective and Platform,”
1906.-—B.B.
T. R. Johnson ends his connection with the
N.S.W. railways at the middle of this month,
when he goes on 10 weeks’ leave, and that
will see his term out. John Harper, the
Assistant Commissioner, who will carry on
the work as Acting-Chief Commissioner, is
a traffic man. whereas Johnson is an engin-
eer; and as they are really traffic problems
which have been worrying the N.S.W. rail-
ways most, the change from the imported
man who had everything to learn about local
conditions to the local man who knows
local conditions from A to Z, should be dis-
tinctly for the better. The new Acting-
Chief Commissioner is a Parramatta native
who has passed through every grade of the
service from porter upwards.
A Continental Sugar Trust is aiming at
the trade of Britain. It is a combination
of 14 big refiners : seven Austrian, five Ger-
man, one Dutch and one Belgium. As a
bait it offers a bonus of lid. a cwt. to those
who will undertake not to buy any sugar
from outsiders. But the British wholesaler
has met the Trust octopus before,
wherefore the members of the Wholei
sale Sugar Dealers’ Association have taken
a pledge not to place themselves in the
power of this one by having anv
dealings with it. How long that good
resolution _ will answer is another matter.
The bait is bound to attract some get-rich-
quick shopkeepers sooner or later, and their
competition will cause trouble. If public
opinion says a thing is bad there is onlv one
way to make that decision effective—by
translating it into law. Then, for the man
who breaks through the good resolution,
there is punishment. But where the good
resolution has not been translated into law
there is in most cases extra profit for the
man who first breaks away from it.

“Gome Over Here” has been pruned of
many of its original superfluities and fool-
ishnesses, and is doing profitable business
in the amended form at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney.
At the Adelphi (Sydney) the panto
“Aladdin” is running smoothly, except that
it has been minus the vivacity of Carrie
Moore through indisposition. The dashing
leg-time revue which closes the first act
continues to be the most discussed feature
of the show. Judgment: Men, “Don’t take
me home” ; women, “Hussies !”
The only objection this writer has to that
amusing, trifle, “Officer 666,” at the Criterion,
is that in this weather it is too much like
hard work to have to laugh so frequently.
The proper play for a Sydney theatre on a
sweltering night is “East Lynne.” The
gentle sound of falling tears is as refreshing
as the trickle of a fountain, and the woe
that falls like a gentle dew from the gallery
appreciably cools the air.
Julius Knight having stopped talking and
retreated from Sydney Royal with the script
of “Man and Superman,” the Waller-
Titheradge co. is preparing for a short fare-
well season of quick changes, commencing
Saturday next. There will be three nights
and a matinee of “Miss Elizabeth’s Pris-
oner,” followed by three nights of “A Mar-
riage of Convenience.” Then, on Saturday
(17th), comes “Monsieur Beaucaire.” of the
beautiful manners and waistcoats.

“The Forty Thieves” panto at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.) has shed another of its
early leaves. Two early leaves, to be pre-
cise. Regan and Ryan, described as Lieu-
tenants of the Forty, who mummed in
unison and were doubly dismal, have de-
parted from the Christmas extravaganza’s
midst, and left no trace behind. They were
an imported pair, and as far as they mat-
tered in the least degree Rupert Darrell,
the local man, is replacing them. Lupino
and Brett have introduced new business to
the panto of late ; also signs of elaboration
are perceptible in the fine spectacular scene
of the cave, where the ballets dance before
Cassini Baba Atholwood what time he is
trying to remember the missing word. Marie
Eaton, as a clean-cut, gentlemanly Abdallah,
continues to yearn for the simple life in
Loveland, and to sing of Australia’s Navy
in swanky naval garments. But although
Marie performs her vocal duties very nicely,
it is not for its songs that the present panto
will be talked about. This is an off-season
for panto ditties. There is no bite in any
of them. They are either insipid variations
of the. stereotyped, or recognisable imitations
of noises that boomed before. Reminders
of “Waiting at the Church,” “Rings on her
Fingers,” “Ip-iady-iay” and other once-
popular song refrains assail the jaded ear.
This odor of. tune from the ocean of memory
is not a bit fragrant. It suggests that
musical decomposition has set in. One wishes
that the dead panto past had buried its dead.
As Beaucaire, the superlative Frenchman
who brings all the British mankind and
womankind of Bath to its bended knees;
the swordsman whose brilliant skill is only
a little less dazzling than his skite; the
Ileaven-born gambler with the ace of social
distinction concealed up his sleeve —as this
stupendous hero of stage romance Lewis
Waller will get a good send-off from Mel-
bourne Royal to-night (Thursday). On
Saturday the co. whereof Julius Knight is
guiding star will appear in “Diplomacy” at
the Royal. The English versionof Sardou’s
old play has been re-written up-to-date.
