West has been devoting portion of his Mel-
bourne programme to a version of Ouida’s
“Moths.” The sex sentiment of “Moths”
seems dull and middle-aged to a population
that now takes it Elinor Glyn neat and its
Hubert Wales unabridged, but, all the same,

of the State of Montana, where the rustler
used to swank with whole arsenals in his

ifc
Taken altogether, the entertainment of the
present team of Dandies at St. Kilda (Vic.)
is the best that Branscombe has yet proffered
in that shrieky suburb. There is not a
“passenger” in the whole outfit, the least
distinguished being a tenor warbler of senti-
mental moonshine. A light comedian who
tangos with the piquant soubrette and
dancer, Irene Vere, is acceptable whenever
he appears; and the other two ladies make
plenty of friends with their cool comeliness
and pleasing voices. Also, there is the
attenuated jester, Claude Dampier, who,
even if some of his jokes suggest ardent
research, has a naturally humorous manner
i:i presenting his discoveries.

The yarn that the Northern Territory is
no good for white settlement recalls the
fact that, on the 31st of this month 110
years ago, Port Phillip, whereon Melbourne
is built, was abandoned by Collins as quite
uninhabitable.
An indignant Melbourne weekly points
out that Best entered State politics as a
sound democrat and throve as a democrat,
but is now a conspicuous bottle-holder for
Fat. “How shall the people punish this
renegade?” asks the agonised editor. The
Bulletin merely observes that no doubt in
Best’s opinion “What’s Best-administered is
best.”
Captain Barclay, who has been exploring
in the Northern Territory for the past two
years on account of the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment, has done good work. “Queensland
is the best of all States,” says he, “and the
Territory is better than Queensland.” But
having put that opinion on record, Captain
Barclay begins to maunder. “That [superi-
ority over Queensland] is,” he concludes,
“why some time ago I recommended that we
should go one better and call it Kingsland.”
Why pursue such shadows? The Territory
doesn’t want a name but a habitation —lots
of habitations —and railways and new har-
bors and more sheep and cattle and men.
Not a drachm of energy should be wasted on
a name, and the man who proposes to side-
track real work even for a moment should be
muzzled.
“Cross Jack”: The holiday season just
missed producing the ferry horror which
Sydney’s crowded harbor is marked up for.
The first escape was in the Cove at Cir-
cular Quay, when, after a bunny hug with
a Manly boat, the old wooden steamer Kan-
garoo became jambed between the big iron
ferry and a mail-boat. Luckily the Manly
boat skipper crawled off in time to spare
the Kangaroo from the eggshell act. The
other reprieve was when the Kosciusko,
laden with picnickers from Middle Harbor,
was caught on a swell and bumped down
on the rocks off Middle Head, where the
punch of the wide Pacific lands like the
steam hammer of Sheol. The succeeding
surge, instead of grinding the steamer on the
rocks, carried her clear, and she floundered
into deep water minus some of her keel.
This incident did not receive much public
notice, but it can hold its own in the front
rank of those narrow squeaks which are
becoming less and less uncommon.
Melbourne City Court bench is now un-
adorned by O’Callaghan, ex-Chief Commis-
sioner of Police. O’Callaghan’s disqualifi-
cations as an honorary magistrate are ob-
vious. His 40 or 50 years’ experience in
the department has naturally biassed
him in favor of the police, whilst familiaris-
ing him with numberless citizens who have
been prosecuted successfully or otherwise in
the past. The paid “beak” may remember
an accused person as one who was proved
innocent on a previous occasion to his (the
“beak’s”) complete satisfaction, whereas
O’Callaghan is more likely to remember him
as a person whom the police had declared
to be guilty, but who “slipped out” of their
clutches. No accused person’s innocence is
ever proved to the satisfaction of the
police. The bench is, in fact, no
place for any ex-detective. Suppose, for
instance, the case of a one-time burg-
lar who formerly had professional deal-
ings with detective O’Calliaghan: he
would be justly entitled to object to having
his former confidant among his judges; yet
if he made a formal protest in court he
would be casting suspicion on himself before
his trial commenced.
Those ingenuous persons who annually
drop several thousand unaddressed enve-
lopes into Commonwealth letter-boxes, and
then blame the P.O. officials for not deliver-
ing them, suffer from a similar aberration
in regard to articles sent by rail, especially
about Christmas time. There were unidenti-
fiable prams, umbrellas,' sporting utensils
and camping outfits at the Melbourne central
parcels office; but the worst trouble was the
dead or semi-dead stock. Poultry (plucked
and otherwise), fish and rabbits arrived
without labels and were thrown into a cor-
ner till the rush abated and someone had
time to look over them; but the rush con-
tinued for days—and rabbits don’t keep
well this weather. Away in a camp on
some creek somebody was blessing lazy city
dwellers for not acknowledging the receipt
of a seasonable gift; and some city dweller
was anathematising forgetful campers for
failing to send along the promised Christmas
tucker; while in the muggy, odoriferous
parcels offices perspiring railway servants
were consigning to perdition every lunatic
who hadn’t brains enough to see that a
deceased hen wore the name and address of
someone at whom they could throw it.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Spasm ode”: “Come Over-Here,” say the baldies
to the bally,’ and the ballet, like Barkis, is willin’.
So we have the gangway. There is no originality in
it. Manager Elliston introduced it at Drury Lane
when he hired the props, used in George IV. ’s corona-
tion and acted the monarch. Hiccuping “Bless you
my people!” he fancied himself the real one. (Lam-
bert, by the way, acted the King of Bavaria with
Lola Montes in Sydney, and Lola said he was better
than the real king.) With the play of ‘‘Tom and
Jerry in London, they had a donkey race round the
back of the pit. A familiar circus act is that of
the drunken , swaggie who wants: to ride, and turns
out to be a star of the co. So much for the newest
‘ features” of revue and pantomime.
“M.L.”: “First Nighter’s” prescription (B. 1/1/’l4)
for popularising the revue in Australia won’t do any
good, for the revue is dead. It never really lived.
Alcohol and casualness make the revue in America!
The booze on the roof-gardens is the sauce piquante.
Scotch highballs at little tables in the auditorium;
in some places your dinner, if you please; Lilian
Lorraine riding up the plank on a white donkey to
the stage; Lilian Lorraine showing her thousand-
dollar stockings on a swing projected over the audi-
ence ; six Kentucky colonels refusing to pay for
their drinks in the stalls, and immediately thereafter
bursting into song about bluegrass and corn whisky •
Roosevelt entering from the orchestra and shooting
property lions—all these things are slow and boring
unless there be Scotch highballs, Manhattans and
old rye with a chaser. And the Australian is too
hardheaded, for the foolishness of the revue nonsense;
he likes his nonsense with some cleverness in it
which makes one wonder how he tolerated “Our Miss
Gibbs,” “Sunshine Girl” (and scores of other Girls),
“Autumn Manoeuvres,” “Luxembourg,” “Blue Moon,”’
“Cingalee,” etc. Certainly he didn’t tolerate them
sufficiently to make revivals payable, and when he
gets Gilbert and. Sullivan again he will just eat it.
Still, “First Nighter” has the right idea of a revue!
The delicate idealism and wholesome humor of a
satyr and a white slave, with the impossible hypo-
dermic syringe and the marvellous perfumed bouquet,
are just the thing—for England and other places
where wholesome views of sex matters are, among the
Best People, “quite bourgeois, you know.”
“0.K.”: John Coates, the burly dramatic tenor
who was so much missed from the last Quinlan
Opera Co., got some enthusiastic notices for his
recent work with the Raymond Roze Co. at Covent
Garden. Of his performance in “Tannhiiuser,” for
instance, a London critic wrote: “The honors went
to Mr. John Coates in the name part. It was pure
joy to listen to his vocal delivery, of the music, his
clear enunciation of English and his perfect phrasing.
To not a few of the audience the part was probably
a revelation, particularly the significance of Tann-
luiuser’s relation of his pilgrimage.” Which is a
good deal more than could be said for either of the
Tannhausers who were heard in the recent Quinlan
season. If the boom is to be repeated in 1915, the
co. will have to include efficient substitutes for
Coates and the stout soprano, Agnes Nicliolls, the
boss principals of the Wagnerian performances in
1912.
“Parisine”: Claretie, the brilliantly successful
director of the Theatre Frangais for 28 years, is dead.
The position is more difficult to fill than any other
theatrical management on earth, and few have held
it long. Four Paris theatres receive Government
subsidies —the Grand Opera, Opera Comique, Theatre
Francais and Odeon. Of course, this implies a high
standard. Yet the public is rebellious against the
schooling it gets, and finance is therefore apt to be
troublesome. The Grand Opera is the easiest to
manage, because nobody expects from it anything but
grand opera, by French composers as much as pos-
sible—Gounod, Halevy, Massenet, Paladilhe, Bizet
and Debussy. With the other subsidised theatres
there is a continual far between Government and
public. The managements are‘always stretching points
to meet the public, whereupon the Radicals declare
that the subsidies are absurd. They point to the
very greatest performers, such as Bernhardt and
Coquelin, having deserted the Comedie. The con-
sideration with them is, of course, merely financial,
and a subsidised theatre must struggle to be some-
thing more than that. So a Government-subsidised
theatrg has its own troubles, and is apt to lose the
services of the best actors and actresses.
“Melb”: Barry Lupino, of the “Forty Thieves”
pantomime, knew Fragson well. (Fragson is the
comedian who was shot dead in Paris the other
day by his demented father.) His real name was
Potts. He began with singing at London smoke
concerts. Then he went to Paris, and took a
waiter’s billet. He happened to bo at a soiree
given by a Parisian journalist, who liked to foster
rising talent. Coquelin was there. Fragson won
his approval with a song at the piano. This led to
his engagement at music-halls. He originated a
new style, which has been carried out by enter-
tainers like Spurr, Gammon and Margaret Cooper.
Or it might be called a revival of the old style of
Parry. He was handy at composition, and turned
English songs, quite fresh, into French, and French
into English. (His mother was a Frenchwoman.)
His success led to an engagement for the Drury Lane
pantomime, where he was welcomed for five seasons.
He broke the charm of the Principal Boy in “Cin-
derella” —was the hero of the pantomime ; its Waller
or Julius Knight. This led to the Principal Boy
being deposed in Australia for just one year, when
Lincoln acted Sinbad.
“Bald.y II.”: Strange it is to note of the French
lady artists who have won world-wide reputations for
wearing about as little as possible on the stage that
the more they put off their backs the more they put
on their bills. Here’s Gaby Deslys, for instance,
discussing the amount of money she finds it necessary
to spend on clothes. She cannot dress on less than
£14,000 a year. Then the beauteous Oecile Sorel
(be still, my fluttering heart!), of the Comedie Fran-
fiaise, has been offered a paltry £BOOO for a four-
months’ engagement in S. America. “What is
£8000?” asks Cecile —presumably of her dress-agent.
“My dresses and cloaks would cost me £4OOO alone.”
It’s a mystery to me when the Gabys and the
Ceciles wear these things.
“Private Boxer”: Many of the critical fraternity
have discovered that “Man and Superman” is “talky,”
and they are right. But what would they have said
had the “Hell” scene—which they regret has been
cut —been played ? Talk about talk ! The Federal
Parliament is a heaven of silence compared with it 1
But cut Shaw’s cackle, and where is he? He never
was a dramatist, and, judging by his latest works,
“Androcles and the Lion” and “Great Catherine”
(which might make money if re-christened “Cather-
ine Wheels” and turned into a Russian revue), there
is little hope of his ever becoming one. He is too
old to unlearn.
Elsie Stanley-llall, the Australian pianiste who has
been teaching for many years in England, has
married Dr. F. O. Stohr, of Northern Rhodesia. She
is the daughter of one of the patriarchs of the S. M.
Herald office.
“Gazer”: It’s genuine that McCormack made
£20,000 here. He deserved it. Bigger men than he
is, or ever will be, have only scored modestly, for
they came to sell us the last remnants of their musi-
cal stock. McCormack gave us the pick of his goods
—fresh and yet ripe. Some of that £20,000 was
for the compliment. It will do us good in bringing
us more artists in their golden prime.
“W. V. Penn”: They’re all wrong who, after going
to “Come Over Here,” have described Daisy Jerome’s
hair variously as auburn, ginger and red. The . day-
light color of her thatch is true terracotta, the tint
Burne-Jones never quite got with his brush. An
elder sister, Sadie, was the hit of the Prince of
Wales’s (London) burlesque, “Gentleman Joe,” in
April, 1895, playing Lalage Potts, a ’Murkan heiress,
who sang:—-
I’m spying around for an English lord
To marry me quick of his own accord ;
But the first that knocks my fancy—wa’al,
If he likes it or not, he’ll wed this gal!
He may fuss around and make a stir,
But I guess that wedding’s got to occur;
The almighty dollar would buy, you bet,
A superior class of co-ro-net.
Sadie was described as “a very pretty American brun-
ette, sparkling-eyed, with much nasal twang.” The
terracotta of Daisy’s hair is natural. Off stage she’s
more of the Continental sassiety ingenue than Cockney
or ’Murkan, and is a very dutiful daughter. Ma
travels with her.
“H. 0.”: A striking commentary on the importing
mania with which Australian theatrical firms are
afflicted is furnished by one of this year’s pantos.
For the principal “boy” and girl parts two over-
seas artists were engaged. Before the opening night
various songs which in the ordinary course would have
fallen to the principal “boy” were transferred to a
minor character, impersonated by an Australian. The
principal girl was preceded by a multitude of puff
pars. ; but before the first night her part had been
“cut” until there was scarcely any of it left, and
after that solitary appearance it was decided that a
whole army of press agents would not be able to con-
vince the public of the lady’s excellence, so she was
gently withdrawn.
Amongst the passengers by the Morea, which
reached Fremantle on the 30th, was J. M. Stevens,
well known in Sydney musical circles. A couple of
years ago his daughter Cecile won a scholarship at
the R.A.M., London, and Mr. Stevens migrated with
his entire family to keep the girl company while she
was doing her academy work. Last year, on a visit,
he brought some moving pictures—“ From Manger to
Cross,” among them —which sold well; and he is
trying his luck again with some other films.
“0.K.”: Recent “Poverty Point” mention of some
Australian boy performers whom the public accepted
as girls gave no place to Gus St. Leon, the most
audaciously successful he-maid of them all. Even a
Bulletin critic some years ago mistook Gus for
Gwendoline when he was perpetrating his harmless
fraud in Wirths’ Circus. Gus used to brave compari-
son by appearing with a real girl on either side of
him. The trio were billed as lady acrobats, Gus
being the champion soinersaulter of the beauteous
bunch. He was convincing until he outgrew the
deception and his tall figure seemed a bit over the
odds.
The last date for receiving musical settings of
Adams’s prize verses is February 20. The adjudi-
cators are Arundel Orchard, Fritz Hart, E. Harold
Davies, George Sampson, Alexander J. Lecke and
George F. Hopkins, representing respectively N.S.W.,
Vic., South Aus., Queensland, Westralia and Tasmania.

Mrs. Charles Bruce Marriott (Ida Lee,
daughter of the late George Lee, _ of
Bathurst, and grand-daughter of William
Lee. one of the first settlers on Bathurst
plains, and member for Roxburgh in the
first Legislative Assembly of N. S. Wales)
has been elected a Fellow of the Royal
Geographical Society in recognition of her
research work in the domain of early Aus-
tralian exploration. Mrs. Marriott’s accounts
of early voyages in the Southern Seas and
her “How the British Came to Australia”
are well known. Of Australian women she
lias been the most tireless in scratching
among dusty old documents.
“F.II.”: Literature-professor Wallace,
the imported brain-worker who was chosen
by the Melbourne University wiseheads in
preference to Murdoch, the Australian, has
unloaded a bitter complaint concerning the
Australian woman-student. His woe is that
discipline is subverted by the behavior of
the female student. “Women speak at lec-
tures in still, small voices,” he declared,
“and the noise is so faint that it can never
be located. When two or three of them get
going it is distinctly annoying. They have
a habit, too, of drooping hairpins on the
floor.” Pity the plaintive professor, “dis-
tinctly” annoyed by the dropping, hopping
hairpin and the tintinnabulating tongue of
the sweet girl-gossip —and imagine Mur-
doch, of Australia, voicing a similar com-
plaint !
“Clay Face” : Bitter is the rivalry of the
Marshall Hall Conservatorium and the
Melbourne University Conservatorium of
Music. So much of the bitterness belongs
to the latter that one of the pan-
jandrums of the University stated to the
press that the rival shop v/as about to close
down. The passing over of Ilarting and
Scharff to the University Conservatorium
was some corroboration of the exulting
statement, but the pioneer institution has
arisen to say publicly and in a loud voice
that it will continue to do business at the
old stand. Certainly the Albert-street fac-
tory has all the honors in the pro-
duction of artists. It started well with
Marshall Hall —a great and true musician,
for all his silly excursions into cheap and
fliprant verse; and Elise Weidermann-
Pinschof still challenges a rival in Aus-
tralia as a teacher of voice-production. Hall
must have had a great personality to stand
the assaults of the daily press and the
rivalry of the “official” concern; though he
was helped by the fact that Peterson —great
showman as he is—is less of a musician.
Fitz Hart, too, is a good successor to Mar-
shall Hall, and the Established Church,
which is the University Conservatorium, will
still be left with a smaller congregation than
the Albert-street Dissenters.
“Lud”: They are saying in the Best
Circles in England that the Duchess of
Marlborough isn’t playing the game. She
summoned a meeting of distinguished people
I —bishops, politicians, industrial heads and
so forth—at Sunderland Plouse, to consider
the Woman Suffrage question. The people
came, with an idea of orating pleasantly, as
a mere introduction to the real business of
champagne and strawberries. But they
found the platform occupied by 12 poor old
women, who did all the speaking. These
Twelve Apostles had had from 20 to 50 years
of cruel sweating. One held up a shirt, and
said : “I have to make a dozen of these for
ninepence.” Twenty years she had been at
that grind. Another, a widow in the con-
fectionery trade, said that for 20 years she
had never been able to spend more than
one penny on a meal. A woman showed a
pair of boot uppers. She makes 12 pairs
for tenpence. Another poor old creature
makes artificial butterflies and flowers at
tenpence a dozen —sold by the sweater for
Is. Gd. each. Another gets threepence a
gross for making match-boxes. Thus did
the Duchess of Marlborough plunge her
guests into England’s rotten economic sys-
tem. Then, to make matters worse, after
the Twelve Apostles of living death had had
their say, the hostess called up a statist.
He said : “These 12 poor old women typify
two millions, few of whom earn more than
12s. a week, and 300,000 earn much less.”
And now you can go home to your own
champagne and strawberries, said the lady
in effect, and think these things over.
THE LUCK OF IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My uncle, converted when lie was 99, lived a beauti-
ful life for two years, and died at 101.
—A visiting Evangelist in S. M. Herald.
Throughout the fevered days of youth,
He cherished young Desire,
Pursuing Pleasure, as she ran,
With wild, unholy fire.
Our local Wowser wagged his head,
And reckoned he were better dead.
Uncounted were the girls he kissed ;
Likewise the drinks he drank ;
A splendid time my uncle had
Though, morally, he sank
So deep in Error's murky swim
That Sin well-nigh encompassed him.
How different my sainted aunt!
A maiden lady, she,
Who spurned the love of lawless man
Till she was thirty-three,
And would have gone on, folks aver,
Had Love not 'then avoided her.
She neither drank, nor swore, nor smoked,
She would not go the pace;
Cold, unrelenting virtue shone
Like gaslight from her face;
Lonely and quite unmourned she died,
Esteemed by all the country-side.
Meanwhile my wretched uncle’s ways
Grew yearly worse and worse;
He sinned so hard that he became
A byword and a curse.
Withal he did not seem to mind;
A blither soul you could not find.
Although he swindled ev’ryone,
And, by this means, grew rich,
His eye was bright, his brow serene —■
Two circumstances which
Impressed me with suspicions dim
That Conscience did not worry him.
At ninety-eight his vigor failed ;
Flirtations rather palled ;
He took to sleeping long and deep;
The bar no longer called ;
He did not need to take folks down,
Being the richest man in town.
And presently he was acclaimed
A good and grand old man ;
One of those epic characters
Built on the British plan,
Who, in the , last analysis,
Have made the Empire what it is.
We saved his soul at ninety-nine;
For two whole years his life
Resembled that of some sweet child ;
Though wickedness was rife
On ev’ry side, he did not fall;
He spurned his tempters one and all.
And now my relative is dead,
And walking hand in glove
(Or so, at least, our parson claims)
With those who dwell Above.
Thus saith his tombstone, spick and span:
"He got there both ways—lucky man!”
N.B.W.

SOCIETY
Thackeray tells how George the Fourth
in the prime of his powers invented a new
shoe-buckle : “A sweet invention ! lovely and
useful as the Prince on whose foot it
sparkled.” According to Sir William Mac-
gregor, the late King Edward made two
things very fashionable —one appendicitis ;
the other a new method of folding trousers.
His Ex. went on to express a wish that
the Prince of Wales, who has lately taken
to studying the bagpipes, “would make that
instrument popular.” Evidently Queens-
land’s Governor clearly appreciates the fine
things of -which the Guelph headpiece is
really capable.

after looking at the men for from three to five
minutes, identified the accused.”
Senior-Constable Riley gave corroborative evidence.

A prize of a nickel-plated corkscrew is offered
to the reader whose correct solution of the above
problem is- first received at this office (accom-
panied, of course, by the usual 3d. in stamps
to defray postage, stationery, office boy’s labor,

Another instance of Young Australia’s
tired feeling and lack of stamina! At Gil-
gandra (N.S.W.) a father and his son fell
70ft. through the staging of a well they
were repairing. Although badly damaged,
with a severe cut in the head which later on
called for several stitches, the son, a lad
of 16, lifted his stunned father out of the
water, and propped him up with broken
planks. Then he climbed up the pump pipe
to the top for a rope, descended with it, and
tied his father to the pipe. Up he climbed
then a second time, and cleared off in a
sulk-p for assistance. Thus Dad was rescued
with sound limbs but a severely injured
back. And, remember, the youngster who
did it —and probably didn’t think lie was
doing anything out of the way—had himself
just fallen the 70ft. and been so badly
knocked about that the doctor had to get to
work on him as soon as the father had been
seen to.
“Briner”: The Belgian training-ship
E’Avenir, now lying at the Melbourne
Wharf, gives us a lesson. “Avenir” means
“future” ; and the ship, a grand four-masted
barque, means that Belgium is determined
to have a navy. The ship, built in 1908 by
public subscription, was presented to the
Government, which uses it for training pur-
poses, and pays some of the expenses by
carrying cargo. Timber and pig-iron were
brought to Melbourne, and a wheat cargo
will be taken back. There are between 4 )
and 50 cadets aboard. After they are
trained they will have to enter the mercan-
tile service, as the navy will not be ripe;
but they will be qualified naval officers and
ready for the. fruition of the scheme. Ger-
many, indeed, has intimated that it will be
willing to take them into its navy straight
away if Belgium agrees. And probably soitie
will go whether their nation agrees or not,
for their training is intensely valuable.
Why not a few such Australian ships? Our
trade could do with them, as the disclosures
in the produce business clearly show.
* * * *
A seaside holiday crowd mostly makes
its own amusements, but the one at
Brighton Beach (Vic.) on Boxing Day was
largely assisted by unwilling caterers or
entrepreneurs. One of them, a cheerful
collector of odd sixpences, was exhibiting
what he openly designated a “Burlesque
Wild Man from Borneo” ; but the obvious-
ness of the old fake failed to appeal to
some humorless amusement-hunters, who
capsized the tent, dismantled the wild man
and chased him into the desert country
around the police station, and then gathered
up the too-deliberate showman and deposited
him in the bay. Heaven knows why they
did these things ; this writer has seen bur-
lesques at city theatres that were much
bigger take-downs than the “wild man.” but
he never heard of a theatrical manager
being dumped in a horse-trough. Picture
shows are evidently creating a dainty,
finicky taste in the public.
“Carmine”: They will persist in begin-
ning at the wrong end with the Australian
National Portrait Gallery. Don’t, I say,
work backwards; work forwards. In other
words, start with the oldest justified claims.
This is the safe plan. Otherwise we shall
have an appalling lot of nobodies, until
there will be a rubbish heap of unknown
Smiths, Browns, Joneses and Robinsons.
I don’t object to Hopetoun, Barton. Deakin,
Reid, Parkes, Kingston or Griffith. But
why such as Tennyson, Gould, Turley,
Salmon and McDonald? If these men
prove their greatness by the test of about
40 years, well and good. The foundation
of a National Gallery lies in such as Cook,
Macquarie, Macarthur. Wentworth, Lang,
Higinbotham. Sturt, Stuart, Kendall and
Gordon. This was the principle in the
London National Gallery. It did not even
bevin by hurrying up with such living men
as Disraeli and Gladstone, but laid the
basis with such as Bacon, Newton, Shak-
speare. Milton, Cromwell, Chatham, Pitt,
Fox, Burke, Byron and Scott.
William Redmond, brother of John E., is
now paying his fifth visit to Australia. Ilis
first visit, 30 years ago, raised pandemo-
nium. He and brother John went round
with the Home Rule hat and had hopes of
filling it. But Sydney was in a spiteful
humor at the time, and hall after hall was
shut against them. They pushed their de-
posit money for the Town Hall slyly into
the office; but when the Council understood
the matter the cash was fired back with
wrath. Several hotels refused them meals,
and Old-Man Parkes urged the Legislative
Assemblv to order the deportation of Willie
as a convicted criminal. The younger
brother had, it is true, been in gaol in Ire-
land, but he was run in on suspicion, not
after trial. Since then he has been really
in gaol—three times, in fact —because of his
orations and other patriotic noises. The
brothers on that first visit succeeded in get-
ting the old Academy of Music in Castle-
reagh-street. and there the hat was well
filled. At the same time Willie first became
an M.P.: a vacancy occurred in Wexford,
and he was elected in his absence to the
House of Commons. The later visits were not
so full of storm, and the bright young man
was able to combine business with love-
making.

* * * *
A hairy man of auburn hue got himself
drowned in the Yarra near Princes Bridge
on New Year’s Day. It was an after-
luncheon tragedy, and the circumstances
were singular. The hairy one emptied his
trousers pockets of eighteenpence, gave the
money to somebody for safe keeping, and
sprang from the Yarra bank into the river.
He could swim like a duck, but his clothes
were an evident drawback ; so three rescue
parties went after him in rowing boats.
One boat with a policeman in it overtook
the swimmer, and the constable made a grab
at him. Unfortunately the hairy adventurer
sank right there, never to rise again. He
had been drinking, of course ; all the same,
it was not beer that brought this absurd
person to his watery grave. He died of
being an expert swimmer. Nobody who
couldn’t swim a yard would have met his
death in that particular way. Alas! how
many drowning fatalities during these hot
summer months are due to the victims’
knowledge of swimming !

Poor Chidley, Sydney’s persistent skirted-
dress reformer, had another brief trip to the
Reception House recently. Just about the
same time hot, blanketed Scotchmen were
blowing screams through the bagpipes and
bidding for heat apoplexy by performing the
old tribal prances in front of a meek, trou-
sered audience at the Agricultural Show
Ground. Tradition enables a man or a class
to do things that bring swift retribution to
the daring pioneer. If only he had been born
McChidley, and peddled haggis in tins instead
of “The Answer” !
* * * *
It is a hopeless age for the Christmas
reveller who tries to live up to the imported
mistletoe and rum-punch traditions of the
season. A jolly Britisher (the charge-sheet
said “Drunk and disorderly”) ventured to
roar “The Mistletoe Bough” on a Manly
tram on Boxing Day. Years ago the rol-
licking old ditty would have carried him
through; but a sober, whispering load
of strangers looked at him sourly, and when
he heartilv wished them and the tram in-
spector the compliments of the season, and
audaciously shook the inspector’s hand, he
wrote his own summons, so to speak. The
world doesn’t throw up its hat as it used to.
Hear the cable talking about the older citi-
zens of New York who stood on the corners
of streets and whooped defiantly because
New Year’s Eve wasn’t riotous enough to
please them !
ALL ROUND THE BARROW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Woman’s figure this autumn will resemble nothing
so much as an elongated pear. —Fashion note.
Some time ago she seemed a dainty peach,
In pink perfection rounded sweetly;
And, as we gazed, we felt no mortal shape
Could ever twine itself in lovelier drape,
Or trip along more fairy-featly.
It seemed, in fact, the rankest heresy
To e’er deny or even doubt it;
And if a critic cavilled at the curve
Of swelling grace, we marvelled at his nerve,
And told him what we thought about it.
But, lo ! to-day the blushing peach has passed,
And Delia tapers from her snowy shoulder
Down to her twinkling ankles; and you share
The vision of a finely-fashioned pear—
A wonder-work of Fashion’s moulder.
From peach to pear, perchance from pear she’s
passing
To some quaint guise of “pine” or fig or mango,
Secure, by some deep spell, to charm us all,
And proud to prove, howe’er the fashions fall,
How far upon this idol-loving ball
Our dainty Delia can go.
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.
A HOLIDAY SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, lam sick of the city noises! Wheels and ham-
, mers and raucous cries
Make me long for the wind’s brave music—make me
wish for the sea’s clean skies !
Out of the mess of dust and paper, out of the heat
and work and fret,
I would go on an out-bound steamer on a mission
of loafing set.
Never a ship goes down the harbor, nosing her lonely
way to sea,
But I wish I was safe aboard her, getting away from
Monotony,
Getting away from prim timetables —getting away
from the clocks and rules,
Dodging the everlasting clatter made “by a million
other fools.
Leaving the bills and work and worry, shoving the
mess of Life aside;
Steaming out on a good ship briskly where the out-
look is clean and wide !
Hear the beat of the busy engines! Hear the snap
of a wind-whipped llag !
Now the sinews of Joy are tightened, and the city’s
a distant hag.
Watch the crone in her smoke-haze sinking where
the sky and the water merge.
Then, oh then, turn a glad face seaward as she
lifts to the first blue surge!
Now with the ocean gates before you, let your
thoughts and your heart go fr.e!
Here at hand for your Youth’s renewing is God’s
valorous, sounding sea!
N.S.W.

The Kirk got a bad advertisement the
other day in Victoria, when a parson’s wife
prosecuted a youth for stealing a sovereign
she had given him to put on a horse. It
turned out that the lady was a habitual,
almost a professional, punter. She had
been punting for nine years, and declared
she was driven to it by the fact that the
Kirk did not pay her husband sufficient to
keep himself, let alone her. The money she
won was used in maintaining the home. It
was a remarkable case altogether, but not
the least remarkable part of it was that
there is one person in Australia, and a
woman at that, who, according to her own
story, makes a profit out of backing horses.

After a calm survey :
Three of the “Commonwealth portraits” are at
last approved by the “Art Advisory Board,” and are
on view to the public in Queen’s Hall, Parliament
House, Melbourne. Of the three, one only can be
said to be of much artistic interest. This is the
half-length of Henry Parkes, by Julian Ashton —a
remarkable piece of character painting; vigorous in
handling, fine in color. Ambrose Patterson’s King-
ston was painted from a photograph, and hardly gave
the artist an opportunity to spread himself. The
figure is somewhat stiff on the legs, but the color
is warm and pleasing, and makes Meldrum’s bewigged
portrait of Chief Justice Griffith look corpse-like.
Indeed, the hands of the latter are those of an
Egyptian mummy. Meldrum has used effectively the
safe academic posture and accessories—leather couch,
Brussels carpet and all —and apart from its un-
pleasant and creepy flesh tints, is as effective as most
of the thousands of official portraits one sees. Fox’s
portrait of Fisher has not shown up so far.
ALIVE WITH CORPSES.
WAVBACK JOHN HOP (called upon to report state of water hole that had been rushed by
travelling cattle): “Oi have to report, yer Honor, that the watherhole is aloive
wid dead bastes .”
Though the rain seems to be holding off,
there was a heavy fall of Sydney tram pas-
sengers the other evening. The brakes of
one of Johnson’s cars failed on one of the
screaming, downhill curves, and, as there
was a stationary car in front of the run-
away, the long slide ended in a deafening
crash. Next instant the atmosphere rained
four tram conductors, two domestic ser-
vants and an elderly lady who evidently
thought it was a disgraceful affair, because
she refused to give her name, and was
driven home in the most respectable cab
available. Neither of the domestic servants
was broken, thank Heaven !
“Adelaide”: The late Jonah Hobbs, of
suburban Campbelltown (S.A.), has left an
estate of £36,700, all made out of his garden
on the Torrens bank. And he is far from
being the only capitalist in that line. Ade-
laide producers cannot understand why so
much of the rest of Australia thinks it can’t
grow a cabbage without the aid of a China-
man, or why it should be thought less hon-
orable to supply one’s fellow-men with beans
than with beef. The rows of fine waggons
and teams outside the Markets any market
morning are ocular demonstration of an in-
dustry kept White, with solid profit to the
keepers.
* * * *
During the Christmas season of brotherly
love and Christian charity, when citizens
were forgiving their friends and trying to
forget their enemies, the Melbourne police,
while suffused with doubts about the genu-
ineness of all this palaver, were much exer-
cised over Sarah McEacharn. There was
nothing specially wrong with Sarah. She
was sane and fairly healthy; but the Law
had laid the hand of correction on her, and
was in a tangle in consequence. Sarah
came under restraint away back in Novem-
ber through throwing herself into the
Yarra, and would have accomplished her
purpose but for the usual interfering small
bov. who rescued her at considerable risk.
The Law patted the youth on the back and
locked Sarah up on a charge of attempting
suicide. The lady, brought into court, cheer-
fully admitted the charge, and assured the
Law that she was sick and tired of life, and
would, if released, carry out her intention
thoroughly and finally. The shocked beaks
remanded her for a week to think it over.
At the end of that time she was still more
firmly convinced of the futility of things,
so the Law recommended a “home,” with
plenty of wash-tub. But the unregenerate
Sarah declined any “home”; she preferred
gaol. After that the Law kept on remand-
ing her until Christmas, when it thought of
her relations. She owned up to sons and
daughters, but knew the whereabouts of only
one, a girl, who wasn’t in a position to
assist her and had troubles enough of
her own, anyhow; so her address was dis-
remembered, too. After that Sarah was
remanded some more, until she finally
wearied of the procrastination and demanded
discharge or gaol. So the Law told her to
go about her business and keep away from
the Yarra. As her remains were fished out
of that stream a few days later, it is evi-
dent she considered herself superior to the
Law’s advice. Yet it is a pity she was
wasted. Very few women keep their pro-
mises so conscientiously.
“See-Gee”: Thus a recent issue of the
official Church of England Messenger in
Victoria
Is it salvation which Professor Ehrlich has dis-
covered in an antidote to diseases which spring from
immoral practices? It will be a godsend to the
innocent sufferers, the children of a libertine and
such-like. It will be a very Devil’s gift if Salvarsan
or “606” means total immunity from the physical
ill-effects of immorality, for then a man may sin
with impunity! And the downward path is easier
for woman, too, who may indulge passion without
penalty, thanks to a drug-store or similar device.
If the Second Commandment’s penalty is thus
evaded, and there are no children suffering innocently
for parents’ sins, there is that one less check at all
events to sin. And we have gone a step further and
abolished by our science the retribution which Nature
exacts from the self-indulgent, sinful human body.
For unmixed diabolism, that eruption is
almost unsurpassable. Nature _ does not
“exact” syphilis any more than it “exacts”
influenza; and not even a church has been
insane enough thus far to denounce any-
thing that would cure influenza. As for the
contention that the universe is likely to
rush the charms of the syphilis scourge now
that it has found a cure, surely this is about
the limit in imbecility. Up to date, has
the Messenger noticed people wallow-
ing to any extent in filth just for the plea-
sure of being cured of a resultant filth dis-
ease?

“Gordon G.”: Cabled that the English
Appeal Court has decided that, by both
Common and Statute Law, the dearer sex
is precluded from practising at the Bar or
elsewhere in the six-and-eight profession. So
the Law in the Fog retains its conservative
old character and stands out as being about
the only trade, business, calling, job or pas-
time which bangs the door on woman’s nose.
The Judges, being a discreet body of men,
seem to have refrained from even hinting at
any reason for their decision, other than
that they were there to administer the Law
as it stood, and without any personal feel-
ing. But the reason is fairly obvious.
Stated baldly, it
is this: they con-
sidered that the
female part of the
community has
not as good brains
as the hairier sex,
and that only the
best brains are
good enough for
the Law. Which
being so, a good Providence only knows
what a lot of us who more or less practise
the profession are doing in it.
**' * *
Evans, M.L.C., Minister for Health in the
17-day Victorian Labor Ministry, approved
of I-lealth-OfScer Robertson’s recommenda-
tion to municipalise the milk supply, with a
separate and specially-guarded supply for
infants. Under Dr. Robertson’s scheme
supply farms are to be specially selected,
and the municipality takes charge of the
testing, pasteurising, bottling and distribu-
tion of the milk in sealed and sterilised
bottles, one distribution per 24 hours being
thus made sufficient. Milk for infants is to
be delivered in “one-feed size” bottles, and
there is to be a regular visitation of houses
by trained nurses to instruct mothers in
artificial feeding and to report on the chil-
dren artificially fed. Provision is made for
the free supply of milk to children whose
parents are unable to pay. After the intro-
duction of a similar supply scheme in
Rochester (N.Y.) the deaths of children in
July and August decreased by 50 per cent,
in nine years, although the population in
that period increased 20 per cent. It will
cost some money to municipalise this pure-
milk scheme, but its profits are enormous
and certain. In the once-a-day distribution
alone and the related fact that four rival
milk-vendors will no longer supply eight
houses in the one street, there is a dividend.
But the greatest profit will be in the saving
of child life. A high birth-rate is valueless
if the newborn dies of a dirty bottle within
six months.
* * * *
“Chasuble”: There has been another de-
velopment of the Wroeite business. After
Wroe died in Fitzroy (Melb.), Fisher, “the
Nunawading Messiah,” tried to succeed the
other prophet, but could only rope in 120
Wroeites for his Church of the Firstborn.
The others repudiated him. The sect, which
had become numerous in England and
America, agreed on a new leader, an ex-
soldier named White, who christened him-
self Jezreel. He managed to raise about
£40,000, with which he built an immense
edifice, known as Jezreel’s Tower, on top of
Chatham Hill, near London. All were to
be gathered in this on the last day. When
he died his wife carried on the business as
Queen Esther. The American gang, how-
ever, disputed her right, and sent over a
leader called Prince Michael, who tried to
wrest the tower from Esther. Probably
they squared it; anyway, the tower has
been for sale, on representations that it
would make an excellent brewery. And at
last a deal is effected —Jezreel’s Tower is to
be a picture theatre! Another fine pro-
perty of the denomination is, or has been,
Meloourne House, Ashton, Yorkshire, built
in Wroe’s lifetime, with money subscribed
mostly in Fitzroy, where the Wroeites, or
Christian Israelites, still keep going in their
large, bluestone church. Visiting it on a
recent Sunday night, I found good preach-
ing of a Methodist order. The Wroeites
keep to their long hair rolled up behind and
their peculiar low-crowned belltoppers, while
the women are really pretty in bonnets.

A HOT WEATHER OUTBURST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Tuesday morning.—Sir Ernest Shackleton
is arranging another expedition to solve the problem
of the Continental nature of the Antarctic.— Cable.
No man who has not actually experienced it can
realise what the call of the Antarctic i 5.... We
didn’t have a bad time at a11....We had a good
stock of literature, mostly books of travel and novels,
and we used to play chess, draughts and bridge.
During the short daytime we had a little hockey or
football. We had plenty of provisions. All we had
to do was to go out and get ice—good hard ice—-
for fresh water.
—Story of an Antarctic habitue, told in March, 1913.
I am the newspaper reader wearily trying to cover
Snow tracks of rival explorers, who must incessantly
hover
Where there are glaciers and bergs. Why should we
lend them assistance,
So they may go and play hockey? Here! let’s make
a gallant resistance!
Stifle their frosty heroics! Bust up their ice cliffs
and blizzards !
Why should we pay for their penguins and what
they have in their gizzards?
What shall it profit subscribers, sweating for coin at
their steady
Jobs, till the tale of the starving and freezing and
dragging is ready?
Why should we sweat for their freeze and the wreck
of some cranky appliance
Smashed by a blizzard “down South” ? In the name
of the god we call Science !
Oh, Blither! And Bunkum ! And Bosh ! Why, this
snow-tramping’s merely a hobby—-
A sort of cool scramble for fame and the freedom
from work that’s too jobby ;
A clear get-away from the Monday to Saturday road
that we travel;
A break from convention and bills, and the little
knots we must unravel.
Antarctic exploring be blowed ! I tell you I’d just
like to try it!
Let’s have medals for plain, summer work and the
heroes like us who stand by it!
tr.s.w.

The Mayor of Rome —the Eternal City—
for the past six years has been a Cockney
Jew named Nathan.
Westralia’s newest Rhodes scholar is a
20-year-old Gawler, a great-grandson of S.
Australia’s second Governor —the man who
got things into the mess that Hori Grey
was later on called in to straighten out.
The Y.C. heroes haven’t a monopoly of
nerve. Charles Fermino, a pumper in. the
Long Tunnel mine, at Walhalla (Vic.),
took hold of a sagging wire to put it in
place, and caught the full force of the cur-
rent it was carrying. ‘‘Don’t touch me,” he
said, as a rush was made to release him,
and coolly instructed his mate to cut the
current off. By the wildest freak of luck
the wires fused, and a life was saved that
may do Australia a lot of good if a big
crisis comes.
LAV. BONYTHON, the just-retired Mayor
of Adelaide.
“Tim” Coghlan, N. S. Wales Agent-
General in London, once a great statistician,
is now a small knight. Timothy is a
Sydney native, born in Campbell-street 58
years ago. His wife is a daughter of the
late Denis Donnelly, of Cowra, who was
M.L.A. for the district from 1891 to 1896.
Dr. Isa Coghlan is a sister, and his brothers
include Dr. Coghlan, the N.S.W. barrister,
and F. A. Coghlan, the permanent head of
the Chief Secretary’s Department. To George
Dibbs is due the credit of having discovered
Tim’s bent for figures, and effecting his
transfer from the job of works engineer to
fhat of Government Statist, in which he
shone as never another Australian had be-
fore him.
Bishop Moorliouse, Bishop of Melbourne
from 1876 to 1886, and afterwards the
Principal Gaiters of Manchester, gave a
garden party at his home in Devonshire on
November 19 to liven up his 87th birthday.
Ironical fate decreed that Robert Harper,
ex-M.H.R., who has always frowned at the
Gambling Evil in Melbourne, should receive
a testimonial from his old constituents at
Hanging Rock racecourse on New Year’s
Day. A platform was erected under the
trees, nigh the track, and during an interval
between races Robert took the platform to
receive the cheers of the electors of Mernda.
After the function they returned their atten-
tion to the Gambling Evil, and their former
member presumably wished them luck. It
is impossible for even an elder of the kirk
to touch politics without being defiled.
Judge Murray, Lieut.-Gov. of Papua, who
has been merely C.M.G.’d, is son of the
late Terence Aubrey Murray, one-time
Speaker of the N. S. Wales Legislative
Assembly and President of the Legislative
Council, and grandson of Terence Murray,
who had a station at Lake George as far
back as 1820. The present Lieut.-Gov., a
fine athlete in his early days, was called to
the Bar in England in 1886, and practised
in Sydney. His knowledge of law, with his
fine physique, his military experience gained
as Colonel of the Irish Rifles and otherwise,
and the fact that he is 6ft.-odd of fervently
patriotic Australian, made his appointment
°s Lieut.-Gov. of Papua extremely proper
and highly popular.
Neil Elliott Lewis, who is to be made a
Judge of the Supreme Court of Tasmania,
was born on October 27, the anniversary of
Captain Cook’s birthday, 55 years ago. He
is a graduate of Oxford and a barrister of
the Inner Temple. The Lewis family first
arrived in Tasmania in 1815. The pioneer
was Richard Lewis, the new judge’s
grandfather, who went into commercial and
industrial pursuits. N. E. Lewis got his
early education at the High School in. the
Hobart Domain, for which James Anthony
Froude, the historian, was once engaged
as rector, but was “scratched” when the
authorities discovered he had written “The
Nemesis of Faith.” Lewis is a Balliol
College man, and worked under Benjamin
Jowett, the Greek scholar. He has been in
politics since 1886, when he was elected to
the Assembly for Richmond. In 1899 he
became Premier and Attorney-General; in-
cidentally, he was one of the framers of the
Commonwealth Constitution. His wife is a
relative of the late Dr, Richard Youl, who
for a couple of generations was City Coroner
of Melbourne.
Andrew Agnew, of Melbourne, an ancient
and well-known Irish Presbyterian, is re-
ported to have sent Christmas greetings of
a bloodthirsty nature to Carson the Revo-
lutionary. In his cable, Agnew told Carson
to count on the help of Victorian Ulster-
men “if trouble comes,” and advised him
that his “splendid work” was being watched
with “admiration and sympathy.” As Car-
son’s work has so far consisted largely in
urging his countrymen to armed resistance
of Home Rule, it would appear that, imme-
diately following the outbreak of inter-
Irish hostilities, Andrew Agnew is prepared
to put on his war-boots and make for Ire-
land by the first boat. In the calm of pri-
vate life the prospective swashbuckler is a
solid pillar of Scots’ Church, in Collins-
street, where the big bank-balances are. In
business he is a wholesale ironmonger, pro-
fitably and peaceably providing coach-
builders with the metal parts of their
vehicles, and. by reason of something he did
some years ago, owning a top-level reputa-
tion as a white man in financial matters.
Died at Marrickville, on Saturday,
Adolphus Philip Clapin, 1.5.0., aged 85.
Mr. Clapin entered the public service of
N. S. Wales as assistant-clerk in the Lands
Department under Lieut.-Colonel Barney,
R.E., in 1850. Two or three years later he
was transferred to Parliament House as
Clerk of Records, what time Charles Nichol-
son was Speaker and Richard O’Connor
first clerk, and his address was Parliament
House until his retirement in 1907. An
interesting picture of more or less historic
personages was taken on May 23, 1887, on
a lawn of Parliament House, on the occa-
sion of a dinner given by Henry
Parkes to the survivors of the first Assem-
bly. There were then 21 living. Three
-—Daniel Cooper, George Macleay and
Thomas Holt —were in England, and died
there; one, Henry Buckley, was in Queens-
land. Four —James Garland, F. T. Rusden,
Richard Hargrave ‘ind John Richardson—
were in the New England district; the re-
maining 13 were living in or near Sydney.
All near Sydney, except A. T. Holroyd,
who was very ill and died three weeks after-
wards, were present at the banquet, and
are in the picture. There were Henry
Parkes, William Montagu Manning, William
Macleay, W. B. Dailey, W. R. Piddington,
jHin Robertson, G. H. Cox, Edward Flood.
•T. N. Oxley, John Hay. Richard Jones and
Peter Faucett. In addition there were J.
J. Calvert, Clerk of the Parliaments, and
the just-deceased A. P. Clapin, Clerk Assis-
tant, who had been officers of the House
when Responsible Government came along.
J. J. Calvert, now in his 84th year, is the
sole survivor of the Parliament and the
banquet.
I'ut this at least to the credit of Captain
Jacky Evans, the more-or-less new Speaker
of the Tasmanian Assembly—he has refused
to heat his head with the useless flummery
of a wig.

In order that our staff may have the full benefit
of the Christmas holidays, we have decided not to
go to press next week. — BILLYGOAT FLAT BOMHAST.
Died on Christmas Day, William Stephen,
aged 85, who represented Redfern and
Botany in the ’9o’s in N. S. Wales Assem-
bly. He was a Freetrader, and supported
Parkes and Reid in turn, although he was
one of the founders of the fiscally-handi-
capped tannery industry. However, he
lived for 56 years in Botany, so that he had
plenty of time to repent.
“Tennyson Cole, the portrait painter,
has,” the cable says, “been fined £lO or a
month for beating Mrs. Roycroft, the wife of
a surgeon, who was sitting for her portrait.
Cole said he had been married twice and
knew how to manage women.”
His medium is oils, but he
Does not stick to convention’s ruts;
When subjects show stupidity
He illustrates them all with “cuts.”
Died at Perth last week Thomas Duggan,
aged 92 years, the last but one of 60
“Fenians” transported in 1868. Duggan,
who had been a National School teacher at
Ballincallig, Co. Cork, became prominent in
the I.R. brotherhood, was convicted of sedi-
tion, and after two years in Portland prison
was sent to Fremantle in the ship Houge-
mont. On the expiration of his sentence
he became a country school-master.
South Aus. kept its 77th birthday last
week, on the beach at Glenelg, where Gover-
nor Hindmarsh, landing from . the Buffalo,
read his proclamation of a new “province”
on December 28, 1836. This year’s roll was
signed by one man (James Chittleborough)
and two women who actually came in the
Buffalo, and another woman who was in the
infant settlement before the ship arrived.
As for the “pioneers”—any one born or
arriving not later than 1840 —over a hundred
of them were at hand.
A somehow pathetic sight these times is
Thomas Playford, politician from boyhood,
several times Premier of S.A., and ex-
Federal Minister. He is ex-everything now
—has even'left his splendid market-garden
in the Hills, and fills in time as director of
the East End market. In the flowing frock-
coat he always wears he looks like a pai’son,
but a light felt hat destroys that illusion.
Generally walking alone, he seems to be
thinking of past battles. And lie could fight
—the way in which he stood up to Parkes
at the first Federation conference made some
history.
From “J.K.M.” :
It was Charles Bradlaugh who taught the Labor
people of Geelong the trick of delivering a prohibited
speech from a boat moored a few yards out from
shore. In (or about) 1861, Bradlaugh, who had
been lecturing in Plymouth, was invited to lecture in
Devenport Park on a Sunday. As soon as he
tried to speak he was arrested. He returned and
was re-arrested. In court he declared that lie would
deliver the address; and he circulated “dodgers” to
that effect. At the appointed time “Charlie” was
there. So was the crowd ; also the Superintendent
with 28 policemen. Even some soldiers were held
in readiness to quell any riot that might'result. But,
instead of stopping in the park, Bradlaugh led the
crowd to Stonehouse Creek, a small tributary of the
Tamar. Here he embarked on a small boat, rowed
out to a larger boat moored within 9ft. of the
shore, and began his lecture. A few minutes later
the Superintendent and his police burst through the
crowd; but when they saw the position they col-
lapsed. The Superintendent tried to get even with
the boat owner; but the boat had been hired previ-
ously, and the owner had no knowledge of the pur-
pose for which it was to be used. Charles Bradlaugh
knew a little about law.
Captain Bruce, of the Torres pilot ser-
vice, finds half-mile margins in 20-minute
courses mere jokes compared with some of
his experiences in “sail.” He was mate of
the ship Garston when she foundered in
mid-ocean, and the captain (Pye)
and 11 men, including the two
mates, were four weeks in an open
boat with a few tins of bully-beef
and enough water to swear by.
On the 20th day after leaving the
wreck the provisions were down to
two tins of meat, and the desperate
crew demanded the lot. Annesty,
the other mate, lay in the bottom
of the boat stupefied by exhaustion,
and Pye and Bruce held the food
against the madmen. Said Pye:
“I’ll throw it overboard first. You
fools ! Our only chance is in mak-
ing the rations last out. If you
eat it all to-dav what will you do
to-morrow?” To which one of the
foreigners replied : “Dere is blenty
of dwo-lekked animalse,” and the
sinister proposal took possession of
the men. Pye and Bruce deter-
mined to capsize the boat and let
all drown together if any attempt,
to kill one of the company fol-
lowed on the proposed casting of
lots, though certainly, lot or no
lot, the choice would have fallen
on Annesty—the most exhausted of
them all. For days captain and
mate kept discipline, in the adher-
ence to the ration scale at least;
and before the scant provision was quite
exhausted the wrecked men sighted land.
After that experience trouble with even a
wild Marine Board doesn’t amount to much.
George V.’s semi-annual branding saw
four Maorilanders decorated with the
pathetic totem marks. Charles Bowen, the
oldest (83), had K.C.M.G. tacked on to
him; but it won’t give him a more gentle
character, nor make him a more able Speaker
of the Legislative Council. Ernest Ruther-
ford, the youngest (42) and most-distin-
guished, was marked K. 8., but it is un-
likely that his great promise in scientific
research will slump on that account. John
Shand, of Otago University, found himself
traced C.M.G., yet it will add not one grain
to his storehouse of knowledge regard-
ing educational matters. Premier Massey
was passed along the P.C. token, which
might stand for Politically Conservative,
but more likely just means the old Petty
Compliment.

Arthur Bristowe, dead in Adelaide after a
long and plucky fight with lingering disease,
was one of that city’s best-known share-
brokers, though he specialised lately in com-
panies’ secretarial work. He was business
manager of the Associated in Kalgoorlie at
one time, secretary to the Fire Brigades
Board of S.A.. and pretty well everything
one can be in the accountancy line. He was
only 44 when he passed out.
A RHYME OF SILKEN FETTERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The new Governor of Victoria “has been married
eight years, and is the father of three children, but
he does not look so much the family man.’’
—Age (1/1/14).
He was a sprightly sort of bloke
Who did not mumble as he spoke;
Nor did he humbly hang his head,
Habitually depressed. Instead,
With quick, elastic step he went
Upon his way, his back unbent;
His teeth were still a perfect set,
Nor was he bald at all. And yet—■
Believe it if you can —
He was a married man !
His coat was neither crudely cut,
Nor threadbare, nor old-fashioned, but
Quite smart; and, though he was not stout,
His shoulder-blades did not stick out
As married men’s so often do.
He was nowise a person who
Appeared downtrodden by his Fate,
Nor driven dully desperate.
Astonishing that he
A married man should be !
No scent of cheap tobacco clung
Persistently his clothes among;
No home-clipned whiskers grew around
His chin. His shoes were new and sound.
To judge by the sweet socks he wore,
He was a dashing bachelor —
A merry, careless soul whose will
Was never crossed by woman’s. Still,
Despite his manner bright,
Married he was, all right!
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The principal feature of the half-year’s
Treasury accounts for N. S. Wales is, as usual of late,
the perfectly mad loan expenditure. This amounted
to £4,478,428, or £248,300 more than in the cor-
responding half of the previous year. If this money
was going into businesses which would earn interest
on the capital the position would not he so bad ;
but, as the official figures show, even the railways
are not now earning interest, so rapidly have work-
ing exes, gone up, and so heavily has the department
been loaded of late with unprofitable lines. More-
over, if the interest on the capital spent on unopened
lines were added to the bill—as it ought to be-*-the
position would be still worse. However, the Govern-
ment appealed to the country asking whether the
drunk should continue; and —quite naturally, of
course—the unsober multitude said Yes. It isn't
much of a time to ask a man whether he will have
a sixth drink when he’s had a fifth. Certainly he
will, and hang the expense! Fill-’em-up-again—and
the baby pays. But the baby isn’t going to do all
the paying. Treasurer Cann, who, by all accounts,
is to be succeeded presently by Treasurer Holman,
has evidently got tired of the worry of floating 12
months’ bills, and has just had a long loan of
£3,000,000 underwritten in London. It is not repay-
able for 28 years and upwards. But it carries 4 p.c.
and is issued at 96 ; so the other way of putting it
is that for 28 .years and upwards the country is
committed to pay £4 7s. p.c. for money, as against
the average rate of £3 10s. sd. for N. S. Wales
loans outstanding on June 30, 1911. That is the
price this country has to pay for carrying out works
at a time when the money market is such that no
sane or knowledgable Government would borrow more
'than it could possibly help.
N. S. Wales State revenue for the half-
year was £8,300,884, or £825,555 more than in the
corresponding half of 1912. The expenditure charged
to revenue account was £8,965,097. That is to
say, the revenue wasn’t sufficient to pay ordinary out-
goings (apart from loan works) by £664,213.' That
looks pretty dreadful ; still it is not quite as bad as
the position was 12 months ago. The deficit at that
time was £1,059.432. Here, then, is a rule-of-three
sum. If the deficit was £1,059,432 at December 31
in one year, and that financial year ended with a
deficit of £1,167,017, what will the final deficit be
in a year when it was £664,213 on December 31?
You can make it £730,000 and keep the change. That
is how the deficit for the current year is shaping.
Yet The Bulletin sees frequent remarks in the
newspapers, inspired by the Treasury, that Mr. Cann
is “sitting down hard.” Even if Mr. Cann knew
how to sit down hard he wouldn’t he allowed to.
“Who is Cann,” fellow-Ministers ask, “that he should
govern his equals?” The sooner Holman gets to the
Treasury the better. He at least will be entitled to
say “No!”
But though the N.S.W. outgoings for the past six
months have been nominally onlv £430,336 more
than for the corresponding half of 1912, while the
revenue was £825 555 more, there are one or two
things to be said. Of the outgoings in 1912,
£211,890 was paid as sinking fund to wipe out old
debt. Nothing whatever has gone that way in the
past half-year. Also, £140.887 less has been paid
in local government subsidies—which means in most
cases that the assistance has been merely deferred.
Also, very little has been paid for maintenance and
repair of public buildings, etc. Deduct those
amounts from the improvement shown by the past
half-vear and there isn’t anv improvement left that’s
worth talking about. In other words, at the end of
the first half there is practically no improvement
on the position which was shown at the end of the
first half of the previous year ; and that year ended
with a deficit of £1,167,017. That, plainly, is the
size of the financial situation which will face Hol-
man when he steps into the Treasury.
Australian foreign-trade returns for 11
months to the end of November: —
Imports (merchandise) . . £70,296.039
Exports (merchandise) . . 64,577,180
Excess of imports . . £5,718,859
This means that in the 11 months Australia lived
beyond its means to the extent of £5,718,859 plus
the total amount of interest on its public and private
loans and the dividends on Australian, shares held in
England. The interest on the public loans for the
11 months was approximately £8,635,000, and the
proportion directly payable to John Bull was
£6,565,000. Exclusive, therefore, of the interest
and dividends on private loans and investments (in-
cluding the very big dividends in Kalgoorlie, Broken
Hill and Cloncurry mining cos.), Australia lived
beyond its means to the extent of (say) £12,250,000
during the 11 months. And then some people say
there really isn't a boom, and that the signs of pros-
perity which they see around them are due to good
seasons and high prices !

This pastoral and pastoral-finance co. celebrates its
50th year by giving its already well-fed shareholders
an extra £13,000 in the shape of a 21 p.c. special
bonus. (Of its usual 121 p.c. div. it calls 2i p.c. a
bonus.) Still, profits are less than they have been
sometimes, though the dazzling £189,349 in 1911
was quite exceptional, and was largely due to the
sale of station properties at prices in excess of book
values. These sales followed on the progressive land
tax, which this and other cos. were so alarmed about.
As a matter of fact the price got for land suitanle
for close settlement has nearly always been far in
excess of the book value —which was the sheep
value. This is very good proof that the land wasn’t
being put to its best use. Further, although a big
co. would have to pay a pretty stiff tax if it held
its big estates, it was able to pass the land on to
people who mostly escaped the tax, the smaller hold-
ings being usually worth less than £5000; conse-
quently the co., though it talked a lot about the
, depreciation of the capital value on account of the
tax, was able to sell the land as though it carried
no tax at all. In other words, if they sold, the big
landlords suffered nothing at all from the tax. Cer-
tainly this co. had to move on and find other empty
spaces, and it preferred its chance of getting them
or some of them in Argentina rather than rely
wholly on the remoter places of Australia. It has
therefore established an office in Buenos Ayres, and
two or three thousand pounds spent in organising the
business there have been charged up with the past
year s working exes.
The amounts shown for the past two years as
written off have mostly gone in writing down the
reserve-fund investments—gilt-edged tnings like
British Consols, which it was thought would give
tone to any reserve fund. Quite a lot of directors
have rearranged their views about the virtue of
British investments for nest-egg purposes. There
was a time when some Australian Boards thought
that the last word was said when they had reported
that their shareholders’ reserves were in Consols.
Since then much good, hard Australian profit has
had to go towards providing for the dreadful shrink-
age that has taken place.
Like a good many old hands at the game of
pastoral finance, this co, doesn’t rely much on the
casual deposit, but raises most of the money it
needs, in addition to its own capital, by debenture
stock. Its share capital is if 520,000 ; its debenture
stock (nearly all 4 p.c.), £2,000,000, less £86,660
which it has repurchased. All other liabilities total
£388,075, including trade accounts and customers’
credit balances. In cash and investments (chiefly
the British stocks before-mentioned) the co. has
£616,089 ; and the balance of its assets consist of
properties and advances on properties, stock and
other securities ( £2,257,183) and premises, advances
on wool, customers’ debit balances and sundries
( £525,054). Going in largely for pastoral property-
owning itself, its profits are erratic, because in the
nature of things the man who runs sheep and cattle
must go into country which the agriculturist wants
least, because its rainfall is least dependable. But it
is a business in which there are wonderful ups as
well as distressing downs ; and for the man who can
hang on long enough, and who knows the business
and doesn’t cripple his country by over-stock-
ing, the profits are far greater" than the losses.
The man or the co. that makes a mess of things is
the man or the co. that either doesn’t know the busi-
ness, or has to realise when a drought has driven
values temporarily down towards zero. And the one
who has to do that is nearly always the one who has
borrowed money which is liable to be called up at
very short notice. Hence the wisdom of this co. in
financing itself on debenture stock.
Melbourne Ice Skating and Refriger-
ating Co.:—

1908 . . 1245 Nil 1500 1735
On pref. shares.
1909 .. 2067 74 p.c. = 232 1500 2070
1910 .. 2366 7| „ „ 375 1042 3019
1911 .. 1963 74 „ „ 375 4607
1912 .. 1325 74 „ „ 375 1500 4057
1913 .. 2768 74 ~ ~ 375 1101 5349
The co. started fairly well, as most amusement cos.
do. ; but when the first ice-skating craze died, it was
in trouble. The directors then turned their atten-
tion from Society to mutton, and are apparently find-
ing much more profit in it. Also, they went in for
the ice-supply business. So the co. has three strings
to its bow now—(1) cold-storage accommodation ;
(2) ice-supply; (3) the Glaciarium, wherein, in the
season, the young thing just back from England may
tell her Johnnie-boy of the ripping time she had on
the ice “at Home,” and thus establish herself among
the Elect. When the capital put up in exchange
for shares proved insufficient to carry out the altered
programme, first £5OOO was raised on pref. shares
and then a small mortgage already existing was in-
creased to £12,500, at which it now stands. Since
the first year the only people who have got any in-
terest on their capital are these pref. shareholders
and the mortgagees. Nevertheless last year’s profit,
after paying interest and pref. dividend and writing
£llOl off for depreciation, was sufficient to show 5
p.c. on the ordinary capital of £24,500. True, there
is some doubt whether £llOl was enough to write
off plant, etc., to cover depreciation, which in this
industry is notoriously heavy ; and if it isn’t enough
the 5 p.c. on ordinary capital wasn’t earned. How-
ever, even allowing for that, the position and outlook
are not hopeless, by any means. Ice-skating, though
it is too expensive to become a popular craze, will
always command a certain amount of support—pro-
bably enough to earn its own exes, and a bit over,
as a rule—while the demand for cold storage and for
ice is likely to go on increasing. The co. owns its
freehold, so if it pays interest it at least is free
from the monthly call of the landlord. Also, it will
never have to pay anything extra for its quarters
even when Melbourne has grown to twice the present
size, and the country grows twice as many lambs,
and this co. has twice the present chances of making
money.

The reserves are made up as follows:

As far as can be ascertained, the plant, etc., are not
written down, the only provision for wear and tear
being made through the Depreciation Fund. If so,
the true reserves are considerably less than £74,122 ;
also, of course, the true profit for the past half-year
is less than £11,369. If in the production of the
half-year’s revenue, plant, etc., have deteriorated to
the extent of the amount which has been added to
the Depreciation Fund, then the half-year’s profit
was £7518, which was only £lOlB in excess of the
dividend ; and, remembering that something like a
construction boom has still been in progress during
the six months, and that prices for timber have been
quite exceptional, this is by no means a comfortable
margin. Certainly the profit is still a great deal
more than it was a few years ago ; but against that
has to be put the fact that the capital, which was
only £77,500 to start with, is now £130,000. More-
over, the co.’s liabilities are again pretty heavy.
This shows how matters have moved in that respect:
Mar., 1907.. £31,415 Sept., 1910 .. £45,112
Sept., 1907.. 39,848 Mar., 1911 .. 32,468
Mar., 1908 . . 38,342 Sept., 1911 . . 38,768
Sept., 1908 .. 60,368 Mar., 1912 . . 46,796
Mar., 1909 . . 89,701 Sept., 1912 . . 72,276
Sept., 1909 . . 50,801 Mar., 1913 . . 55,836
Mar., 1910 . . 45,606 Sept., 1913 .. 70,958
Ihe co. doesn’t seem able to keep the figures down in
spite of the frequent calls for new capital. 'this
seems to be due mainly to the accumulation of
stocks: in two years they have increased from
f 61.510 to £101,435, while advances on logs and
stock in transit have gone along from £23,617 to
£ 04,598. An accumulation of £70,000 of extra
stock in two years seems much for a co. with a
capital of £130,000. Possibly further increased
costs are threatening the ' timber-millers—the
men in the toilsome business certainly are not
satisfied with the money they used to make a few years
ago. But whether prices for logs will go still higher
and stay up there is a matter of opinion. One thing
seems certain—-the retail prices which have ruled
for timber during the last year or two will not be
maintained when the construction rush is over. The
N. S. Wales Government alone spent £7,000,000 or
so of borrowed money last year, and, though it has
•just had another loan of £8,000,000 underwritten
in London, it has had to pay such a price as it has
never had to pay since the hoom and the burst of
25 years ago. Nothing is surer than that the recent
rate of borrowing can’t continue; and nothing is
surer, either, than that the people who have gained
most, directly and indirectly, by the boom while it
has lasted are the timber-merchants and other sup-
pliers of construction materials.

Profit is shown “after making full allowance for
everything in the nature of bad and doubtful debts
and the usual appropriation for depreciation.” What
the “usual appropriation” is we don’t know. And,
of course, the visible profits may be made large or
small by the very simple process of making the
“usual appropriation” small or large. It is a case
in which the investor has to take a great deal on
trust, not merely in regard to this matter of wear
and tear and depreciation generally, but in regard
to stocks and other things. Stocks, the directors
say, are “valued under cost”—which is an elastic
remark. It may mean that, in addition to accumu-
lating visible reserves, the directors have put them-
selves as safe as they know how by writing down
assets below their true value, and thus creating in-
ternal reserves. The Bulletin certainly wouldn’t
blame them for doing that sort of thing. The timber
man, and especially the timber-man who deals so
largely in big sticks, and does so much business with
Governments and other public bodies, has been having
such an innings lately as he can hope to have only
twice or thrice in a lifetime. Hence the prudent
timber co.’s management will undoubtedly, if it
wants to save itself trouble in the future, drop a
little of the fruits of the present abnormal harvest
into a stocking, to draw upon when conditions
change.
But even if the Allen Taylor people are really
doing no better than they show, they are doing pretty
well, the lay-by after paying the 10 p.c. div. being
£3302 for the year. Moreover, debts are very
moderate, wherefore a slump wouldn’t find the co.
loaded with interest on liabilities incurred against
heavy, dull stocks. This shows the movement of the
figures in that and other respects:—•
Liabilities.
