The “cash, etc.,” item above is exclusive of £20,300.
to the credit of the Insurance and Reserve Fund;
this is invested apart from the business, mostly in
bank deposits and public securities. The co.’s liquid
assets therefore exceed the liabilities. Nevertheless
“in pursuance of the gradual process of development
foreshadowed in previous reports,” 25,000 new shares
(one to five) are to be issued at par—a nice little
bonus, seeing that the co. may, on its figures, reason-
ably be expected to pay 10 p.c. on the increased capi-
tal. Altogether, the accounts and the report make a
good impression. True, the hand of R. McC. Ander-
son, one of the managing directors, can be traced in
the report, and one wouldn’t expect R. McO. Anderson
to put things badly if they could be put attractively;
still, allowing for all that, there is the fact that
the div. is apparently being very com fortably earned,
while the co. has so few liabilities that their total is
exceeded by the liquid assets, without even reckoning
book debts as liquid assets.
* * * *
Once a year, generally on New Year’s
Day, we get an extra-choice bit of nonsense in the
daily papers —a return showing the new cos. regis-
tered during the year, along with their capital. So
far as N. S. Wales is concerned, this is how the
figures have run for the past six years:—
Companies. Capital.
1908 .. .. 196 £3,850,175
1909 . . . . 251 6,791,157
1910 . . . . 329 6,975,691
1911 .. 393 10,517,217
1912 .. .. 428 13,896,231
1913 . . ■ • 429 12,125,737
Of that £12,125,737 about £550,000 was paid in
money by the public. What a wild farce the whole
business is may be judged by the figures for two
moderately new cos. whose accounts have lately been
dissected in the Wild Cat Column. The nominal
capital of those two concerns is £1,000,000, and
for that amount they figure in the Registrar’s return.
The net capital actually subscribed was under
£30,000. The majority of the new cos. registered
asked for no capital from the public at all: it was
merely a case of private firms being turned into
limited cos. without the issue of any new capital.
The inflation of the figures shows one thing clearly
the unhealthy condition which has been produced
by the Government’s borrow-and-boom policy.
“Y. and A.” : Australian Stock Exchange
mining business all through 1913 has not been large
or profitable. There has been no big new field found ;
no distinctly new processes have been started on old
fields; no Bullfinch boom has helped the weary
broker. The truth is there are too many brokers
on every Australian Stock Exchange. In Melbourne
there are 129 seats, in Brisbane 28, in Sydney 66,
in Adelaide 72, in Hobart 11, and Launceston 25.
In every instance these figures are twice what they
should be. Half the number of brokers in each city
could do the work and earn a living. Why, even the
Paris Exchange has fewer members than that of
Melbourne —the number there is limited to 80.
The Stock Exchange of Melbourne Co.,
which owns the Stock Exchange building and Safe
Deposit, made a poor net profit for 12 months of
£556. The ground and building cost £271,877
during the land boom, and the property appears in
the books at £68,614 —which is about its market
value to-day. The capital paid up is £108,055;
loan on mortgage, £82,500 ; loan from Stock Ex-
change, £17,000. So the total of capital and lia-
bilities is £207,555, now represented by the build-
ing valued at £68,614.
The New York Life office in Australia has
been told by cable that the co.’s new business in
1918 exceeded £47,672,000, which is the limit
allowed under the N’York insurance law. The head
office added its congratulations on the Australian
Department’s contribution to the result.
* * * *
According to an excellent trade summary
in Sydney Telegraph, 60 leading Australian invest-
ment stocks, quoted on the Sydney market, showed
an aggregate rise of £2,335,800 during 1913. The
average rise in quotations during the j'ear was 4.6
p.c. In the two previous years there had been shrink-
ages. The market value added since 1900 to stocks
then on the list has averaged 18 p.c. Practically the
whole of this addition occurred between 1905-9
inclusive —the years when business earnings bounded
upwards as a result of good seasons, and when, as a
general thing, wages hadn’t moved up very much to
cut into those extra earnings.
“C.E.”: It may be regarded as certain
that the four motor-’bus cos. in Melbourne will be
amalgamated. They are conferring on the point.
Look at the development. The so-called Motor
Omnibus Co. has 18 ’buses, with seven ordered. The
Motor Bus Service Co. has four, with 12 ordered.
The Globe Co. has seven, and Withers and Sons four.
Thus there will soon be 52 ’buses. The rapidity
with which they have sprung up has only been
measured by the difficulty of getting them. As in
all such cases, amalgamation is forced on by hostile
parties. There is expected legislative and possible
municipal “interference.” The Tramway Co. is on
the qui vive, too. It was suspected of having a
finger already in the motor pie, but has none. In
1915 it hands over its lines to the municipalities.
Who can doubt that its directors contemplate a
plunge into motors then ? Further, there are rumors
of foreign capital coming in. And, meantime, the
municipalities—well, they apparently are waiting
till they will have to pay the usual big price to buy
somebody out.

The change in the management of Great
Cobar was apparently due to the ructions raised by a
London committee appointed at a meeting of dissatisfied
shareholders. Evidently ' shareholders _ haven’t yet
realised how bad things are, and believe that mis-
management is all to blame for their troubles. As
a matter of fact, those troubles started before even
the co. was formed. The sampling of the mine, to
begin with, gave results which the old Cobar Syndi-
cate had never been able to approach; but it was
no business either of the syndicate or of the syndi-
cate’s manager, G. H. Blakemore, to tell them that
their figures were too high. Even had the figures
been right the “watering” was outrageous; with the
figures wrong, the capitalisation was simply ridicu-
lous. Then the Australian manager, who knew the,
mine and what it was capable of doing, was got rid
of and a man who didn’t know it was put in charge ;
and an utterly unsuitable plant was provided at great
cost. The co., finding itself in the soup, might
now do the graceful thing and call in Blakemore for
a report. He, anyhow, is the one man who has run
the Great Cobar successfully.
* * * *
The first report by the directors of the
Wild Cherry, Ardlethan (N.S.W.), is not good read-
ing. The co. has a nominal capital of £150,000 in
£1 shares, and £105,000 is paid up. “Ore in
sight”— i.e., measured up on three sides —is 30,600
tons of an assay value of £55,400, and there is ore
at grass valued at £5265. The co. has a million
gallons of water in its three-million gallon tank, and
hopes to mine and mill for 12s. a ton, which would
give a net return of £37,000 —if all the assay value
is recovered. Recovery percentage will, however, be
an unknown quantity until after a month or two
of practice. A mill is to go up at once to treat 300
tons a week, and “Mr. E. P. Johnson, consulting
engineer to the c 0.,” believes that “even 1 p.c. ore
would leave a considerable margin of profit to the
c 0.,” which statement is merely hot air. Certainly
the estimates of ore in sight include 500 tons worth
10 p.c. and 4300 tons worth 3 p.c. ; but the balance
of the estimate is inade up of 25,800 tons of 0.5 p.c.
Of the ore at grass 212 tons represent £3124; 475
tons represent £1714 ; and the balance of 835 tons,
worth £427, was not worth mining. Of the £15,000
placed to working capital, the co. has spent £2608
on mine development, £B3l on office, legal and
accountancy charges, and £1791 on “plant, machin-
ery and water supply.” “Preliminary expenses” were
£3367, in additionto the purchase price of the pro-
perty, i.e., £90,000. The co. has £6400 on hand
in cash, fixed deposits, loans at call and stores. The
position of the show at flotation may be figured from
the fact that six months after purchase for £30,000
cash and £60,000 in shares, there is a possible profit
of (say) £30,000. The name of William Soutar on
the Board is an earnest of fair dealing; but at
latest it looks not improbable that somebody else
will be engineered .on to the directorate.
Broken Hill Prop, has been shaking up
its lead and silver output in view of the closing of
its financial year on November 30. Hauling 25,152
tons out of the mine, milling 23,641 tons and
regrinding 20,427 tons of dump tailings, was some-
thing unusual for a four-weeks’ run. The output of
domestic lead rose to 3367 tons in November, followed
by 4822 tons in the next four weeks to December 17.

New Burragorang, Yerranderie (N.S.W.),
before closing down for the holidays, despatched 10
tons of ore to the smelter. They are stoping on a
6in. vein over No. 5 level; and that is the sort of
proposition Burragorang is. Next door the Yerran-
derie is down 1410 ft. The lode is a true fissure, but
mostly it is little more than a streak. Altogether
the district, from 1900 to 1912, produced 53,667 tons
of ore, containing 5,380,7990 z. silver, 99510 z. gold,
and 12,032 tons of lead. The feature is the high
average—over lOOoz. per ton —for silver. In 1912
the output was 7055 tons of ore, valued at*
£126,859, or an average of over £l7 per tom There
is nothing exceptional in small shipments averaging
£2O per ton ; in fact, the Colon Peaks ore averaged
£25 for 2016 tons.
* * * *
Great Fingall (W.A.) is nibbling at the
Big Bell leases, which the Great Boulder refused to
swallow. The show may not suit the Boulder, and
yet be worth something to the Fingall. For instance,
the former would, in the event of purchase, have to
lay out a big sum on plant and equipment, whereas
the Fingall could put down a tram-line and rail the
ore a distance of 20 miles or thereabouts to its own
mill. The Bulletin hears, however, that the
Boulder sampling showed the value of the property
to have been much over-rated locally.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yds. of
road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yd., and the cost of the spalls per cub. yd. averaged
2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub. yd.,
broken and spread. Their last contract under the
hand-breaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per cub.
yd., stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a savin;/ of 2s. sd.
per cub. yd. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [3

South Kalgurli (W.A.) is still looking
for wealth in the Hainault lease, but so far without
success. When the South Kalgurli took it over the
show was almost as bare of ore reserves as old-
woman Hubbard’s cupboard was of bones, and a
good deal of money will have to be sunk in develop-
ment before any substantial return in the shape of
mill supplies can be looked for. In any case the
property has long since passed its prime, and is
never likely to yield much profit to its present
owners.
* * * *
In 1913 Great Boulder’s income in excess
of mining, treatment, development, capital and other
Australian charges was approximately £300,000, or
a few thousands less than in 1912. To date the mine
has produced about £50,000 short of nine millions
sterling, and has paid nearly 44 millions in divi-
dends. With the passing of its main ore shoot into
the Horseshoe, its days as a big gold producer are,
however, numbered.
News from the front indicates that all is
not well with the Associated Northern Co.’s Vic-
torious leases at Ora Banda. Values are alleged to
be patchy, extensions short, and extraction trouble-
some. The show is capably handled, but the best
management that ever breathed can’t put gold into
ore, and there are still some refractory conditions
which baffle scientific treatment.
Whim Well (W.A.) held its annual meet-
ing in London on November 27, when the total ship-
ments for the year were given as 7850 tons, of a
gross value of £80,708, and the ore at grass as
60,600 tons, worth 44 p.c. There were also said to
be 100,000 tons of developed 5 p.c. ore in the
mine, while the probable ore of the same grade
above water level was estimated at 10 times that
quantity] The net profit for the period under review
was £20,175, which, added to the previous year’s
balance of £9714 (after providing a 5 p.c. div. paid
in July last, and the manager’s commission), gave
a total of £29,889 to deal with. Of this amount
£9761 has been written off, leaving £20,127 to be
carried forward. The heavy drain on the co.’s cash
resources incidental to the completion of the Murex
and other plant is now declared to be at an end,
and increased divs. are promised. Frank Powell has
just had a look at the property on behalf of some
London and Paris stockholders.
Bones of the chairman’s statement at the
Whim Well meetings: Ore accumulations on
surface now total 60,000 tons, of a gross value of
£200,000. .. .The net profit that will be derived
from this is estimated at £BB,OOO. . . .The ore imme-
diately available in the mine is computed at not less
than 100,000 tons, and this, with the 60,000 tons at
grass, should provide about three years’ mill supply.
.... Handsome profits are expected from the treat-
ment of this stuff.... Co. now has a splendid pro-
perty fully equipped, and with millions of tons of
ore awaiting development. As regards the financial
position, owing to increased cost of labor, delays in
getting plant to work, etc., the original estimate for
the cost of concentration plant had been considerably
exceeded. Consequent on these delays, further large
tonnages augmented the smelter piles, meaning a fur-
ther lock-up of the co.’s capital. Now all is clear
ahead.

Dividends still keep beyond the reach of
Menzies Consolidated (W.A.). So far about
150,0000 z, (fine) have been dug out of this mine,
and of this total ll,ooooz. were unearthed last
year. The revenue account for that period, with
£79 brought forward, shows a credit balance of
£4093 ; but with £2540 written off for machinery,
equipment, shaft-sinking, etc., only £B6 is left
to carry forward. The co. hopes to distribute profits
some day. It is much the safest to leave it indefinite
like that,
Oroya Links (W.A.) took £39,868 out of
its Kalgoorlie properties for the quarter ended Sep-
tember, and laid out £34,870, inclusive of what
was spent on plant and development. Royalties
derived from tributors totalled £349. A good deal
of time and energy was given to making the
Croesus Prop, shaft on the Eclipse leases the main
hauling way. This work is now completed and will,
it is claimed, greatly facilitate operations and reduce
costs.
* * * *
Burbanks Main Lode (W.A.) announces
a profit of £5466 for the past financial year. De-
duct £lB7B absorbed by a 5 p.c. div. paid in May
last, and a balance of £3588 is left. This sum
has been carried forward. Undivided profits now
total £15,964, but all of this has been consumed
by mine re-equipment and development. As a
result scrip holders have to do without the usual
end-of-the-vear div. The existence in the two bottom
levels of shoots of gold assaying up to 20dwt. and
25dwt. per ton, and the fact that the mine is now
equipned with up-to-date plant, gives, according to
the Board, “reassuring prospects of an early re-
sumption of returns to shareholders.”
* * * *
Lancefield (W.A.), with its miles and
miles of workings, unsatisfactory plant and dismal
record, has become the nropertv of the Kalgoorlie and
Boulder Firewood Co. Jack Dunstan, one-time metal-
lurgist at the Associated, and more recently super-
intendent of State batteries, has been appointed
manager, and word is being passed round that he will
have things in full swing again inside six months.
The co. starts with hundreds of thousands of tons of
ore in sight; and if the mine can’t be made to pav
with this stand-by, it is a greater delusion than its
worst enemy ever reckoned it to be.
From Boulder Perseverance’s latest quar-
terly report: “Exploring the older workings of the
mine has disclosed good grade ore with every pros-
pect of benefiting the reserves. Notably is this the
case between the 700 ft. and 900 ft. levels on the F
lode, 700 ft. level Perseverance lode and the 200 ft.
and 500 ft. levels El Ora lode. The low grade of the
ore crushed during the period under review is due to
the fact that but a small tonnage has been available
from the richer stopes. Ore reserves remain practi-
cally unaltered.” From ore treatment £66,615 was
derived during the quarter, but working exes., with
£7053 spent on development, swallowed up £62,850
of this.
* * * *
Ziegler’s, at Kalgoorlie (W.A L is re-
ported to have some ore in sight above 150 ft., and is
being provided with a Huntingdon mill to chew it up.
The show is leased by the little Adelaide Enterprise
Syndicate, and is perched on the side of Mount Char-
lotte, about a quarter of a mile north of Hannan’s
original find. An expert recently sent from Adelaide
to size things up reckoned that there were dividends
in the ground.
Bullrush’s paralysing circular announcing
a drop from 365. to 295. in the manager’s estimated
value of the ore reserves, coupled with the conse-
quent refusal of Gold Industry, Ltd., to carry out its
agreement to guarantee calls, has knocked the co.
clean off its financial perch. Several thousand pounds
are said to be needed to give the mine a fair chance
to show its real capacity, and although strenuous
efforts are being made to get temporary accommoda-
tion from a certain mining crowd, negotiations look
like ending in failure. Meantime work is at a stand-
still, and some difficulty is likely to be experienced
in getting in calls.
* * * *
Princess Royal (W.A.) now provides
means of subsistence for a tribute party or two, and
furnishes an occasional div. in the shape of royalty
and battery pickings to the co. A shareholders’ roll-
up was held in Adelaide the other day, when the
amount in credit was £467, made up of cleanings of
battery plates, sale of concentrates and royalties.
Two Northern tin shows are gaining favor
in Melbourne, where dealers have given Lode Hill
shares a run on the strength of borehole tests and
favorable reports. This property gave no fortune to
the hard-working party which stuck to it for years,
but if tests go for anything, there is enough tin
about to give the co. a good start. The water-
supply has been greatly improved, and the decom-
posed granite, of which the hill mostly consists, will
in future be handled to better advantage. If there is
a fly in the co.’s ointment it is that there are
hard spots in the granite, and the tin contents vary
considerably. The Annan River Co. has placed its
preference shares (24,000 at 20s. each), and pump-
ing machinery is being installed to get a supply of
water from a dam under construction. This hitherto
struggling concern is expected to treat 30,000 cub.
yards per month. When the Annan tin field was dis-
covered tin oxide was selling at about £56 per ton.
Favored with double that price, sluicing of large
faces of low-grade dirt should be profitable.
Chillagoe looks in for its third sixpenny
call, making shares paid up to 9s. 6d. There are
1,200,000 shares with 'a nominal value of 10s. per
share. Ore that averages from £4to£ 5 in value would
under ordinary circumstances give handsome profits ; but
in this case the supply isn’t regular or abundant, and
it is drawn from too many small mines to allow
of low costs. Mount Mulligan coal may help a little;
but the real anxiety is to find a good metal mine.
St. Mungo, in the Cloncurry district (Q.),
has had a dry half-year. While the copper market
was good the little co. gouged out its rich ore
merrily. Then came the day when development had
to be looked to, and profits disappeared. Only
£llOO worth of ore was raised during the six
months, and the exes, were £1676, including £64
for depreciation of plant. The outlook is, however,
still cheerful, and with half a dozen faces of the
rich but bunchy ore from which the mine has
hitherto made its money, dividends ought to come
back again within the next few months. The
financial position is still quite good: liquid assets
exceed the liabilities by about £l5OO.
Manager Corbould’s summing-up to the
directors of Mount Elliott (Q.), after going through
the concentrating mill: “Your position to-day is:
You have a certain tonnage in Elliott and Consols
mines of high-grade ore in sight, from which hand-
some profits can be won ; also you have a tonnage of
probable ore which will, I think, later on be ore in
sight. But a time arrives in the life of a property
when, the points of attack being less owing to the
narrowing down of faces, it becomes impossible to
win the necessary tonnage to keep the treatment
plant in full operation, with the result that costs
become high and profits fall away. Below the No. 4 level
in Elliott, the high-grade ore, in small patches, lives
down; but the facts at present are that the high-
grade ore is small and scattered over too wide a
channel to pay. The Consols has the making of a
good mine; but so far developments do not warrant
classing it as a property from which a large tonnage
per day can be relied upon to keep a treatment plant
going on its own ; and the ore, being high in sulphur,
should be reserved for blending. Some of our pro-
specting ventures give promise of becoming mines of
medium-grade ore; but before a copper proposition
on this [Cloncurry] field is of any value as a profit-
earner it must be coupled up by rail in order to
handle the necessary tonnage economically. The
high-grade ores are excellent profit-producers so long
as they continue; but the medium and lower-grade
ores are the mainstay in giving long life. Viewing
the copper mines of the world, the grade of the ore
gets poorer with depth. There are exceptional mines,
but few ; and it is not to be expected that Cloncurry
is going to be an exception. The permanency of a
mining co. is in the medium and, later, the lower-
grade ores ; and before the high-grade ores give place
to the low it becomes a matter of looking ahead and
increasing the tonnage, altering and enlarging the
treatment plant to cope with the new conditions, and
making a smaller profit per ton over a larger tonnage
treated. It is most advisable to further secure or
absorb certain properties to supply a central treat-
ment plant with a large tonnage of medium-grade
ore; and I am convinced that no mining field in
Australia offers a better proposition for investment
than Cloncurry, on the line of linking up a group
of properties by rail and erecting a central treatment
plant to treat a large tonnage of medium-grade ore.”
Brilliant Extended; Charters Towers, has
declared a sixpenny, payable this month. A good
average return has been forthcoming, in spite of the
hot weather, which makes ventilation difficult. The
Deeps comes second to the Extended in point of
stone crushed: for 11 months, Extended, 23,960
tons, £54,030; Deeps, 7856 tons, £33,388. The
Deeps continues to sink under difficulties, for the
flow of water is heavy and the ventilation imperfect.
A big improvement in the latter respect will arrive
when the Blocks shaft meets No. 4 level; it is
close there now.
* * * *
New Brilliant Freehold, Charters Towers,
is letting the battery talk. The last lot put through
was 646 tons for £2136, somewhat poorer than the
previous 612 tons for £2383. The local market is
slack, because the bottom level (No. 6) is showing
too much white stone.
The Papuan Brilliant and Victoria,
Charters Towers, has connected its workings with the
old Havelock Moonstone shaft, and improved ventilation
has followed. The co. will continue to raise stone from
the footwall and hanging wall sections of the Moonstone,
and will also be able to prove the value of the cross
reef. This mine gave £12.408 from 1793 tons last
year, an average of over £6 19s. per ton. But the
bottom level is the poorest and reserves are light,
so, in spite of its flash of prosperity, the Papuan
is small goods in the market.
At the Hampden-Cloncurry meeting a
while back the chairman’s account of development
work was cheerful, as every chairman’s speech is ex-
pected to be. The deepest mine is the Duchess, whose
next level will be at 700 ft. To expedite prospecting
two winzes have been sunk from the 550 ft. level.
They met with rather long blanks from 70ft. to 89ft.
and from 62ft. to 87ft. respectively. These two
winzes are 140 ft. apart, and the one furthest north
is 365 ft. from the shaft. How much of the 365 ft. is
blank and how much is payable cannot be told until
the 700 ft. level is through. At the Hampden there
have been a few much-rieeded developments of pay
ore ; but it is very difficult to size up the value of a
streaky show of this kind.
* * * *
“A.5.1t.0.”: An assay plan of the Great
1 itzroy’s Laloki (Papuan) option shows that, in the

was 43dwt.
“M.C.”: The Laloki (Papua) will prob-
ably be much gambled over this year. Great Fitzroy
has clinched the bargain, and will probably issue a
report about the big basic ore body, described as
being very similar to the one worked in Mount Lyell’s
famous open cut. It is worth noticing, however, that
Mount Lyell’s pyritic ore is now down to about
s p.c., and another pyritic mine—Many Peaks (Q.)
—has also fallen off in copper values. Laloki’s pre-
sent values are no doubt due to local enrichment. At
Mount Lyell approximately 100 tons of pyritic fluxes
are used to smelt 56 tons of silicious ore. The pro-
blem at Laloki is how to get the silica.
Mount Lyell reports 533 tons of blister
from 25,396 tons of ore, a fair return considering
that the four-weeks’ run ended on Christmas Eve
and the smelter had to make sure of a supply of ore
through the holidays. That meant running with
only two furnaces for eight days to conserve the
supply. For 12 weeks (October 2 to December 24)
78,783 tons smelted have given 1646 tons copper,
showing an average of 2.08 p.c. Taking electrolytic
copper at £7O, the average extraction works out at
295. per ton for copper and from ss. 6d. to 6s. for
gold and silver, or together (say) 355. per ton of ore.
Costs to produce fine metal don’t average more than
255. per ton of ore, and with cheaper electric power
there will be a chance of clipping something off that.
Increased smelting to the full capacity of 400,000
tons a year, producing 8000 tons of copper, would
also pull down costs. Averaging the extraction at
2 units of pure copper (a 50 to 1 ratio) a cut of 2s.
per ton means saving from £30,000 to £40,000 in
12 months.
North Mount Farrell is one of the little
Tasmania West Coast mines that plug away, and
occasionally give shareholders a taste. In October, it
paid a sixpenny div., and another (the 12th) will be
available this month. The profit for six months to
the end of October was £5488; depreciation ate
£lO2B of that, and the balance was available for
dividends and for that stand-by fund which the co.
has never neglected when things have been at all
prosperous.
* * * *
Williams Fancy, the Berringa (Vic.)
mine whose people know how to keep exes, down,
did better than usual for its shareholders last half.
Out of 6fdwt. stone, which brought in £18,279, a
mine profit of £8691 was made. In divs. £6600
was paid during the half and another £l2OO was
made available to pay shareholders’ New Year’s Day
exes. Mine and mill costs were together 11s. per
ton, 2s. lOd. per ton was put into development, and
management, contingencies and all other extras ran
to under Bd. The good prospects which make Sam
Stretch and his company joyful are beyond the big
break south at the bottom (No. 7) level, where
there is about 4 ft. of stone worth from 6dwt. to
Bdwt. ; and most of the ground above this point—-
south, that is, of the big break : —is virgin. Manager
Langsford is to tickle this part of the mine during
the half-year, and if it gives the same answer as
the No. 7 has done, shareholders won’t know them-
selves.
* * * *
Though the actual consumption of tin is
really not increasing very fast, the payable tin-
bearing areas of the earth are so limited that the
world’s output has all it can do to keep up to the
demand. In fact, if tin statistics are to be believed,
the demand has actually been ahead of the supply
during the last few years, with the result that visible
stocks have shrunk as follows: —
1909 . . . . 20,918 tons
1910 .. .. 17,194 „
1911 .. .. 16,514 „
1912 . . . . 10,997 „
And it is still more remarkable that this shrinkage
of stocks has occurred during a boom in the tin
market, which one would expect to result in a largely
increased production on the one hand and a gradually
restricted consumption on the other.
Dr. Wade, the English petroleum expert
imported by the Commonwealth Government, is at
present examining the bore at Vailala, 160 miles
N.E. of Port Moresby. He will then prepare a geo-
logical map of the district and report generally
about the petroleum industry in Papua. Meantime
he has suggested Port Romilly, about 40 miles west
of Vailala, as a suitable harbor for oil vessels instead
of Yule Island, which is 96 miles SE. The oil area
is estimated to cover 900 square miles. The stuff
so far obtained is a light oil of a paraffin base, and
it is so scant in quantity and poor in quality as to
suggest that the true reservoir of petroleum has not
yet been struck.
The Great Cobar slump in London has
been so bad that even the £lOO debentures got down
to £55; and.if that is a fair value for debentures,
how much less than nothing are shares worth? As
a matter of fact, with anything like decent manage-
ment, there is no visible reason at present why these
mortgage debentures should have shrunk below par.

Recently the spirit moved a Fijian in-
vestor to ask the London Mining Magazine why a
local banana and sugar-cane buyer had been ap-
pointed ’boss of a big copper-mining concession.
The reply he got was that, while the editor was
unable to see how experience in the banana or
sugar trade qualified anybody for the management
of a copper co., it afforded at least as good a train-
ing as the command of a regiment, the possession of
a peerage, eminence in law, or unusual skill in
cricket. As he was unable to explain the relations
between bananas and copper, he returned the 4s.
sent by the investor to cover the cost of inquiry. It
is amazing how some people drop into mining billets.
The Bulletin has known and seen mines managed
by a cobbler, barber, lawyer, bank manager, school-
master, orchardist, engine-driver, dentist, brewer, and
phrenologist, and knows of instances in the West
where mines have been reported on by a blackbirder,
cooper, drummer, sewing-machine agent, barman,
clergyman, land agent, and undertaker. The last-
named furnished the most sensible and accurate
opinion of the lot.
* * * *
“0.K.”: A recent epidemic of fires in Mel-
bourne oil and color warehouses will be turned to good
business account by the insurance companies, as a
matter of course. There is a combine in the insurance
trade, and the tariff for oil and color stores will no doubt
be raised. Private houses, built of brick, are practi-
cally safe from the fire-fiend, in a general way of
speaking. Millions of tenants pay fire-insurance pre-
miums all their lives, and get never a sixpence from
the companies. But if a miraculous epidemic of
brick dwelling-house conflagrations came to pass the
rates would go up. The combine treats every notable
loss of the moment as a reason for increasing profits
in the future. A great game is fire-insurancing! The
shares of one co. on which eighteenpence only' has
been paid out of pocket are quoted at 50s. And the
bloated capitalist who sells his £75 investment for
£2500, after having drawn fat dividends thereon for
two-score years, reckons that he is reaping the just
reward of Private Enterprise.
Though others may have idled during the
past few weeks in M.L., the various Coal Merchants’
Associations did not. Instead, they put on a spurt, with
the result that in the last two months they lifted
more coin out of the public pocket than in any
previous two months. In Christchurch they raised
Kaitangata coal from 38s. to 445., though the mine
did not cease work, and the product did not cost one
penny more to land in Christchurch. Retailers who
had large stocks on hand, and who refused to join in
the great organised steal, are now being boycotted
or threatened with boycott. Yet if you mentioned
preference to unionists to those organisations they’d
bare their fangs and snarl in two octaves.

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, January 5, 1914.
My Dear Arinx, —
Our Agricultural Show Ground palpi-
tated with tartans on New Year’s Day.
The Highland Gathering was mercifully
tempered bj r a cool breeze; but, in spite of
this dispensation of Providence, some of tae
inhabitants of heavy-weight woollen plaids
looked warm enough to sit on an ice-pack
without noticing any inclemency in the
weather. Governor Strickland was shad-
owed by A.D.C. Robins (a long, tliinnish
figure clad from head to toe in white that
made you blink, unless you had your smoked
glasses) and Help Macnaghten. The lat-
ter is a fine, strapping young man, with a
Scotchbyterian’s Sunday expression and a
stupendous forehead that fights its way out
of a small Scotch cap pertaining to the
militia. High Commissioner Reid waddled
across the ground during the afternoon, and
Dugald Thomson, little heard of in public
these times, was in the throng. The Ameri-
can boys, with their lean, tanned chief,
Piexotto, tramped on to the ground. They’re
a mixed assortment as to size ; the smallest
urchin looks young enough t > dote on candy.
The Roman chariot race was the big item
of the 1914 Highland Gathering. You’d ex-
pect it would be, of course, and recall the
good old days, when the first Roman chariot
bolted down Princes-street, Edinburgh, and
Julius Caesar fought the first kilt he met
at the border. That’s a long time ago, and
even Eittle Arthur’s History of England
forgot to mention it. Anyhow, Herminius
Cowley and Mamilius Earp drove their
ponies round the show in Roman waggons
about the size of round bath tubs, till the
spectators yelled with excitement, and the
ponies’ tails and a cloud of dust galloped
together to the winning post. Herminius
Cowley got away from his rival in the last
heat and was crowned with a Highland
Roman wreath instead of the more useful
sun-bonnet.
The American “White Sox” and “Giants”
team is not entirely composed of millionaires,
as on a hurried look at their general ap-
pearance one might suppose. There is
only one appallingly rich man, .Comisky,
in the company. He has made his pile at
baseball —began life as a baseball player,
and will continue to run baseball for all it
is worth (which is a few million dollars to
him) till he passes out. The rank and file
of the team struggles along on a wage of
£lOOO a year and all expenses paid. Why
don’t girls take up baseball instead of
crochet? Manager McGraw, they say,
draws £5OOO per annum. He doesn’t expect
to find anything better on earth to play on
than Sydney Cricket Ground, though the
Sox and Giants found it a bit fast on Satur-
day afternoon. The baseballers have char-
tered the St. Albans for their world tour,
which will cover 47,000 miles. There are
nine brides, two young children, and the
superfluous dollars already mentioned as
ballast.
R. L. Baker, better known as “Snowy,”
gave a dinner to the baseball men
on Saturday evening. The Australia’s draw-
ing-rooms were used for the purpose, and
brand-new Gaud-Mayor Richards took the
chair. “Giants” and “White Sox” purred
at the compliments their host poured over
them at the joyous meal, and then petrolled
off to the Baker Stadium.
The new Jap Consul-General for Australia
is invoiced under the name of Seizaburo
Shimizu —which has a shudder for us in its
first and last syllables. The Kumano Maru
brings this Oriental potentate to us.
When the news of the Tasman’s bad luck
in bumping up against Bramble Cay was
flashed here, most concert-goers wondered
where Nordica kept her diamonds. When
the lady is splitting the welkin with Brunn-
hilde’s battle-yell, her head usually flashes
like a lighthouse. As it turned out, the Tas-
man wasn’t going anywhere near the bottom.
But we couldn’t guess that from the first
wireless message. Hence the local chatter
over the prospective waterlogging of the Nor-
dica diamond mine.
That meek English publication, The
Gentlewoman, makes extracts from Miss
Jessie Ackermann’s book; to show 'that
the Australian husband is occasionally
Mud. “If he lives in , a town he
will keep a motor especially and
entirely for his own use, sending it to a
garage when he does not need it. He bul-
lies his pool', patient wife, who in most
cases has helped him to build his fortune,
and browbeats his ladylike daughters. A
most obnoxious habit of his is to poke about
the pantry. Taking his carriage he returns
to the grocer goods he considers unneces-
sary. He is abominably mean and close-
fisted. If a buslnnan and desiring .a holi-
dav, he takes his carriage” (observe the
carriage as the bushman’s motive power)
“and horses, and leaves them at -the little
township till his return from the city, thus
leaving his wife and daughters’’ (the sons
presumably go off with Dad in his carriage)
“without means of getting out for weeks at
a time.... How different are the English
husbands!”
So this is the Australian Benedict whom
we see at 5 p.m. every day, a beast of bur-
den, carrying in one hand a large leather
bag, and loaded to his top waistcoat button
with brown-paper parcels of fruit, cakes,
sweets and Heaven knows what other com-
missions for the wife and kiddies. This
average plain citizen husband is _ not
an angel. He may smoke a bit and drink a
bit and wipe his muddy boots inside the
hall instead of remembering the doormat.
But if he isn’t an angel it is well, for his
wife does not (as yet, of course) belong to
the Celestial Choir either. Nevertheless, he
is willing to swelter in the city for weeks
at a time while Mrs. Plain Citizen and her
brood are gathering a fine crop of freckles on
some cool hilltop.
The adoring husband in excelsis belongs as
a class to Australia and the U.S.A. He is
a queer mixture of self-effacement and doting
devotion, the husband of a local “Society”
woman. He sits in his own motor by the
kind indulgence of his wife and daughters
when they find it convenient to have him.
He pays for their amusements with prodigal
generosity, and he is the generally-useful
man who fetches and carries for the most-
spoilt woman, outside the U.S., who speaks
the English tongue.
Dr. and Mrs. R. H. Todd, after tumbling
over the face of the earth for the last six
months, at a speed that makes you giddy
when you hear the details, got safely ashore
off the Orvieto as soon as it hugged this
port. They didn’t go to Jericho, but they
did race in and out of nearly every other
well-known address you can think of. As
the versatile Todd is on the Council of the
Sydney Zoo, he hobnobbed with lions, tigers
and other notable personages in all kinds of
foreign menageries, from Nuremberg to
Cairo. Nuremberg, by the way, which is
not much past the era of the shoemaker
Hans Sachs in some of its customs, has one
of the finest and most modern Zoos in the
world. Its lions roar in leafy retreats only
separated by a fancy lake from the startled
spectators on the opposite shore. The trav-
ellers tore through Rome, Florence, Berlin,
Munich, England and France, did a
richochet to Egypt and Cyprus, and arrived
here intact. Before removing her hat, little
Mrs. Todd fixed her pince-nez firmly on her
nose, and began to look for printers’ errors
in the Woman’s Budget, which she edits
in her overtime.
Astronomer Evershed and his wife (she’s
a sister of Mrs. R. H. Todd) had time to
rest the soles of their feet in this port after
leaving the Orvieto en route for Maoriland.
The learned Evershed is from the Kodai
Ivanal Observatory in Southern India. The
Dominion wants his advice as to the best
site —at little Nelson or elsewhere —for its
proposed solar observatory. The star ex-
pert is a tall, thin man with a scholarly
droop of the shoulders, and he’d hand you a
chair in the usual absent-minded astronomi-
cal way as if he were reaching for a star
or feeling for a sun spot.
Josephine Cohan, now playing in “Officer
666,” says that Uncle Sam’s States are still
mad about ragtime. She met it over there
last year in her rush to N’York, and was
staggered to see her young son lead his
grandmother forward to dance ragtime under
the domestic roof. “Everybody’s doing it,
that’s how it is! It’s in the air, and you’re
catching it here, too! Also I noticed the
N’York girls had developed the ‘ ingenue
slump’ while I was standing up straight in
Australia. Of course, I didn’t know it was
the ‘ ingenue slump’ when I saw it last
summer —I just wondered what was the
matter with them all, and what kind of
gone-to-pieces epidemic was raging in the
town. Everybody’s doing it! So I tried it,
and brought it back to Australia in October.
Then my husband took notice. ‘You mustn’t
stand like that—why don’t you straighten
up?’ ‘Straighten up!’ (You can guess I felt
hurt.) Why, I’ve been practising the slump
all summer and —I suppose the floppy style
is getting hold of your girls too. It’s like
ragtime—everybody’s doing it.” When
Josephine, as the Heiress in “Officer 666,”
puts that brainy head of hers into a brown
velvet hat like a rolled pancake, and appears
in a jacket to match, with a skirt the color
of a flush rose in a dead faint, she is so
“cute” that you can’t remember whether
she has an ingenue slump or a spine like a
straight steel rod.
“Aladdin,” at the Adelphi, is filling the
big, cool theatre. Grace Palotta is now the
lad with the lamp, Carrie Moore being out
of the bill. She starts business in a saucy,
cherry-colored suit —the kind of garb Adam
may have yearned for when he first took to
trousers. They’re only a few inches short,
but an elegant leg gives every excuse for the
brevity. As far as one can judge from
handsome Palotta’s present form, she pos-
sesses the secret of eternal youth, and may
go on playing the part of Principal Boy for
the next half-century. Mabel Batchelor, the
Princess, looks about 12 years voung, and
tosses her sweet dramatic soprano round the
roof to loud applause. The music—mostly
by conductor Wynne Jones —is so good that
if it had been imported at enormous expense
I suppose we’d read more about it over the
breakfast egg. Acoustics are less good at
the Adelphi ; nevertheless, “The Trail of the
Donesome Pine” makes the rafters ring while
the Princess lets them have it from the
stage, and a quartet in the d.c. give their
harmonious support.
A citizens’ banquet to R. L. Baker, who
is rushing off to America with his wife in
the Ventura, occurs before this reaches you.
As an athlete and a man, “Snowy” is one
of this town’s most popular facts. Re his
forthcoming scamper, which will cover most
of the earth in about five months, he says:
“You’ll be surprised to hear that in Finland,
where it snows for about nine months in the
year, swimming is compulsory by law, yet
we haven’t got it here with a sunny sky and
the sea lapping our shores.” He is going
to pick up some more physical-culture cargo
in Finland (which he saw last in 1907-8),
and in Sweden and Denmark, and he will
even have a look at the biceps of Laplanders.
“I’m out for information all the time —in-
formation and champion boxers. .My wife
goes the whole tour —and it is going to be
pretty rapid—with me. She can type and
do shorthand, and insists on acting as my
secretary—that’s her plan. Later on , I’ll
ask her gently what salary she expects.”
A swell boxer, I understand, has to be
approached these days with a low salaam
and large bags of gold. R. L. Baker is out
to catch the French boxing Adonis, Car-
pentier, and the American duke of the ring,
“Packy” Macfarland. Carpentier, who is
a good-looking youngster of 21, is earning a
mere £BOO a week at the music-halls. When
he travels on boxing business he has a re-
tinue like a nabob, including a manager, a
valet, two trainers and a secretary. He
is supposed to be the best-dressed man prac-
tising the art of giving black eyes. Mac-
farland, Mr. Baker assures me, never moves
off his native sod without his mother, two
sisters, a trainer, and a few other necessary
comforts. As the manager who engages
either of these czars has to pay all the
travelling expenses of the friends, relatives
and vassals he is out for an expensive time.
Mrs. and Miss Clark, of the pearling
family of New Farm, Brisbane, are bustling
up to Kosciusko as I write. Mr. and Mrs.
Woodhouse, of Barraba, are packing up for
the same address.
Ex-Minister for Education Fowlds, of
Maoriland, is putting his hairbrushes and
other baggage together for a trip to Eng-
land. He comes this way to catch his boat.
Mr. Fowlds is something of consequence in
the socks and shirts business for a crust;
but the crust is so nicely buttered that he
doesn’t fag at the trade nowadays. He is
the man who went forth to preach what he
termed the “new political evangel” when he
broke away from the Ward fold. That was
the end of him.
Mr. James Craig and his daughter, Scot-
tish tourists who have been sauntering
round this hamlet, are off to Rotorua
(M.L.) this week. They are devotees of
the rod, and mean to fill a few baskets with
rainbow trout before leaving the steamy
shores of Geyserland. The Dominion Gov-
ernment, by the way, has been overhauling
the Rainbow family in those parts. It
was getting too tame and plentiful to give
any sport. This season it is fighting the
hook instead of climbing ashore after the
bait.
Mrs. Mount has just come ashore here,
after an absence of six years on the other
side. She is the mother of singer Violet ot
that name, who went to London and made
a fuss by wearing a mask or something of
the kind at her first appearances in the
Smoke. The sweet uses of advertisement
and the mysterious title “L’lncognita” gave
the Australian a lucky start.
N.S.W. and other competitors for the
billet of organist and choir-master in St.
Andrew’s Scotchbyterian Church, Brisbane,
were beaten by a Victorian named Victor
Galway. The actual wage for this much-
coveted organ-grind is only £lOO a year.
But it’s a big advt. to begin with, and
brings the winner a snug prospect of pupils
waiting on the doormat. Then come the
perquisites. If St. Andrew’s does a brisk
business in weddings, the fees for churning
out suitable music usually fall into the
pocket of the official organist. Hence the
struggle for the Brisbane billet.
Conductor Leonard Francis, of Brisbane
Liedertafel, is holidaying in this State. He
is a golfer of some prowess, and has been
vigorously smacking the little white ball at
Leura and Kensington.
Two swiftly-disappearing motors rushed
through the Bathurst and Mudgee streets
during the recent holidays. After a blame-
less career along country roads, they slowed
down at Medlow for a rest and a fresh teed
of petrol, then set the compass for Sydney.
Inside the cars were Mr. A. E. Forster, of
the bedstead manufacturing firm; young
Marich, his brother-in-law; the Tillock
Bros, and Mr. Rowle-y Bull. The beginning
of this rush was punctuated by a pause at
Goulburn, where cricketer Eric Bull was
busy on the wicket. Somewhere in the
middle and hind parts of the tour the hurried
young men found time for golf. _ .There
seems to be a good deal of energy in this
country even in mid-summer, in spite ot
Foster Phraser’s fears that we are decading.
After one or two. fill-up appointments, the
M.L. Tourist Office in Sydney is now in
charge of a permanent head. W. R. Blow
fills the chair formerly occupied by
the late E. H. Montgomery, the little man
with the many friends The new official is
a son of Maoriland’s Under-Sec. for Public
Works. He has been overlooking the soap-
ing of geysers and other departmental busi-
ness in the Rotorua Tourist Office for the
last six years, and the pumice-stone, district
loaded him with appreciation and solid goods
when he left. W. R. Blow has a forceful
chin, a mild manner, and a cargo of useful
commercial habits which he picked up in
the service of the Bank of New Zealand
before he was offered a billet in the M.E.
Civil Service. As a sideline, he has been
known to sing an evening hymn in a sweet
tenor that rivalled the chirrup of the best
boy in the choir.

remarks that one of the sturdiest votaries of
golf in that waterside village is ex-Senator

SO, and means to grow old some day.
Miss Grace Lawrance, one of the two
sisters in charge of the Glennie Memorial
School for Girls, Toowoomba (Q.), came
back from Europe in the Orvieto. She
has been holidaying in her native England
for nine or 10 months. The huge seminary
that was built as a memorial to the late
Anglican Archdeacon Glennie has a special
reputation for sport. If the Misses Hair-
bows of the academy have ever been beaten
at out-door games by other Bananaland
damsels they never mention it, even in a
whisper, to their school inarms.
A Bowral correspondent sends a line about
a recent “Bob hop” in that dressy neighbor-
hood. “Everyone invited gave a shilling
towards expenses.” (Bowral is a Society
retreat, so they do things extravagantly.)
“Every girl, in addition, took a contribution
to the supper. The secretary saw that
there was no overlapping, and was very firm
when too many offers to supply the sugar
rushed at her notebook. ... I had a thrilling
time getting to the hall, for roads are bad
and drivers careless, and my contributions
threatened to wobble down the front of my
dress. We began to dance at 8 p.m.....
Mrs. Bloomfield was official shepherdess of
the youthful flock, and kept awake for any
symptoms of ragtime. The first occurred early
in the evening, when a huge Sydney beauty-
man grabbed a small partner and bounded
up the hall. The girl reminded you of an
ant walking off with its supper. As Mark
Twain observed, an ant always get a
tight grip on forage twice its own 1-A 1 .:!.
Bowral is still rather early-Victonan. but
we ragged joyfully till midnight, and the
old-fashioned folks could only roll _ then-
eyes in awe-stricken silence... .The district
feels its pulse beat faster, however, for it s
full of Sydney visitors; the Macneill Simp-
sons, the Spencer Bruntpns and Dorothy
King, the Claud Readings, the Falks, and
crowds of city people are here. Hotels and
boarding-houses are bulging with prosperity,
and we’ll rag the Old Year out.”

Concerning the constant terror to wind and
limb caused by the motor-hog who races past
islands in the stream of street-traffic marked
“Wait here for trams,” a much-travelled
Australian tells me: “In Sydney you don t
know motoring yet—as they do in the
States.” (“Thank Heaven !” is the only suit-
able interjection just here.) “There they’ve
got ‘the speed bug.’ This insect some day
will exterminate the whole race of Murkan
millionaires, by egging them on to petrol into
Eternity. Barney Oldfield, the U.S. speed
king’—he is not a millionaire by an odd
chance —has one 120-h.p. car, but his favorite
chariot is a 240-h.p. motor. I’m told you
can’t see it as it passes. You just feel the
breeze lifting your feet off the road. That s
not a personal experience—l heard it m uNew
York. Anyhow, if a car runs you down
here, it’s because you know a
motor from a funeral in this city.”
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.
“G.V.W.”: The idea of utilising prison
labor for afforestation has a little more in
favor of it than mere sentiment, the only
thing that “Tex” places to its credit in B.
11/12/T3. There are splendid plantations
scattered throughout M.L., planted wholly
or partly by prison labor, which are a valu-
able asset.' With the growth of. the trees
and the increasing demand for timber, the
value will climb faster than compound in-
terest. That the work has been done well
the age and height of the following classes
of vegetation bear witness : Douglas fir, 14
years, 35ft.; Californian redwood, 13 years,
30ft.; Eucalyptus viminalis, 13 years, 65ft.;
and Eucalyptus siberiana, 13 years, 45ft.
The allegation that prison labor is the most
expensive is so far from the truth that “Lex”
will have to take compass-bearings to hnd
his way back. At Hanmer last year the
cost of opening tree holes with contract free
labor was 19s. per 1000; with prison labor
15s per 1000. The average number em-
ployed was 15.22 prisoners per day; and
their supervision cost £l7l for the nob. E ree
labor averaged 5.35 men per day, and the
supervision cost £9O. Each prisoner s labor
for the year was valued by the head foies oi
(not a prison official) at £74. What its
value to the State will be when the trees
reach maturity it is impossible to say. And
to the individual prisoner the value of (say)
18 months or two years at forestry, as com-
pared with a similar period at boot-tinker-
ing, for instance, will be more considerable
any way one looks at it.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, January 5, 1914.
My Dear Yarrie, —-
New Year’s Eve in Melbourne was an
almost doioious event. At 11.30 about 500
people were seated, limp and silent, on the
steps of the G.P.O. They all seemed to
belong to the chronically-depressed type, and
looked as though they were making good
resolutions for other people. Others made
tracks for late suburban trains, as if they
had repented of a rash resolution to wait
until midnight had struck. The only hilarity
I saw was provided by a straw-hatted youth
who danced a gutter tango with a joyful girl
his own size. They had a good hundred
yards of Flinders-street to themselves, and,
as they danced, she held his ridiculous,
narrow-brimmed headgear while he waved
her hat with the fashionable nodding rose
clamped at the back. A policeman was
eyeing them in a hostile manner when I
left.
The weather at Flemington on New Year’s
Day was beautiful enough to be cut out and
framed. Shoals of the Second Best people
attended the proceedings, but there was a
regrettable absence of the Pride of the
Market, including, of cohrse, the vice-regal
Denmans, who had gone to Perth for the
Christmas races. (In Australia, whenever
you see officialdom hurrying, it is making
for a race meeting.) The Frank Maddens,
however, put in an appearance, Mrs. Frank
in a well-seasoned costume. A Gentleman
Help of the Denman household made a
matinee hit with a full hand of girls; while
the beautiful Croker, beautifully booted (I
don’t mean that he was kicked) and gloved,
looked as if he had been gummed to his
clothes. The real sensation of the day was
the extensive X-ray performance of a lady
escorted by a gent, of the vintage of ’49 ; but
a secondary thrill was provided by a biscuit-
tinted vision sporting a pinny or smock, and
wearing a bouquet in the place that a
Highlander hangs a sporran.
Society (a tired typewriter insists on
rendering it “Sosuety”) has given the
charity fete racket a rest, and retired to the
coolth of Tasmania for the horSe-race and
warship season. Mrs. Earle, who is in the
habit of secretarying fashionable raids, is
taking a holiday with brother Dr. Pardy,
while Miss Michaelis, whose hobby it is to
assist unrecognised talent, has departed for
Wellington, accompanied by Mrs. Nott and
the severely classical Dorothea Spinney.
Robert Harper, whose electorate recently
faded off the map, slipped quietly out of
politics, after 34 years of them. But Kyne-
ton, Macedon and Gisborne rose up with an
address illustrated with photographs, and
presented it to him during an interval at the
Hanging Rock races, on New Year’s Day.
The veteran, in reply, said that it was a
mistake to cut his electorate to pieces. He
would have been earning that photograph
album still if Mernda hadn’t met the same
fate as the goat in front of the express.
The Bowes Kelly tribe met the New Year
on the wild, wet road to Tasmania. They
will be located at Norton Mandeville until
the holidays are over. The head of the
house is mourning the loss of elder brother
Hubert, who passed out in Sydney some
days since. The deceased left a large for-
tune and a widow, but no children.
The Faulkner widow, with daughter Mrs.
Mcßean, has left the Riverina station for
a while and is now browsing on the green
pastures of Menzies’. Grand-daughter
Mcßean made a dash straight at Ceylon
last month. Her companions in the charge
were Aunt Carse and Cousin Jack. The
tour has a time limit, as Frank Carse’s
marriage with lawyer Brett’s daughter is
listed for February. Only royal personages
can be absent from their own nuptials.
Queensland squatter Cobbold is now rest-
ing his fevered brow on a Western District
cow, having invested about a quarter of a
million of his savings in Yarram Park. Bob
Cain, who helps to set the fashion in socks
and ties in the most leisured circles, calls
Mr. Cobbold uncle when he is not studying
the fashions. If uncle is very nice he will
nrobably call a favorite pair of socks after
him.
Ethel, the daughter of the late Felix
Levien, who used to sleep in the Upper
House, has sprung a surprise on her friends
by publishing the belated news of her
marriage with one Guy Elwin Harvey. The
Melbourne edition of the Elwin Harvey
family is in the habit of playing the heroine
in yellowdramas.
Tristan Biisst has won the Henry Berthier
Scholarship, a Trinity College plum, and
he also grabbed the Old Melbournian prize
for literarv excellence. He is a Church of
England Grammar School boy. So Mamma
Biisst is just now the proudest woman in
Melbourne. Two elder sons of the family
got all their musical training in Australia,
and they are both doing notably well in
Germany. One is waving a baton over the
heads of an orchestra.
Gracie Neal, of Grace Park, Darling
Downs, who always descends on Melbourne
at Cup time with a tremendous stock of
clothes, is booked to marry Frank Miller,
son and heir of the Alex. Millers, of Gee-
long.
Tenor Dufault is now simply and beauti-
fully referred to as “Paul” by devoted musi-
cal girls. They linger yearningly over his
front name until it sounds like the mellow
note of a cow-bell heard at night.
Mummer Harcourt Beattie and his wife
send rather lugubrious New Year greetings.
The card is solemnly inscribed “Tempus
fugit” on a dark shield. As though one
needed reminding of that!
Dashing Mrs. A. J. Patrick (she was
Joan Meynell before the beautiful young
man who played King Love in “Every-
woman” made himself her husband a couple
of years back) has a walking-on part in
Lewis Waller’s “Beaucaire.” The lady has
only to look handsome, and she can do that
without trying.
One of Melbourne’s beauty girls, who,
however, is not really and truly in Sassiety,
recently spent a bx-ief fortnight in Sydney,
and has been pursued homewards by an
intimation that she must hold herself ready
to co-respond when called upon —unless her
alleged partner in indiscretion can manage
to keep the case out of court.
Another Hobart girl has attached a naval
man. Leila Gibson, of the great Tasmanian
sheep clan, is the girl, and Lieutenant J. N.
Howie, of H.M.S. Sealark, is the lucky
sailor.
Some local girls lost their chauffeur lately
while motoring in Tasmania. He wasn’t
carried off by Death, as you might suppose,
but by the police, who wanted him worse
than the girls or Death did.
Vivian Parker is to take the racing yacht
Killara round to Sydney, and act as skipper
during the February water-flights, the owner
of the craft being unable to spare the time
to take the tiller himself. Viv. Parker afloat
reminds one of old times, especially since
his one-time brother-in-law, Charlie Wallace,
is once more takinsr an interest in the sport.
Mrs. Viv., a dashing, Titian-haired beauty,
and the elder sister of Ruby Wallace, has
been flatting in New York, but will return to
Australia in time to see the boats up-anchor.
Hair dyeing is becoming a fearsome busi-
ness in Paris. Some of the smartest re-
turned voyagers declare they have not yet
the moral courage to do worse than color
their locks a fiery red ; but the Parisian ex-
tremists are defying Nature and coloring
their natural thatches weird blues and
greens. The color idiocy is exhaustive to all
bar the ash-blonde ; any darker tint requires
preliminary bleaching. To be really chic
you dye your hair, your furs and your
poodle all the same color, so that the passer-
by can see at a glance to what degraded
dog you and your furs belong. How any-
body can say that woman is not the intel-
lectual equal of man —!
The Palais de Danse is the essence of
stodginess—much to the disappointment of
the suburban saints, who had expected to
gaze on Wickedness from the safe elevation
of the Esplanade. The facade of the place
is glittering (in accordance with the oldest
saintly traditions of Vice), but the hall be-
yond, which is open to St. Kilda’s observing
eye, merely shows solemnly-revolving
couples who seem oppressed by the weight
of the moral responsibility they are bearing.
A crowd of watchers collects on the Espla-
nade above; they pay nothing, but, like all
deadheads, they criticise keenly. A fashion-
able ballroom, especially after supper, would
provide just what the most moral gazers are
looking for. In the Palais, no wealthy
squatter-johnnie ever approaches a richly-
gilded heiress with the polite query, “This
is our little tummy-rub, I think?” as hap-
pened audibly in one of the Very Best ball-
rooms the other week.
A packed congregation attended at the
Savoy on New Year’s Eve. A special ser-
vice had been arranged for the occasion, in-
cluding the release of a number of pigeons
with trinkets attached. Every woman who
was able to catch a pigeon (those they had
brought with them were disqualified) won
its more or less precious cargo. An in-
spiring moment was when tlxe Most Ro-
mantic Actor at present in Melbourne, moved
by the pathetic little gasps and starts of
his leading lady, who was cut to the heart
by the fiutterings of the scared birds, rose
to his feet and addressed the assembly in
shocked tones, exclaiming: “Gentlemen,
haven’t we had enough of this? It isn’t
sport!” Females in his vicinity at once
felt a throb of sympathy for the pigeons;
but the manly protest was scarcely audible
above the chatter and the screeching, so the
picturesquely indignant mummer had to re-
sume his seat without a single “hand.”
Mixed bathing, under conditions which
even Mrs. Grundy would wink at, is going
strong at Kenny’s Ladies’ Baths, St. Kilda,
every week-day from 6 to 9 a.m. On the
Sabbath morn the sexes are at liberty to
mingle in the sea for an hour longer—six
to 10. Mr. “Bill” Kenny, who manages the
baths, and personally supervises these “Con-
tinental” gatherings, has long been keen on
the idea of an enclosed mix, but it was not
until last summer that he got some of the
Very Best people to come along in family
parties and make the desired splash. Now
that he has got the clientele, he wants a
larger hath area. St. Ivilda is little else
than boarding-houses these times, and every
boarding-house could send forth its own
little _ mixed contingent on a balmy early
morning. Already the toniest caravanserais
are supplying the new and reprobated
“Palais”, with bunches of two-steppers on
balmy nights, and the mixed waltz is intro-
ductory to the mixed wash.
It is a severe wrench for women dog-
fanciers to leave for a foreign sprint.
Quarantine restrictions and merciful anti-
dog laws that sailors keep handy make the
life of the person who takes her dog with
her hardly worth living. So the yelping
darlings are nearly always left at home.
Mrs. Keep and daughter Heather, whose
hearts beat responsive to every bark, left
last week for England, after solemnly in-
ducting their friends into the charge of their
various treasures. The temporary keepers
are having an anxious time. A baby can
always be anchored safely in the house; but
a treasure that has four good legs to run
away on, and is perpetually threatened with
fleas, is a really serious proposition.
A crateful of panting Christmas ducks
had been dumped from a country train on
Toorak station. The birds were waiting
open-beaked for death, when a hurried but
humane porter pushed them under a water-
tap that was dripping briskly. At once the
crate became a mass of fluttering wings and
delighted quacks. Hot railway passengers
who witnessed the little act of charity mur-
mured their appreciation, and a fat man.
who looked as if he always overate himself
on principle, puffed along and stirred them
up with his walking stick, so they would
all get a share of the water and arrive in
better condition at the table.
A very grand female is taking an interest
in a forthcoming charity spree. Another
grand female wants to be interested, but
won’t if the first stupendous lady is in-
volved. The organisers of the charity are
perspiring freely over the problem in social
strategy, for though both of them want to
work for charity, neither of them is charit-
able enough to work with the other. Don’t
some people make you tired !
Flying has taken its proper place in the
columns of the fashionable journals. The
writers thereof describe the latest designs
in flying dresses, mention who flew, and
where, and, finally, record the fact that
Mrs. Altitude or Miss Flighty has gone to
Cannes, or the dogs or elsewhere, to recover
from her recent flying accident. Appen-
dicitis is not in it now with a fall from the
blue. . 1 foresee, too, that the girl who has
been indiscreet will not now be referred to
as “one of the fallen.”
The young woman who allows her dusty
fiance or otherwise to bounce her round the
country in a side-car had better persuade
him to fix an extra lamp on the wheeled
armchair. The sputtering couples are very
numerous on the military road that curves
round the bay, and they go hopping along
at night with just one light on the cycle side.
Even the conscientious driver (I understand
there are a few of them about), observing
the one light, may just allow road-space for
an unaccompanied “road flea”- —the Euro-
nean nickname for the hooping travellers.
The girl who sits perilously on the
seat behind the driver is much safer (in a
traffic sense) than the lady of the unlighted
armchair.
The Arts and Crafts Club is to have a
whole gallery of the Exhibition barn at the
A. outbreak this year, instead of being
shoved into the usual corner. The vote and
interest of a vice-regal dame are certainly a
great help.
“Rosna’s” notes from London:—
Ruth Bannister, the artistic Sydney-side girl, has
had a royal command, or, rather, her wood-carving
has. Her panels of waratah and grapes, which have
been on view at the Lyceum Club, are to pay a visit
to Buckingham Palace, where Queen Mary will be
pleased to cast her royal eye over them.
Several talented young Maorilanders are among the
would-be mummers toiling at Tree’s Academy of
Dramatic Art. One is Marjorie Butler, a Wellington
girl whose father was a Judge of the Maori courts.
Another is Shayle Gardner, a lengthy youth who is a
nephew of the Bishop Boyd Carpenters. Probably,
however, the Church and the Stage are not yet
sufficiently allied to make the connection very useful
in this city of much wirepulling.
Millais’s “Diana Vernon,” Edward Stott’s “Re-
turning Home” and Sargent’s “Autumn Leaves” are
the latest notable purchases for the Melbourne Gal-
lery. The Adelaide Gallery scores with “Sowing the
Seed,” a fine picture by William Orpen, secured at a
cost of £7OO. Margaret Macpherson, the Adelaide
artist, was commissioned by the S.A. Government to
look at this for the City of Culchaw. The bronze
puma, bv Swan, recently secured by this same damsel
and Will Ashton, is said to be honestly worth several
hundreds more than the price paid.
Lillah McCarthy, who is Mrs. Granville Barker,
inherits a useful little pile through the death of her
father, Jonadab McCarthy, F.R.A.S., who was an
authority on the geology of the Cotswolds. The fair
Lillah’s share will, it is said, amount to something
like £50,000, and this should indirectly be an aid
to the repertory movement. With a useful wife like
that, Granville Barker should be able to play what he
likes, no matter what the silly old public wants.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Sturt statue fund is increasing so slowly that
I expect to he quite dead before the memorial is
erected. One donation outstands like a bright night-
light. J. H. Symon has given £25, and he will make
it £5O if ten £5 contributors can be found.
Miss Mary Gavin Gardner is engaged to Dr. J. E.
MeGlasham. He is a B.Sc. and an M. 8., and she a
B. Do you wonder at Foster Fraser’s eulogy of the
Adelaidean intellect ?
Governor Allardyce, of the Falkland Islands, stayed
the night at the Max Andersons, and dined with the
Bosanquets at Marble Hill, before joining the English
tnailship. He has governed the Falklands since 1904.
In addition, he is not only a cricketer but a jour-
nalist.
The Ways are at Sea View, their farm near Noarlunga.
He is convalescing in the guarded way that is essen-
tial at his time of life.
Everybody—meaning most of the people with money
—is away, which has been bad luck for Anderson’s
theatrical co. and Paul Dufault and other showmen.
Most of our ancient noblesse are at Victor Harbor.
The 11. S. and P. M. Newlands, for example, one or
two of the Stirling clan, and Mrs. Dudley Hayward
and her two sons. On New Year’s Day the Governor,
unflinchingly resolute as ever, attended the local
regatta, Captain Fletcher aiding him. Mr. Dutton,
jun., stood him lunch, and the head of the family—
he of the R.Y.S. —gave the vice-regal party the use
of his yacht. So, altogether, lI.E. did the thing in
moderate comfort.
Commemoration Day was celebrated by a hot
luncheon in an absolutely boiling Town Hail ; and a
tepid luncheon (nothing was really cold that sizzling
day) for the women pioneers downstairs. The
Mayoress looked after her guests in the intervals of
mopping her fevered brow, assisted by Lady Bosan-
quet and her daughters, the Butler dame, Mrs. Rounse-
vcll, Mrs. F. W. Ralph, Mrs. S. J. Jacobs, Miss Law
Smith and Mrs. Fowler Stewart; all of whom did a
good deal of brow-mopping themselves.
There was an impressive and expensive crowd at
the second day of the Port Adelaide Christmas meet-
ing. In the two days over £36,000 went through
the Tote. Puringa broke his leg at the starting-post,
and had to be destroyed. A number of other things
—lunches, teas, violent disappointments, flirtations,
gown exhibits, and so forth—also transpired, but the
above-mentioned episodes were the most important.
The new stipendiary stewards got busy promptly
at Morphettville on New Year’s Day. Half a dozen
jockeys were fined for being late, and owners were
warned solemnly that in future disreputable colors
and ruined gear in general would not be passed. This
to those racing-men who for years have clung to
mouldy old caps and jackets, rusty stirrups and a
strip of sacking for a saddle-cloth was a bitter, bitter
blow. Under the new regime Private Enterprise is
to be interfered with seemingly to the extent of even
being debarred from the luxury of dirtiness. The rac-
ing was good, and the St. Leonards Handicap yielded
a £123 dividend, Robona being the responsible agent.
Airmen have no luck at flying in Adelaide. At the
first attempt, several years ago, the imported machine
was burnt before it could make an ascent. The
second model swerved in rising, cut off a boy’s hand
in the pressing crowd and gave up the game. Last
week A. W. Jones got fairly going, and made two
gate-money ascents from Cheltenham racecourse, with
other successful spins on his own account—once with
a lady passenger. Then one morning he flew right
over Adelaide, and kept on for five miles, lost his
way in returning, ran out of petrol, and had to do
a grand volplane. A swift descent is very like a fall,
and the last 80ft. of it was so much of the real thing
that the man was knocked out for an hour or two,
and the machine for a week or two. But plucky
Jones says it was sheer bad luck, and he means to be
up again shortly.
The lady who flew with Jones, as before-mentioned,
is the daughter of the licensee of the Gresham Hotel.
Yours affectionately.
JOHANNA.

“Kuscobin”: It’s not every man knows when he’s
on a really good thing—quality calls to quality just
as deep calls to deep. But there are some good
things no man can miss—and one of them is Helidon
Spa. Just you watch the connoisseur call for his
drink next time you’re in a high-class bar. No doubt
about him —he says right off “Whisky and Helidon.”
Sometimes he 6ays this or that brand of whisky—
but he’ll say “Helidon” every time. The wine drinker,
too—hear him call foy “Claret and Helidon” these
hot days—he knows that just asking for Spa may
mean anything down to plain Sodawater. That’s
the tip—and it’s well worth remembering. Always
split it with “Helidon” and you’ll give a sparkle, a
snap to your favorite liquor that brings out the best
that is in it. So healthy too —Helidon Spa is the
only true Australian Mineral water that has genuine
Osmotic qualities—therefore now and at all times
“Cornstalk”: It does an Australian good to travel,
if only to find out how good Australia is. I’ve
lately been about Germany and the Continent of
Europe a deal, and used always be meeting folk
going to “take the waters.” There’s no doubt these
Mineral Springs in Europe do a lot of good, but I
was surprised to come across a statement in the
Lancet some time ago definitely stating that our
own Helidon Spa was the finest mineral water in the
world. I tell you this was a startler to me, but
since I’ve been back I’ve tried it and quite agree
with the Lancet. The best whisky and the best
wine both taste better still if you split them
with Helidon. A doctor chap explained to me
that the reason of that was that Helidon had a high
percentage of Lithium—it mellows the whisky and
prevents that next-morning head by eliminating any
noxious ingredients from the system. So it does too
—you take my tip and always
Split it with

Tooth is mighty and shall prevail! Bella
Singh, a Hindoo, caught his hand between two cog-
wheels and damaged it badly. He was carted to a
pub and left there till the doctor was brought. Saw-
bones tied up the hand and then decided that Singh
could do with a bath, so he ordered one. But Bella
wouldn’t be washed by anyone who wasn’t a Hindoo,
and as there wasn’t another Hindoo handy he re-
mained dirty. Then the doctor ordered a feed; but
the Hindoo struck again, because the tucker wasn’t
cooked by one of his faith. So he starved for four
days. Then a sympathetic stranger took pity and
offered him a drink. Instantly there was a change.
Singh didn’t wait to find out whether the man who
THE OBLIGING PARENT.
“I told your father I couldn't live without
you."
“ What did he say?"
“He offered to pay my funeral expenses."
was shouting was Hindoo or Esquimo. He just had
the beer and said nothing. Perhaps there is a pro-
viso in the Book of Caste Rules and Regulations
excepting the real necessaries of life from all restric-
