“Eddyson”: This is the story of the
simplest woman I never knew. Her name was
Agnes (pronounced “Hagoness”) and she was the
lawful wife of John Henry Spotswood, of Gin’s
Hill (Vic.). Men tire easily of simple women like
Hagoness, especially when they have the pale,
pathetic, obtruding eyes of a hapless nanny, and
wear fawn freckles. John Henry tired of his wife,
and after about 10 years determined upon his course
of action. The recent death of Lemmon had opened
a way of escape for him. He put several small
articles in a gin case, and, driving his grey horse
Geordie, and leading his cattle pup, he went openly
over the hill to the habitation of the widow Emily
Lemmon, which henceforth was his postal address.
Mrs. Spotswood made no outcry. She seemed a
trifle dubious about her rights for a time, but on
the fifth day after Spotswood’s change of residence
she went across to Mrs. Lemmon’s in her old sun-
bonnet, and put her head in at Mrs. Lemmon’s kit-
chen door. Spotswood and the widow were at mid-
day dinner. “Will you gimme me husband, Mrs.
Lemmon ?” said Mrs. Spotswood. On second thought
she added “please.” “I will not,” said the widow.
Mrs. Spotswood lingered for a few moments, as
though half-inclined to think something remained to
be said; then she turned and went home. That
night there was a tap at Mrs. Lemmon’s window,
and Mrs. Spotswood’s voice said: “Gimme me hus-
band, will you ?” Mrs. Lemmon made no repty, and
20 minutes later a voice at the keyhole said:
“Gimme me husband!” After that, whenever Mrs.
Spotswood had 10 minutes to spare, she went over
the hill to widow Lemmon’s, and asked at a window,
or a keyhole, or any handy crack, for the return of
her wayward husband. Her request was wholly dis-
passionate ; it was more like the appeal of a house-
wife requesting the return of a borrowed stewpan
than the heartfelt plea of a grass-widowed woman
for restitution of valuable conjugal property. This
course of conduct was continued for five months ;
meanwhile Hagoness managed the poultry farm and
the bees, and made no complaint among her neigh-
bors. But, finding that several hundred respectful
requests breathed about the domicile of Mrs. Lemmon
had produced no results, Mrs. Spotswood went into
the Springs, seeking legal advice. She knew she
had been deprived of property and rights that were
hers in the eye of the law, and she put her case
somewhat ambiguously to constable Tom Elphin-
ston. “If somebody takes somethin’ what’s mine,
what do I do?”,asked Hagoness. The constable ex-
plained the usual procedure in such a case, and in
due time the suit of Agnes Spotswood against Emily
Lemmon, widow, came on for hearing. Neither
party Was represented by counsel, and Mrs. Spots-
wood was immediately non-suited. She had pro-
ceeded against Emily for petty larceny. Having
exhausted the law, Agnes fell back upon her own
methods. She continued to pay calls on the widow
Lemmon, and to put her reasonable request to that
lady through the cracks or at the bedroom window.
Now and again she wrote letters couched in inoffen-
sive language, calling upon Mrs. Lemmon to deliver
up John Henry Spotswood by return, but .these, too,
were without effect on the wicked widow of Gin's
Hill. For two months “Jumbo” Tutt, a fruit-
grower up the creek, had been down with a serious
illness, and Mrs. Spotswood had been nursing him.
“Jumbo” arose a very grateful man. Mrs. Spots-
wood refused to take his money, and Tutt swore
a booming oath, and said that if there was anything
he could do for Mrs. Spotswood, short of laying down
his life, he would do it. “I’d like me husband
persuaded to come back,” said Hagoness. This hap-
pened at a quarter past four in the afternoon. At
twenty minutes to six “Jumbo” Tutt drove up to
Mrs. Spotswood’s door. He had Mr. Spotswood in
the dray. John Henry was a pitiful spectacle; he
had two black eyes that resembled over-ripe figs, his
left ear was like a toy balloon, his nose was a pal-
pable misfit, and he was so utterly used up after a
long and horrible fight that “Jumbo” had to carry
him from the dray to the colonial sofa in Mrs. Spots-
wood’s front room. “He’s come back to you, ma’am,
free and hearty,” said “Jumbo,” and Hagoness, kneel-
ing by John Henry, gazing through her tears at his
blasted head, murmured joyfully: “I knew he on’y
needed a little persuadin’, bless ’im!”
“II.S.”: In Malekula (New Hebrides
group) feasting time comes round with the ripening
of the yams. The feasts are given by men aspiring
to chieftainship, of which there are four grades—the
bara, gulgul, mulun and mara. All below the rank
of bara are commoners, whilst the mara is the high-
est dignity to which the heathen aspires, and very
few reach it. The entrance to all of these ranks is
by the giving of feasts, at which enormous numbers
of pigs are slaughtered. The tucker must be of the
male gender, with long tusks, and in order that the
tusks may not be injured the boars are tied with
about six inches of rope to a tree stump for years.
The tusks curl round and re-enter the jaw and pro-
trude on the other side, to curl round again. The
suffering of these sacred pigs is terrible. Ordinary
teeth are knocked out to give play for the big tusk,
and a tusk that has pierced the jawbone is especially
prized. The real god of the Malekulans is the pig.
Two or three atone for all crimes against property
or person, ten purchase him a wife, a hundred tuskers
slaughtered at intervals raise him to the rank of mara,
while his happiness in the next world depends on
the number sacrificed at his funeral. Recently, an
old chief, finding his end approaching, and anxious
that his funeral should be duly honored with the
highest ceremonial, sold off his seven wives in order
to raise a. further 70 pigs to add to the sacrificial
herd. There is also some slight idea of a blood
atonement. If a man has committed a crime, or
two tribes have been at war and desire to bury the
hatchet, pigs are brought to the public square, where
friendship is renewed by the chief killing one with
a spear, saying, as the blood flows, “It is peace.”
It is, as far as the pig is concerned, anyway.

“Gip”: Are foxes snake exterminators?
Some bushmen round here (Monaro) say they are.
Shearers and “down country” men (everything’s
“down” from here) “take their colonial” that where
foxes are thick snakes grow beautifully less. An
old grey-beard assured me that he “got” two foxes
with a strychnine-powdered snake last summer when
the raiders had grown too cunning to consider the
commonly-used suet.

“Lagani-Namo”: The R.C. Mission head-
quarters at Yule Island (Papua) is a complete little
cosmopolitan colony. The priests and lay-brother
tradesmen hail from all parts of Europe, and the
settlement is easily the most progressive in Papua,
When all Port Moresby’s food came out of tins,
Yule was eating fresh beef, bread and vegetables.
(The beef was preserved under dripping, and kept
indefinitely.) Among its other adjuncts were a
printing press, a telephone and a still. I’ve heard
Scotsmen declare that Yule’s barley-bree was the
.......
veritable balm-of-Gilead. One of its best virtues
was that it was gratis to whoever had time to call
in passing. One day Lieut.-Governor Wm. Macgregor
happened to do the calling, and the guileless bro-
ther-in-command invited him to criticise their
spirits. “ ’Tis beyond criticism,” said Mac Willie.
But he had to remember, as head governing official,
that it was contrary to the law, and when the
Merrie England departed the still went with her.
“Fisho” : Was out at Warrnambool (Vic.)
the other day, looking for something to eat, when a
commotion on the sandy bank of the river attracted my
attention. Went along to reconnoitre, and found a nice
mullet, still alive and kicking, but high and dry
about a foot from the water. He had lost his tail,
and plainly the accident was very recent. Within a
yard of him was the cause of the commotion —a big
barracouta, who in the excitement of the chase had
neglected to take sound-
ings, and was conse-
quently stranded also. I
seized the opportunity—
or rather both of the op-
portunities. The mullet
was evidently an aider-
man in the fish world,
judging by his girth
amidships ; but the ’couta
was the longest and lean-
est of his kind I had ever
seen, although I have put
in many hours in the
’couta boats catching
“axe-handles,” as the fish-
ermen call them on ac-
count of their length,
breadth and curves.
Strange to say, although
sharks, stingrays and
every other marine ani-
mal short of sperm
whales are frequently
seen and caught in this
river, the ’couta is a very rare visitor, some of the
oldest fishermen declaring that they have never seen
one inside the bar.
“Gip”: Met a cannibal tom cat last week
and on the day of introduction he ate our fafnily
of seven kittens. My attention was first attracted
by the mother’s growls and hisses as she stood out-
side the nursery in the garden, fierce as a tigress.
The strange tom was just disappearing with a kitten
in his mouth. I went inside, and again the mother
cried out in distress. I rushed to the rescue in time
to see Gabbett disappearing with a second
kitten. While I watched, the brute kept off, though
I sometimes caught sight of his greedy yellow eyes
glaring from the long grass. By nightfall the bewil-
dered mother was wandering round the garden paths
with her one remaining kitten in her mouth. I
did not actually see any eating or killing done, but
next morning found the body of the seventh kitten
minus head and shoulders. And nobody can con-
vince me that the little thing chewed off its own
head.

THE CREEK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twixt high banks on either side
Dark and slow its waters glide;
Sombre, down its course it creeps,
Secret with unlitten deeps.
Ancient trees in serried ranks
Halt their armies at its banks;
Drawn along the neutral space
Stand they ever, face to face.
And, while those old soldiers stand,
Fronted down each narrow strand,
From their source, in mountains high,
Endless march the waters by :
As when through the parted throng
Pageants gravely wind along,
Bearing in procession past
Emblems of their creed or caste.
Where it rounds some shaded bight,
Deep its pools, and dark as night;
There the sun in vain essays
Entry to its guarded ways.
There it is the fish-folk glide
Shadowy in its sluggish tide,
Lurking into stony rifts
Where the under-current drifts.
Down its livelier fluxes sail
Vagrant leaves and petals pale,
Flotsam from the tangled glooms,
Smitten buds and ravished blooms.
Dark and slow its waters move,
Down its deep and ancient groove ;
Drawn for ever towards the sea
Through a dim futurity.
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

TRIED IT ON THE HORSE.
“When driving from Tamworth to Bowling Alley
Point one of my horses took bad with gripes, and
got down kicking and struggling,” says Mr. Sam
L. Pyrke, of Bowling Alley Point, N.S.W. “Those
who passed me considered the horse would die. As
I always carry Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and
Diarrhoea Remedy for my own use in case of emer-
gency, I put a whole bottle of it in half a pint of
water and gave it to the horse. In less than a
quarter of an hour he was up and started to feed.
It is Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea
Remedy that I have to thank for saving me a good
horse.”*

“Xox” : Much information has been dis-
bursed concerning the homing instincts of cats,
horses and prodigal sons, but apparently no-one has
noticed that it is very pronounced also in reptiles.
Drinkwater and I drew a £lO prize in Tatts., and
vve considered the occasion sufficiently momentous to
require celebration. Aided and abetted by divers
bottles, we celebrated for two days. Then Nemesis
arrived. Blue snakes, green snakes, spotted snakes
with red lights on their tails, and vari-colored snakes
poked the tongue of scorn at us, gambolled before us
contemptuously, and threw back-somersaults at us
with an abandon that nearly drove us mad. At last
we could stand it no longer. We tore out of the
hut and raced through the serub to the railway
station like the kangaroo on the postage stamp flee-
ing before the wrath of Agar Wynne. The snakes
were in close attendance. We clambered into an
empty truck and pulled a tarpaulin over us, but the
shakes got in under it and taunted uS with dur
helplessness. Then the truck was picked up and wd
were carried 106 miles before a disrespectful porter
pulled us out and insulted us. The snakes were still
with usy but after a sleep in the park we awoke to
find them gone. Elated, we returned to the station
and jumped the rattler" back to our siding, accom-
panied by four bottles of rum. The station-master
helpfid us along the road, and when we reached our
lint there Werfe' all i he ktiafeeg Waiting for us. Thev
hald outpaced th£ ifditi:

and span have arrived, aiid the oid-timers are moving
On and out. It is regrettable that, with these will dlis
dppear a wonderful stoVe Of Island lore and history.

have written!

“Geebung’’: I wonder how many Abo-
readers have seen the mistletoe bird, the little chap
that spreads the mistletoe from tree to tree. He is
something like a robin, but red-headed, and very
shy. His residence is unlike that of any other bird
I have ever seen, being made of cobwebs, woven
together like a piece of silk and formed into a bag
more like an old-fashioned watch-pocket than any-
thing else, This is fastened over a slender limb of
a tree, and has the ehtrance at one side. And you
ought to-see the quantities of the fruit of the mistle-
toe the proprietor can put away at a sitting! No
wonder the pest spreads.
* * * r *
“Gouger”: Working alongside of me in an
open cut not long ago was a man we called “the
Mad Swede.” He would swing his heavy pick with
almost maniacal energy, bringing down showers of
earth that suggested a miniature landslide. Now
and again he’d rest on his pick, glare
at the “face,” then burst into long
peals of laughter. We forgave him,
because he did more work than the four
of us put together. “What’s he doing
to you now, Switzer?” I asked, when
one day he laughed louder and longer
than usual. He looked at me and said
quietly: “She hold out her arms to me,
like before she died. My little girl!
But ze doctor; ze cursed doctor, he
always hold her back! Oh, my little
girl, I shall reach you yet!” With one
of his awful laughs the Swede turned
again to the “face,” and the loose earth
avalanched from his pick. We found
out afterwards that the death of his
baby daughter years before had turned
his brain. The wet season was on us,
and the big, open-cut became dangerous
to work. Masses of earth and rock,
loosened by the heavy rains, would slip
from the granite bottom with a crash.
One night there was a terrific thunder-
storm, and next day, just before noon,
there was an ominous crunching, fol-
lowed immediately by a warning cry.
We all sprang back except the Swede.
He stood there, madder than usual that
morning, working frenziedlv under the
crumbling bank. We shouted, and
shouted again. The roots of a big tree
creaked, as the weight of earth slid
from high over the bank. Then it
came, a roar, a moving wall of earth, of
bounding rocks and dragging treees. The Swede
vanished, as a fly would under a boot. Though it
took us only 20 minutes to dig him out, he was
dead. But if he’d got a fair deal he had found his
little daughter again.

Jobber : N. or M.” and his Tasmanian
pal “Maurice Weston” (B. 20/11/’l3) remind me of
old Jacky Dow, on the Murrumbidgee. Jacky never
refused a man work, and set chaps at some queer
jobs He had one opening and closing a gate all day ;
another bailing out a fair-sized waterhole with a
pannikin. He put one cove wheeling stones in a
barrow from one paddock to another, and then put
him to wheel them back again. A Yankee struck
the station once, and the old man set him turning a
grindstone. After dinner, the boss went to see how
the job was going, and found his nibs in a ham-
mock reading and working the stone by an arrange-
ment he’d fixed up with an old bicycle. Jacky fired
him on the spot.
O.B.”: Bill and I had the night of our
lives when we struck Melbourne. When the last
pub door banged we started for nowhere in particu-
lar and finished up on the steps of Parliament
House. I was sleeping soundly when two fire
engines, with sparks pouring from the funnels,
dashed madly down from Eastern Hill on their way
to a call. But Bill saw, through drink-sodden eyes,
these monsters come and vanish with a rattle into
the blackness of the night. The first I knew of the
proceedings was the fool kicking me madly and
shouting, “Gorstruth, Jim, wake up ! Mishd sight of
a lifetime! Gorblime! They’re moving Hell; I jest
seen two cartloads go past.”
“Wilder”: If I was the judge I wo.uld say
that Jobson won the Maddest Hatter Stakes. He
had a mania for telephones. Just outside his stringy-
bark humpy a kerosene tin with the top cut out was
nailed through one side to the top of a stump. Dang-
ling from the open end was the receiver, which con-
sisted of an old hollow saucepan-handle. A string
proceeding from the tin led in a circular direction ;
it was about 300 yards long, and was fastened at in-
tervals to trees. It ultimately ended in another kero-
sene tin on top of another post. Jobson’s hut was
about a hundred yards from the creek ; it was built
on a flat that had been tipped on its end, which
meant that Jobson had to yakker hard to haul up his
armful of firewood or his cans of water. If the
’phone “rang” during his ascent, he would drop his
load and scramble like blazes up the incline to his
end, grab the saucepan-handle and yell: “Hullo!
Who’s there? This is Ben Jobson speaking.” Then
dropping the receiver he would streak to the other
end and shout, “That’s you, is it, Jobson? This is
Thompson speaking. Mr. Hooley says if yer like to
bring yer rabbit skins ter town, he’ll give yer eighteen
pence a pound for ’em.” Jobson would then career
back to his end and scream: “Tell Hooley ter keep
his blarsted eighteen-pence, d’yer ’ear! I kin set
nineteen and a ’arf from old Billy, the ’ide-buyer
and he calls fer > em ’ imse lf.” Jobson would baM
repeat this pantomime three or four times during the
process of hauling his load up the hill. Occasionally
if nearly pumped out by his previous exertions and
he received a call just as he was about to pick up his
load again, he would tell the ’phone to go to Heaven
and wait till he came up. He might still have been
sending his messages, but one night an angel ap-
peared to him and told him to shoot his three neigh-
bors for poisoning his drinking water; and the
hta removed S ** fuWl the contr act, had
“Silas S.”: Parkinson was a hero, and I
can prove it. He was going home from Coyes, full
of whisky and fine feelings. His track led him
along the bank of the river. At Rule’s landing he
saw It. It was a man in a black suit, wearing an
old-fashioned belltopper hat, floating face down in
the dark waters. Parkinson waited for nothing but
to tear off his pink tie and one shirt cuff before hurl-
ing himself into the river. He grappled with the
drowned or drowning man, and towed him to the
river’s bank; then, having hauled himself and the
waterlogged stranger on to the bank, he dashed
wildly to the east and yelled “Help!” at the lower-
ing bush, after which he dashed wildly to the west,
and yelled “Help!” at the heedless hills. Parkin-
son’s third dash brought him back to the spot re-
cently occupied by the human jetsam. The figure
was gone from the shore, and out in the stream
floated a man in black, wearing a quaint, superfluous
Paris hat. Parkinson tore off his other cuff, hurled
his collar far from him, and dived into the river.
He rescued the perishing once more, dragged him
higher up the bank, rolled a large rock partly on to
him as a tether or stay, and after dashing blindly in
three directions and screaming “Help!” to the un-
heeding waste, headed for Rule’s house. In 15 minutes
he was back, running well ahead of the whole Rule
family. Parkinson dashed to the spot where he had
left the alleged deceased, and halted suddenly. De-
ceased had shifted again. There was no body on the
shore, but far out in the moonlit river floated a
semi-submerged Wesleyan in black garments. “Blowed
if he ain’t gone an’ done it again!” wailed Parkinson.
Parkinson tore off one boot, - flung his cap at the
moon, and took an heroic header into the tide. For
the third time he rescued the sombre citizen, but
this time he landed him on the wrong side of the
river, and while the whole Rule family, numbering
15, was getting out the boat, Parkinson was shout-
irg to the heavens and the hills for help, and holding
the would-be-suicide tight to ten a firma. The Rule
family rushed to his assistance, and found Parkinson
working like a machine, applying the rules for the
restoration of the apparently drowned to the well-
made, grass-stuffed scarecrow from their own orchard.
The apparently eccentric conduct of the scarecrow
was due to the wanton frivolity of a band of young
fishermen camped in the scrub near by. I know for
a fact that Parkinson did not receive the Royal
Humane Society’s medal. The Royal Humane
Society takes no account of good intentions.

“Tekelo”: Mrs. Urumangi. of the Solomon
Islands nobility, visited me the other day. Her eight-
moon-old daughter suckled at her breast. Between
draughts the star-eyed infant sucked its mother’s pipe
(loaded with strong Emu twist) in placid content.
Everything was quite ordinary; nobody was sick,
and, thank God, nobody AVowsed.
* * *
“Ess” : On swampy land, or on any flat
country after a spell of rain, the bullfrog band per-
forms melodiously day and night, but is heard to
much better advantage at night. The singers have
a great variety of notes, and seem to follow one
another as though some musical genius had arranged
the symphony. The result (when you are not listen-
ing for horsebells) is pleasant enough. Now and
again they croak together, uttering one rousing blast,
which is followed by a slight pause; then each one
takes up his place again in the corroboree, the deep
bass of the old bullfrog being particularly notice-
able. Nor is the concert confined to one spot; it
is heard all over the flats where water is lying, a
thousand voices muffled by distance mingling softly
with the* strong clear notes of the near performers.
THE ENTHUSIAST.
PASSENGER (to pilot): “Look at that sea!
What a dreadful risk ships take , don't
they!”
If you look in the pools and gilghi-holes vou will
notice huge patches of white froth, and here and
there little discs of white eggs floating in a jelly-like
mass. These are the frogs’ nests. In warm weather
the eggs are soon hatched, and the water becomes
alive with darting and wriggling tadpoles. In this
stage the creatures are more fish than frogs ; but in
a few days they sprout legs, and the tail disappears.
If food is scarce and they are confined in a small
space, they make dinner of one another’s tails. When
the pools are drying up the frog takes in a cargo of
water, and buries himself in the mud, which cakes
over him. There he may remain for many months ;
but as soon as sufficient rain comes to loosen his
prison bars, he leaps forth to wet his whistle and
give it lip.
THE SUNDAY BEER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
M'Canister was going home
At ten o clock on Saturday.
His noble brow was marked with loam,
His air was bibulously gay.
One bottle ’neath his dexter arm
He bore his Sabbath pangs to charm.
M'Canister was shocking drunk
(His Saturdays were often gay).
On nearing home he stumbled plunk
On Higginson, who drove a dray.
A barrel perched upon the car
Held pecks of corporation tar.
Poor Mac, in-toed and bleary-eyed,
Gazed sadly on the mighty keg.
“Oh, you’re the lucky dog,” he sighed.
“Say, howjah do it, mate, I beg?
Here’s me, to damp me Sunday throttle,
Must cart it in a measly bottle!”
VICTORIA.

The Employment of Prison Labor.
“Notbell” for the defence: —
“Lex’' (B. 11/12/’l3) arraigns the Holman Gov-
ernment’s scheme of afforestation by prison labor;
and I am for the accused. It is freely admitted that
N.S.W., like the rest of Australia, needs tree-plant-
ing very badly. Also, it is extremely improbable
that any Government, whether Lib. or Lab., would
devote large sums to such a project, which
could show no return in money for 50 years or so.
Therefore, it seems a reasonable proposition to em-
ploy the cheap prison labor available for this pur-
pose, more especially as there is a very decided and
very natural objection to prisoners competing with
free labor in ordinary industrial enterprises.
“Lex” says the scheme is devised to give N.S.W.
prisoners as good a time as possible, and he is
troubled because the plan may somewhat ameliorate
the hard conditions of their punishment. But it may
be that its promoters have broader and more humane
views. It is an echo of the bad old system to say
that once the evildoer is in the clutches of the Law
he should pay the penalty in blood and tears. Mod-
ern methods aim at reformation first. If the trans-
gressor is to be of any use to the community after
his sentence is over, surely we should try to make a
better man of him while he is in gaol, instead of
damning him, body and soul, as is the custom where
ignorance, stupidity or brutality holds the reins..
But “Lex” has another objection to tree-planting:
“The gathering together, for months and years at a
time, of a number of unmarried men, or men whose
wives are necessarily separated from them, is a- si 7 ;®"
able enough menace to morality in itself.” But will
the menace be less if these men, marrkd or un-
married, are undergoing “separate treatment,” cooped
up alone in little stone cells and shut off from
fresh air and sunshine? “Lex” approves of “separate
treatment,” but does solitary confinement necessarily
improve the morals of sinful man ? Seventy years
ago Charles Dickens said of it: “I am convinced
that there is a depth of terrible endurance in it which
none but the sufferers themselves can fathom, and
which no man has a right to inflict on his fellow
creature. I hold this slow and daily tampering with
the mysteries of the human brain to be immeasur-
ably worse than any torture of body; and because
its ghastly signs and tokens are not so palpable to
the eye and sense of touch as scars upon the flesh,
because its wounds are not upon the surface, and it
extorts few cries that human ears can hear, I the
more denounce it.” A healthy, outdoor life, com-
bined with association under sensible supervision,
seems to be, “Lex” notwithstanding, a much more
promising method of moral and physical reform
than the sedentary gaol occupations, and the torture
of separate treatment which “Lex” advocates.
“Lex” further asserts that “the existing prison sys-
tem of N.S.W. is admittedly one of the best, if not
the best, in the world.” Who says that? Who admits
it? And even if it is good, can we learn nothing?
Have we, and we alone, reached the height of per-
fection ?
Says “Lex”: “They [the prisoners] learn useful
trades; the regular hours and exercise and the
absence of beer set them up in health.” Surely their
health would not be likely to be any the worse if
they lived in a tree-planting camp away on the
mountain side! Does that sound any less salubrious
than the aioomy confines of Darlinghurst or Parra-
matta? And does the prisoner in those horror-
haunted institutions ever learn a useful trade? “Fully
20 trades are taught in the gaols,” says “Lex.”
According to the latest Prisons report they keep
blacksmiths', painters’, tinsmiths’, carpenters’ and
other shops going for the necessary work of the
prison, and there are other shops, as mat-makers’,
tailors’, saddlers’ and bootmakers’, where work is
done tor the gaols and other Government depart-
ments. But it is more than doubtful whether the
prisoner learns enough of any trade to enable him
to earn a crust on his release. By far the greatest
number of prisoner-tradesmen are employed at tailor-
ing and bootmaking; but is it reasonable to expect
that a “tailor” who has been making rough prison
shirts or trousers for years, or a bootmaker who
has learned how to fashion a pair °* ol “® sy
shoes, could compete on equal terms with the skilled
tradesmen of the outside factories? In the report
referred to, the Director of Prison Labor observes.
“One of the great difficulties which a prison-taught
tradesman has to contend with, if he endeavors to
continue his trade outside, is that he is a slow work-
man compared with the free tradesman. So much
for the present system of “teaching prisoners trades.
The tree-planting is bound to be done badly, UEX
thinks, because “it is no game for the mexpen-
enced, being really a branch of agriculture. It is
more difficult, then, to teach a prisoner how to plant
a tree than it is to teach him the bookbinding or
bootmaking that “Lex” prefers! And from another
point of view, surely a knowledge of agriculture,
however slight, is a more useful asset to the dis-
charged prisoner in Australia than the ability to
patch a battered hall-mat or crudely half-sole a pair
° f Finally, tlie afforestation scheme “has been a
failure, economically and otherwise, in Maonland,
says “Lex." Well, it has stood for 15 years; and
“Lex" may remember that the persistence of a
scheme for 15 years—in the case of the N.b.W.
prison system—was accepted as evidence, not oi
failure, but of success.
The appalling Vic. honorarium is saved
from his own dunderheadedness at times.
The other day several Brunswick (Vic.)
fruiterers were proved to have sold cherries
which were described as “simply rotten,
“decaved into pulp” and “emitting an awful
smell,” whereupon three amateur beaks mur-
mured “Fined 10s.” But the prosecuting
inspector pointed out that the minimum pen-
alty was £5, and the shocked trio had to
impose it. People who know the law are a
great trial to the amateur beak.
“Bobstay”: The International Life Sav-
ing Conference is to recommend to the
nations represented that foreign-going steam-
ships should be required to carry life-boat
equipment for only 75 per cent, of the
people on board, the remaining 25 per cent,
to be provided for by life-rafts, of the re-
versible pontoon pattern. ’Twas ever thus.
After the first gasp of horror at the Titanic
disaster the Board of Trade hurriedly de-
cided that life-boats to carry all on board,
or the complement as per schedule, must
be provided. Now that the great tragedy
is no longer a nine days’ horror the good
intentions may go to the usual place. There
is by no means a unanimous opinion as to
the efficacy of the life-raft. True, the con-
trivance can’t sink, and it is more likely to
be launched in time of trouble without
smashing into matchwood than a life-boat,
and once afloat it is more roomy; but it has
the great disadvantage that it can move only
at the will of the wind and waves. It is
expected, of course, that the boats will tow
the rafts, but this is not infrequently im-
practicable. Whatever is finally decided
upon Australia must accept, and, that being
so, the best of representation should have
been provided. As it was, Captain Collins,
of the High Commissioner’s office, was the
Commonwealth’s delegate ; and he is a mili-
tary officer who knows all about the sea
from the smoking-room of a mail-boat.
“Sapphire”: The Australian Naval Col-
lege, at Geelong, which selects its youngsters
before they are 14, undertakes to finish oil
their general education, besides giving them
a technical training for their chosen profes-
sion. Is it an oversight that in the history
course no mention of Australia appears.
Some reputedly well-informed people appear
to believe that this land does possess a his-
tory, pocket-edition certainly, but fairly in-
teresting, and for us more important than
any other literature in the world. In fact,
the youngsters in the public primary and
secondary schools have to “swot’ at it, and
it is included in the Arts course at the Uni.
But the young naval officer of the future is
to remain ignorant of the story of the land
that has trained him, employs him, and is
supposed to inspire him; and how he can be
expected to successfully lead men of Aus-
tralia into action in such circumstances is
one of the things I don’t understand.
From a much-travelled “Battler” :
Australia has held disillusionment out to me with
both hands. 1 read of your fine manly democratic
spirit, yet never in any white mans country have.l
met so much crawling and toadying. X hit a job m
a little country store, with an Australian for my mate.
1-Ie told me to drop calling the man who paid me
my wages “Mister,*’ as he said it sounded too like
deference, but I noticed that every time the boss
came into the shop this stalwart flew all round
like a fly in a bottle to create an impression that he
liked work. Another disappointment is with regard
to the Army. Wherever else I have been the young
fellows took a pride in their soldiering, and when 1
saw the first line of Australia’s defence slouching
along like a flock of sheep, with a worried-looking
officer walking behind with a face expressing a stand-
ing apology, I gasped. The Bulletin s remarks
about the possibility of Jap invasion and the need of
protection come nearer the cold, hard truth than
the majority of untravelled Australians seem to
imagine, and such a mob would just be sport for
our yellow friends. I’ve seen the Jap and now Ive
seen the Australian; and at present all my money
would go on to the former if it came to a scrap,
with no navy or seasoned troops to help. A Jap
or an Indian can be as good a soldier as a pampeied
white, and it’s up to our side to shape better and take
the business far more seriously than it is doing so
far. I’ve served in several regiments and I know
a bit about the game, so this is not all arm-chair
twaddle, as my instructor’s certificates could prove.
Anyhow, I’m going to do my modest duty to the
country I live in now, and offer myself as a volun-
teer officer, if they will take me. It’s no use
merely grumbling; even your “Alligator’ Johnson
couldn’t kill an Asiatic invader with a speech.
“McNorth”; Now that both Poles have
been visited and any doubts as to their
whereabouts laid at rest, and it has been
definitely proved that they shift 3ft. in 75,684
years (figures quoted from memory) and that
the adjacent country is even more desolate
than the Federal Capital site and will never
be worth tuppence as a tourist resort or any-
thing else, isn’t Polar exploration about due
for the count-out? Yet news comes of fur-
ther proposed expenditure of life and money
in alleged exploration. What for? The
ends of the Earth are unquestionably
the most barren and valueless places in
existence. They have no mining or agri-
cultural future; the man who would
sink through 50ft. of ice before he
struck the ground would simply fritter
away his time ; while the cocky who rose in
the morning to find an iceberg on top of his
pumpkin patch would at once talk wildly
of taking his capital out of the country. He
would also have labor troubles, for the hired
man would refuse to work from sunrise to
sunset in a country where the day lasted
six months. Why not give the useless ends
of the Earth a spell and have a go at the
middle’ Australia and Papua have any
amount of country which offers a greater
return for exploration— country of which
we know very little, more than we do o the
Poles, yet much of it immensely fruitful be
yond a doubt. To get the human and other
treasure spent on the ends diverted to the
middle we must knock the halo oft the i olai
explorer; we must, the Poles having heen
duly found, take the men who henceforth
want to star as Polar heroes and put
them in their proper place among those use-
less freaks who bang pianos for 97 hours
or twirl Indian clubs for 17 days llsec. on
end. Then the foolish game will tail down
dead.
From London: —
How would you relish going to sea with a Captain
Sinker! It is the name of the commander of the
Southern Cross, the steamer that carts the Anglican
Bishop of Melanesia from one group of Pacific islands
to another. In spite of his depressing name, the
Captain has been making a breezy speech at a mis-
sionary meeting in London, starting off by saying
that, though he had been twice shipwrecked, once
frizzled in a burning vessel, twice ice-bound and .once
captured by head-hunters, he never was so fright-
ened as he was at the moment when called upon
to make his first speech to John Bull and his
missus.
“Pontius Potter’s” contribution to the
Tasmanian-corpse symposium:
This talk of Hobart tombstones recalls a man who
does not grow a slab on his grave. Wainewright
(you can spell it without the “e” if you like), poet,
painter and expert poisoner, is buried in the convict
section of the Trinity burying ground in Park-street.
After a term at Port Arthur he spent his latter end
as an assigned servant at the Hobart General Hospi-
tal. The resident surgeon, Dr. Bedford, had a small
studio fitted up, and employed Wainewright to make
sketches of interesting operations, etc. _ Fate played
into the poisoner’s hands once by admitting to the
hospital an official from Port Arthur. , He was in
a dying state, and the attendant was instructed to
make the passing-out as comfortable as possible.
Wainewright recognised his old enemy, and the
dying man’s last hours were just about as nice as a
devil with an artistic temperament could make them.
Wainewright has been woven into a good deal of
fiction. Dickens made a short story out of him
which brought the author £lOOO from an American
paper. On the whole, the money was well worth the
story.
“Green Tape” on a gamble:—
W. H. Whiddon’s appointment as N.S.W. Income
Tax Commissioner came as a surprise packet to the
tipsters, who thought they could pick it in two. A
few months ago Whiddon was third in the Depart-
ment. Then Cantry, the secretary, was taken seri-
ously ill, and Whiddon was made acting-secretary.
Next Spiller paid the last tax, and thus Whiddon
suddenly finds himself head of the Department. He
joined as a temporary clerk in 1895, and was made
a permanent hand no more than eight years ago.
His rapid rise has been helped by the vagaries of
successive Governments in changing the incidence
and method of tax-gathering, with consequent ups
and downs in the Department’s importance. But
though these changes favored Whiddon by clearing
out the men in front of him, his last promotion
came as a surprise; it was actually made before his
second-last promotion was gazetted.

A British combine, representing a capital of
£35,000,000, and headed by Lever Brothers, has been
formed with the object of manufacturing soap in
China. — Cable.
The Yankee Meat Octopus, having laid
its plans in Australia, is now sizing-up
Maoriland. Its agent is at present in the
Ricketty Isles surveying the prospect on
behalf of Armours, who are one of the
Trust’s big tentacles.
Joe Cook, speaking from the dolce far
niente of recess, remarked that the only
way to make the position of affairs in the
Federal Parliament satisfactory is to appeal
to the country. Well?
Joe Cook, prostrating himself before the
almighty bagpipes at the Sydney Highland
gathering on New Year’s Day, generously
gave Scotland, all the credit for showing the
early Joe Cook how to build up his char-
acter and become a better Joe Cook than
ever. And Scotland, gazing on the com-
pleted Joe Cook, cheered him to the echo or
.thereabouts. Plow these national festivals
do bring out the latent modesty in people!
Carr and Catts, two N. S. Wales Labor
members in the Federal Parliament, put up
the record for least attendances last session
—24 and 26 respectively. When these
figures are compared with Bruce Smith’s 65,
it is not to be wondered at that Tory Joe
Cook’s majority of one was sufficient. Had
every Laborite attended 90 per cent, of the
sittings—surely not an impossibility—Cook’s
slender majority would have been worried
into its yawning grave long ago.
The most cogent defence speech made on
the breaking-up day of the Fed. House was
bv Higgs, who hates defence. Higgs
was saying that the Boers could shoot any
head that peeped out from a rock. Piggot,
of Calare, interjected: “They did not shoot
you!” A few more “words” followed ; then
Pliggs, imploringly, to the chairman, “Will
you _ protect me, sir?” There comes always
a time, too, when the country which for
some reason or other (often because it has
too many Higgses) can’t defend itself, ap-
peals to the nearest kind gentleman: “Will
you protect me, sir?” And the kind gentle-
man generally does it by making a meal of
the defenceless suppliant.
The N.S.W. second ballots recalled one
phase of the old electoral system. Prior to
the Act of 1892 the elections used to start
in Sydney and straggle through the country
for three weeks or a month. Whichever
party won in Sydney had a pretty good
chance of getting good wins in the country.
Moreover, candidates who lost their seats in
Sydney could fight for a fresh lease of life
in the backblocks. Thus Parkes and Robert-
son, when beaten in Sydney, both got in for
country seats. O’Sullivan, beaten in South
Sydney, went straight to Queanbeyan and
was triumphantly returned for the first time.
Parkes declared that “the door of his political
life was closed for ever” when East Sydney
would not have him ; but a back door in the
country was promptly opened, and he
slipped in quietly on his stockinged feet.
“F.H.”: The newest Victorian Ministry
is taking as few chances as possible in con-
nection with the by-elections on the 15th
inst. Prahran is one of the two metro-
politan seats to be contested. At the last
general election, Prahran had three polling-
booths —one at South Yarra, handy to the
northern and more prosperous portion of
the electorate; one at Prahran town hall, in
the middle of the Prahran division; the
third at Windsor, where there is a packed
area of wage-earners. Windsor was the
only booth which showed a majority for
Labor. This time there is to be no booth
at Windsor, and the fagged toiler will have
to walk a long step to the town hall to re-
cord his vote. Liberalism, of course, will
see to it that the Windsor Liberals don’t
have to do any walking.
“Hair Space,” from alongside a Victorian
ditch:—
Lots of farmers in the northern irrigation area of
Victoria are being converted into good Unificationists
by the divided control of the Murray waters. The
Watt Government, in pursuance of its irrigation
policy, is anxious to construct a weir at Torrumberry,
which would render possible the irrigation of a large
and promising area; but South Aus. objects. The
latter State is anxious in its turn to push on with
the Queen Victoria works on its part of the river;
but Willie Watt, by way of retaliation for the
South Australian embargo on his weir, announces
his repugnance to the South Australian improvements.
The net result is that both matters are hung up.
and the public in both States suffers. Exactly what
would happen if Willie went ahead and built his weir
and disregarded South Australia’s frenzied protests,
no one seems to know ; but the prospect of the Watt
Government doing anything definite is not hopeful.
Meanwhile many good Liberal cockies are beginning
to ask why the Commonwealth doesn’t take over the
river; and if the whole sacred Constitution had to
go into the melting-pot to allow it to be done, they
would cheerfully lend a hand to put it there.
“Patty” Glynn goes off on behalf of the
Commonwealth to talk with Sir Edward
Grey about the New Hebrides. Penny
? stamps should do nearly all this business;
: otherwise, as an intermediary, McMahon
Glynn is the best man at the disposal of the
Cook party. New Hebrides may become a
big question; to the minds of many they
must become strategic islands. Australia is
therefore right in getting a foot in while
the door is ajar. Glynn doesn’t know every-
thing about what Australia wants, but,
taking him all round, he is an Australian
of good stamp. He has been in these parts
since 1880 and a politician off and on since
3887. His merit in debate is that he tries
to understand his subject before he gets up
to discuss it. He was born in the potato
blight ”f nearly 60 years ago, in the har-
rowed province of Munster, and still carries
with hi. i a brogue which fully supports the
name of Patrick McMahon Glynn on his
visiting card.
Hugh Mahon’s return unopposed for
Labor’s Westralian pocket borough, Ival-
goorlie, was partly an accident, but mostly
the result of his opponents’ tactics before
the selection ballot. Mahon is not person-
ally popular in the State, else Gregory had
not beaten him at last election. But the
friends of his opponents (Green, ex-State
member, and Union secretary Glance) so
bludgeoned one another that ordinary union
men were lost in bewilderment and ugly
thoughts; and in their bewilderment they
dropped both local men, and chose the cold
outsider.

THE MECHANICAL TOY.
Members of the Royal family kept strictly to the religious spirit of the day. The Queen’s gifts in-
cluded new dresses for the servants. The King’s gifts included mechanical toys for his younger children.
Cable.
The Port of London Authority lately
called for tenders for lock-gates, bridges and
other ironmongery for the Albert and Royal
Albert docks. German and British firms
competed, and the Protectionist foreigners
underbid their Preetrade rivals by £170,000,
or 26 per cent., and, of course, got the con-
tracts. Meantime, some people reckon that
Germany is building battleships to conquer
Britain. Maybe. But it looks superfluous.
As soon as the constitutionally-nasty Joe
Cook had got safely into recess he said that
his Government had come through “without
any of those adventitious aids by which cer-
tain people elsewhere in the Commonwealth
have kept themselves afloat —I mean we
have had no Willis to fall back upon.”
Which was quite true; but they had a John-
son, who fired Josiah Thomas out of the
House for making a courteous statement
that the Opposition had lost confidence in
Johnson; and they also had a Cook who,
while himself throwing insults at Johnson,
was so bent on protecting Johnson from in-
sults that he had McGrath fired out for tell-
ing his constituents what he thought of
Johnson. Let the Government thank its
stars that it didn’t have the “adventitious
aid of a Willis.” For its purposes a Cook
and a Johnson are far safer and a lot more
useful.
Lloyd George in a sort of Christmas
carol: —
He despised success unless a real effort was to be
made to reduce the overwhelming extravagance and
expenditure on armaments. If a saving were effected
it would be possible to sweep away the tea, sugar,
coffee and cocoa taxes....
As the day of a Labor Ministry at West-
minster draws nearer, the greater becomes
the necessity for Australia to look entirely
to itself for defence. While the Liberals or
Conservatives are in power, they may be
relied upon to make an effort, should Aus-
tralia be attacked, to save their invest-
ments, no matter what further hardships
might fall upon the shoulders of the
struggling masses in Britain. But when
Labor gets a clear majority in the House
of Commons, its first concern —and duty—
will be to see that the working-man and his
wife and his children are properly paid,
housed, clothed, fed and entertained. When
all that has been properly attended to, it
may look round to see what has become of
“the Empire.”
Labor-man George Jones, beaten for the
Inverell seat in the recent N.S.W. elections
was one of the victims of State Frights.’
Ihe State members, distracted by the atti-
tude of Holman, McGowen and Beeby, when
called upon to support the first referendum
came more or less slowly—save a few all-
wool Federalists, like George Black. George
Jones didn’t come at all; so the big-Aus-
tralian A.W.U. men waited for him in his
district. They didn’t support the Liberal •
they simply killed George Jones by inertia’
Those bushmen, though they may not talk
much, have long memories.
Though it has so
badly neglected the
development of out-
ports, the N.S.W.
Labor Government has
at least broken
through in one place.
The old Sydney bri-
gade would never lis-
ten to border railway
propositions that
might feed Melbourne,
preferring to leave the
N.S.W. farmer with-
out transport and
markets; but the
Holman and Watt
Governments have
agreed on the exten-
sion of Victorian lines
into N. S. Wales at
Euston, Wentworth
and Moama, and of a
N. S. Wales line from
Nimitybelle through
Bombala and Delegate
into GippsJand. This
is all excellent as far
as it goes; though it
is a shrieking absurd-
ity that the traffic of
Gippsland should be
dragged past Twofold
Bay and Jervis Bay
to Sydney.
During last Westralian Parliamentary
session, the landlords’ House rejected no
less than 16 of the Bills passed by the
people’s. House. The Opposition leader
chortles over this and says it points to the
immoderation of Scaddan’s demands. What
it really does point to is the dreadful fact
that, in an allegedly democratic country,
with over 152,000 electors, less than 10,000
'propertied, electors return a majority of the
members of a Second Chamber which has
the power to veto the measures passed by
the popular Assembly.
Fitzpatrick, the clattering tinpot of the
Liberal band in N. S. Wales Assembly,
started his noise in the new Parliament by
complaining that Treasury officials had the
effrontery to pay him £34 by cheque on
December 23 (his salary as M.L.A. from
December 6to December 31). His grievance
was, not that he had been paid in advance,
but that the Treasury hadn’t paid the
money into his bank. (Bank unnamed.)
The luck is to the impudent these days.
The person who had the real grievance was
the taxpayer, who witnessed Fitzpatrick
grabbing (in advance) £34 for practically
one day’s work, knowing that the bird
wasn’t to be recalled to its perch until Par-
liament reassembled at some vague date in
the uncertain future.
A Melb. Age writer, talking vaguely of
the “gang of schemers” who are alleged to be
doing the dirtiest things on the Victorian
turf with absolute impunity, says:—
The bookmakers know the gang, and hate it like
a plague. Many of them are deeply in debt to
members of the gang. The bigger racing stables
dread the gang, but are often obliged to “work in”
with it in order to avoid disaster. On the other
hand, many small stables are compelled to propitiate
the schemers in order to live. .. .In a former day
the public used to be exploited by the Ring. To-day
the bookmaker and the public are both being ex-
ploited by these schemers.
This is putting the case from an angry
Ikey point of view. The bookmakers seem-
ingly live as expensively as ever; hence all
the means to carry on the business of being
“exploited” by the gang must come out of
the pockets of the backers, who lose, lose,
lose. So the public is more outrageously
victimised than ever, and there is more
reason than ever why the Tote should be
legalised. No gang can exploit the Tote.
THE G.-G. AND THE GEE-GEE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Governor-General and Lady Denman arrived
at Fremantle. The G.-G. said he regretted his stay
would be brief. Having seen racing in other parts
of Australia he thought he would like to see the
p erth Cup run. —Perth telegram.
It was a Governor-General,
His lady and his seneschal,
His footman and his admiral,
The lot their country’s pride ;
Who shall contemn,
Flout or make mock of them ?
Who shall deride?
Ai am the Go-vernah, who is the true Imperial link,
And through my glass his Majestay unto these States
doth drink.
Ai rivet true the happay chains that bind Australia’s
bones
Unto the British Umpiah when Ai lay foundation
stones.
Ai make as nothing Taime and Spaice—Ai bridge
the leagues of sea
By shaking hawnds with toaday tradesmen at my
taired lev6e.
Ai make the people think King George the Figure-
head Heaven-sent
When Ai go with my outriders to open Parliament.
Ai take the chay-ar at dinners to the Socialistic
fleet
Bought by these Labah fellahs, and Ai sit with
them at meat.
And Ai tell how England’s gloray is her navay, brave
and gay,
And Ai recapitulate the statements of Trafawlgar’s
Bay.
Ai baind six sovereign States in indissoluble bonds,
And Ai oft repeat myself, like a chain of Lachlan
ponds.
Ai’ve the kingly ways of horse and hound—Ai trip
to loyal towns,
To gahther with my subjects on the Heath and
Epsom Downs.
Ai’ve the true Imperial spirit, so Ai baind with
Taime and Plaice,
And govern all my subjects in our glorious Pride of
Race.
The pride of race at Caulfield, and the Sydney
A.J.C. ;
Onkaparinga, Melbourne Cup, and ’cross the Tasman
Sea.
Ai prove Imperial fitness for all “Colonies” on earth
By triumphs out at Flemington, and Bowen Park,
and Perth.
It was a Governor-General,
His lady and his seneschal,
His footman and his admiral,
Who sailed from there to here.
For going round from Cup to Cup,
And cheering when the number’s up,
And bleating when men dine or sup,
He takes ten thousand pounds a year.
VICTORIA.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

BAD HEADACHES.
ALMOST IMMEDIATE RELIEF OB-
TAINED BY TAKING DR. MORSE’S
INDIAN ROOT PILLS. CROW’S NEST
LADY’S PRAISE.
“I took Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills in
Melbourne, on the recommendation of a
friend, for the relief of habitual constipa-
tion,” writes Mrs. Lillie Pearce, of Lane
Cove Road, Crow’s Nest, North Sydney, “and
found them do much good, almost immedi-
ately relieving me from the dreadful head-

NOTIONS
Artesian, in winning the Perth Cup a
second time, belittles the form of the best Westralian
racers. Artesian’s first capture of the Perth Cup was
in December, 1911. Next year he came to Melbourne
for the big Spring meetings, and was somewhat
fancied for Melb. Cup with only Bst. up. He finished
twelfth. Apparently the Westralian crack was no
stayer in such company. Last week he carried 9st.
71b., and won the two-mile race in 3min. 33 l-ssec.
* * * *
“0.K.”; A more or less indirect result
of the Blackpool case was the resignation last week
of one of the V.R.C. stipendiary stewards. Mr. S.
Griffiths, the “stipe” in question, found his job
“uncongenial and unsatisfactory,” he says, quite
apart from the protracted unpleasantness incidental
to the disqualification of Blackpool a year ago. It
was unlucky for the “stipes” that they started their
professional career by making a bloomer in the case
of a gee whose owner could afford to fight and fight
for further inquiry. Also it was more unlucky for
the owner of Blackpool that the “stipes” were then
on their trial, so to speak, and the V.R.C. rulers
naturally did not want the new tribunal to have its
first decision upset. Everything happened for the
worst. Owing to the Blackpool mistake, and the
pertinacity of Frank Meyers in forcing the V.R.C.
committee to practically admit the error, the early
zeal of the poor “stipes” has become paralysed.
Having caused a lot of trouble by doing one wrong
at the first meeting they attended, the unlucky trio
seemed afraid to do the right thing at subsequent
meetings. So they did simply nothing to speak of,
and called it “uncongenial” work.
“Hermes”: The resignation of V.R.C.
stipendiary-steward Griffiths has caused a hullabaloo.
Griffiths says he found his position “uncongenial and
unsatisfactory.” Why? For a long time the atmo-
sphere of Vic. racing has been filled with rumors.
The largest and most persistent is to the effect that
there is “a gang” which exploits bookmakers, horse-
owners and public. In the old days the Ring ex-
ploited the public; now a “gang” is said to exploit
the Ring. This “gang” is alleged to have so many
jockeys in its grip that it can practically fix the
result of any race. It is even said that many big
bookmakers are heavily in debt to the members of
the mysterious combination. It is also alleged to
have got control of the judgment of sundry handi-
canr>ers. When a market has been made, and the
public steered on to certain gee-gees, its agents
quietly back the certainty, and when the numbers
are up Joseph O’Cohen and the gang cut up the
money collected from the public. It is a tall storv,
but there is something in it, though whether it had
anything to do with Griffiths’s retirement is quite
another matter.

Punters at Flemington received a New
Year’s warning against the error of their ways. The
two first-favorites that got home were not very widely
backed, whilst the winners of the two principal events
Standish Plate and Bagot Handicap—were rank
outsiders. Captain White, generally regarded as a
has-been, reasserted himself by winning the Standish
flutter in fair time, with 9st. 51b. up; and St. Vano,
lightly weighted, led the field all the way in the
Bagot.
Three sons of Flavus were victorious on
New Year’s Day. Alured took the valuable Tatt.’s
Club Cup at Randwiek, and Captain White and Re-
caller won races at Flemington.

The second day of Tatt.’s Meeting at
Randwiek (Sydney) was a more pleasant outing for
punters than the first, as in four of the six races the
first or second favorites got home, causing Michael
McNoses to wear a disappointed expression as he made
for his buzz-cart. In the Novice Handicap the public
swag went on Sound Asleep (what a name!) at 6 to
4 on. In the Flying Welter First Principle was prime
favorite at 9 to 4 against; but there was a fair
demand for Maltchester at sixes, and he got home
by a head. The New Year’s Gift proved to be a
correctly-named event, as Popinjay (7 to 2 against)
was well loaded with the gold of Demos, as were also
Gigandra and Border King (9 to 2 against). Golden
Hop had a good following at sixes. Popinjay just
managed to beat Golden Hop by a head. The big
event of the day, Tatt.’s Cup, made glorious with
1500 added sovereigns, rather redeemed McNoses’s
sinking fortunes, as the public made Coradil favorite
at threes, and took Limelight as a saver at fours.
Those who backed Alured, the winner, were lucky
enough to get eights. Still the public fancy was not
far out, for Coradil ran into second place, with Lime-
light third. The Nursery Handicap also ended in
favor of the bag. Mountain Knight, the winner, going
out at sixes, while Indiscretion starred at 5 to 2
against, and the second favorite, Secret Service, which
was also second finisher, got away at 9 to 2 against.
But the punter came back with a biff in the Welter,
and recovered some of his own by backing Crosska
at 6 to 4 against. Crosska landed the boodle by half
a length.
The Tote did things again at Adelaide on
New Year’s Day. In the Maiden Plate Bob Soult,
second home, paid £6 9s. In the New Year’s Gift
Master Maelgwyn, the one that arrived behind the
winner, was worth £5 Bs. In the St. Leonards
Handicap Robona handed £123 7s. to backers, and
the second was worth £2 165.; and in the final, the
Marino Handicap, First Shot, the winner, paid £l4,
and the second, Lontar, £4. Once again, has any-
one ever known Judas Skinnem lay odds like these
on placed geegees?
S.A. Jockey Club’s stipendiary stewards
(Hogan, Tom Wigley and Clem Hill) got to work
for the first time on New Year’s Day at Morphett-
ville. They swept up all kinds of things demanding
criticism, from late scratching or late starting to
looking back in a race, the careless tying of a ban-
dage and torn jockey’s “colors.” Hawker’s Tadanga
only just won the weight-for-age; her old rival,
Puringa, had been shot the same day, after an injury
received in racing the weA before.
The sporting event of the week in Sydney
was the visit of the American baseballers. First of
all the Chicago White Sox hurled itself upon a
N.S.W. team and stamped it flat by 10 to one.
Probably it would have been worse than that if some
Americans had not played with the local team. Still,
Australia made a better show at baseball than
Yankeeland did at football, or even cricket. But
the event of the day was the match between the
White Sox of Chicago and the New York Giants.
It was a terrific struggle, and when AVhite Sox entered
on the last phase the side required one to tie and
two to win. It got the two and won by five to four.
On Monday it repeated the operation, and rubbed it
in, the Giants being overthrown by 10 to five. The
American play is indeed wonderful. These fellows
probably know nothing else, but at baseball they are
past masters. Their work absolutely glitters, and is
the apotheosis of professionalism. Reg Baker enter-
tained the teams at a dinner at the Hotel Australia
afterwards, and then drove the men to the Stadium
in motors, while The Maeinsplosh entertained the
ladies, by deputy, at the Tivoli. It was a great
night.

The N.S.W. Swimming Championships at
Domain Baths on Saturday provided an excit-
mg finish in the 100yds., although the Half-mile was
a regular walk-over. In the 100yds. Longworth, the
holder, found himself up against Barry and five other
devils almost like unto himself. A fearful struggle
took place, at the sight of which the crowd went
mad especially when, at the finish, Barry, Long-
worth and Solomons literally hurled themselves in a
bunch at the tape. But Barry hurled with the most
ioice, and left the other two behind, dead-heaters for
second place. In the 880yds. Barry did not com-
pete, neither did Solomons. Longworth went to the
front, and kept going further and further in that
direction till he had won by 20 yds.
* * * *
This season’s Sheffield Shield turmoil has
a delightful halo of evenness encircling it. N.S.W.,
Vic. and S. Aus. have jumped on each other once,
with N.S.W. in slightly better position on averages—
thanks to its walloping of Vic. with an innings to
spare. South Aus., however, is bringing Clem Hill
and W. J. Whitty into the fighting line for both
matches at Adelaide—an effort intended, probably, to
frighten visitors when they get on the Wheatfielders’
patch.
South Aus. earned all the glory surround-
ing its defeat of N.S.W. Early in the game it
looked an odds-on chance for Ma. The Croweaters
were 26 behind on the first innings, and left N.S.W.
only 265 to get at the second attempt. But the
splendid fielding of South Aus., backed by the
bowling of Crawford (5 for 71), crumpled the
N.S.W. team right up. The effort was one of the
finest put up by South Aus. for a long time, and
showed that Clem Hill is far from indispensable to
his State. The team is almost one of colts, and
proves that the Wheat State (which, by the way,
doesn’t now produce half as much wheat as N.S.W.)
has plenty of gopd young blood waiting for an oppor-
tunity to justify itself. Mayne’s only veterans were him-
self and Crawford, while Trumper controlled a first-
class team which included four other “players”
besides himself (Massie, Bardsley, Macartney' and
Kelleway), who could not well be left out of an
Australian XI. Yet the colts outplayed the veterans.

If the slump keeps up, the N.S.W. Cricket
Association will have to look round for a vaudeville
troupe to help things out. The match against
South Aus. returned barely £5OO, or about £250
less than last year’s fixture; and the two-days’ outing
with Maoriland was very poorly patronised. In the
midst of this decay the country associations are
yelling for financial support; and the Association
is beginning to wander round with, straws in its
headgear.
Charlie Gore, one of Maoriland’s most
popular ex-cricketers, was caught by Death, off
Pneumonia, at Wellington, recently. He played for
his club, his province and his State from about
1890 to 1907, and was one of the most stylish bats-
men, brilliant fieldsmen and true sports that wore
the colors of any of them. He was only 42 when
his score was totalled.
At Christchurch (M.L.) the other day
there was a cricket match between the Railway
men and the Hotel employees. The ticket-snippers
piled up 129 in a very short time. Then the beer-
pump hands went to the sticks, and the Railway
put Beere on to bowl. And Beere, having the usual
demoralising effect, bagged 7 for 19.
When the N.S.W. and Vic. Xl’s faced
one another at Melbourne on New Year’s afternoon
the conditions were perfect, so N.S.W., having won
the toss, went to the wood and proceeded to do
some solid scoring. Collins, 73; Cody, 68; Bards-
ley, n. 0., 81; and Kelleway, 47, were the chief
actors m the raising of the Ma State score to 285
for six wickets. On Friday the score was lifted to
409, Bardsley being the not-out man with 124.
Macartney, though suffering from gastritis, played
and got a very respectable 53. Ryder took the bowl-
ing honors with five wickets for 138 runs. He was
well supported by Armstrong, whose three wickets
cost only 62 runs. The Victorian first innings was
ghastly, no less than four men (one absent) failing
to score at all. Armstrong headed the list with
34, Matthews was run out at 27, Carroll fell for 26
and Park went down for 25. Sundries did bril-
liantly for 17. The bowling laurels were well
divided, Massie taking three for 59, Kelleway three
for 15, and old, reliable Folkard two for 12. Vic-
toria followed on, and by night had lost two
wickets for three runs, including one sundry. On
Saturday the rot continued, in spite of a splendid
stand by Baring, who whacked 122. The only other
double figures were the 25 of Carroll, the 27 of
Matthews and the 10 of Sundries. Two men failed
to score and the side vanished for 199, leaving Ma
on top by an innings and 59 runs. The Victorians
were unlucky to this extent—two of their players
were practically disabled.
Tom Garrett, Ma’s first Public Trustee,
was a member of the pioneer Australian XI to visit
England in 1878, and was also in the teams of 1882
and 1886. lie was one of the most successful bowlers
who ever represented Australia in England. His
average in 1878 was 10.30 per wicket. Cricket 36
years ago was different from the game to-day, but
Garrett s figures were only once beaten—by Spof-
forth, the demon,” who completed the tour of 1880
with an average of 8.28.

“Stumps”: N. S. Wales Cricket Ass. has
decreed that the competition for the premiership
shall continue during the absence of the State rep.
team. the obvious result is that the clubs which
supply the cream of their players to the State eleven
are penalised. This may add to the uncertainty of
the final result, but it can’t be considered fair to the
affected clubs. University without their Massie
could not get the weak Middle Harbor team out on
Saturday, and this might cost ’Varsity the premier-
ship. Of course, it may be shown that Glebe with
four players in Melbourne trounced Balmain by bat-
U P i* 3 * n ', ns . for 7 wickets; but Balmain was
without Folkard, its only good bowler, so the cause
explains the result, as usual.

harto”: Here, are we getting slack?
Anyway how many records do we hold now—other
than fool records? The laurels for cricket, boxing,
tennis and sculling*, have all been taken from us. But
the record for continuous ball-punching is still ours.
Beginning at some hour on Monday night of Decem-
bei 22 at Albury (N.S.W.), one Barrie continually
punched the inflated bag till the same hour on the
following Wednesday, thus adding 180 min. to the
previous world’s record of 45 hours for this branch
of lunacy. And he did it, too, at a time when he
might have been well employed punching out bush-
fares with a green bush. If anyone smashes this
record I hope it won t be an Australian.
Amongst arrivals by the Morea, which
reached Fremantle on the 30th, is Barney Thompson,
the sole surviving member of the Joe Thompson
brotherhood, so well known in the Australian betting
ring m # the years that are behind.
A. London paper has published some weird
details of the betting mania in the North of England.
One man challenged “All Yorkshire” to a matchbox-
throwing competition, and staked £lO in earnest of
his claim to unrivalled skill. Brick-carrying is a
better-known outlet for the gambling spirit. Profes-
sionals in that line of art practise for hours every
evenmg. The artist takes a brick of a certain weight
and thickness between his thumb and forefinger and
walks till it drops. Or he remains stationary and
holds the brick against the clock. It is a ridiculous
sport ; nevertheless a brick-gripping match between
a workingman expert and an athlete of the Sandow
cult would be interesting. Feats of finger-strength
111 „ the . Physical culture curriculum. Clarence
Weber, for instance, can tear three packs of playing
cards with his fingers. Would he be a champion at
holding a cube of baked clay between forefinger and
thumb ?
