Of 64 who started from Sydney in the
motor-cycle reliability contest, only 23 reached the
Melbourne G.P.O. without disaster of some sort.
was the first to strike trouble, his machine
skidding soon after starting, and throwing him against
a tram. He lost half an ear, but rode on, after
sternly rebuking a John Hop who wanted to take
him to the hospital. Between Gundagai and Ger-
manton a flying stone threw his belt off the pulley
and .lambed the brake pedal when he was descending
a hill with a sharp turn at the bottom. Brakeless
he had to let her go—into a gutter 2ft. deep. He
was shot over the handles, and some damage was
done to the machine, but he straightened things
somewhat with a fence rail and bumped on, doing
the remaining 21 miles to Germanton in 36min.,
J?d re P or ti n ft there on time. He ultimately reached
Melbourne with the loss of only three points and
the above-mentioned portion of his anatomy. R. T.
Jones struck a deep gutter at Benalla, and somer-
saulted twice, but lost only a few inches of skin and
one point. A filthy patch of half-made road near
i ass capsized many competitors, T. Purves being
one of the worst sufferers. He had 15 falls altogether
during the run. E. C. Tyler and C. J. Kelynack
turned turtle in a 2ft. hole near Benalla,' but
both “reconstructed” and reached Melbourne without
the loss of points. A. Levi also came through
scathless, though had he been three inches further
a-rear when crossing the railway at Wangaratta the
express would have brought his remains down. A.
Sutherland broke a rim early in the ride, repaired
it with strips of tin, and struggled on, only to have
it buckled near Benalla. He walked the five miles
and bought a new one, and was thereabouts at the
finish. J. A. Fair jambed his finger at Wangaratta
and later again hit the same finger when removing
a piclced-up horseshoe. It’s a man’s game, all right.
Jack Donaldson, the Victorian sprinter,
put in a fine run at Cardiff (Wales) recently, when
he covered 220yds. in 21-Jsecs. and burst “Lachy”
McLachlan’s record of 21 2-ssecs. made at Napier
(M.L.) in 1902. Donaldson has now removed
nearly all the famous names from the record-
book. In getting over 220yds. in i-sec. less
than even time his run was probably better
than that in which he lifted Harry Hutchens’s figures
off the list for 300yds. Hutchens created 30secs.
(even time) for the distance as far back as 1884.
Donaldson is a warm-day runner, and complains if the
sun ducks behind a cloud for afew seconds. For him to
beat the 220.yds. figures during an English winter is
therefore almost marvellous. It goes to show that an
athlete can reproduce on foreign soil his best form,
provided he is properly acclimatised. Donaldson left
Australia in April, 1912, and it was not until a year
later that he eclipsed his best times. Chatting about
climates, Donaldson told the writer that Johannes-
burg (S’Afriea) is the best running place in the
world. The air thereabouts means yards to a runner.
On this point Postle, Conway, Mick Terry and Don-
aldson all agree.
The recent surfing fatality at Cronuila
Beach (Sydney), whereby three members of a family
were drowned, two of them in going to the assistance
of the other one, should make men who allow their
daughters to surf-bathe think hard. It must be
always remembered that surfing, though delightful, is
a dangerous pastime. Death is always hovering on
the line of breakers. In this case a party of ladies and
children went surfing on a (to them) unknown and lonelv
beach, and the onlv male with them was an heroic
boy of 12. The elder girl of the family walked into
the surf at as dangerous a spot as there is on the
coast near Sydney—a place where the shore suddenly
falls away to a great cliff, and where two warring
currents struggle for supremacy. She was quickly in
difficulties, and the hero of 12 swam out to try to
rescue her. He, too, was powerless against the
undertow. Then the younger sister of the first girl
went out to do or die—and died. First of all, there
should have been a danger notice at such a death-
trap. Secondly, families should never go surfing
where there are no life-savers on duty; if they do,
they should provide themselves with lifebelts. Had
that plucky 12-year-old boy had a cork jacket he
would have been able to keep his aunt afloat till
assistance came. As it was. when he was brought
out his heart was still beating faintly, but he was
too far gone to resuscitate.
The race for the Motor Boat Speed Cham-
pionship of Victoria, on New Year afternoon, ended
very unsatisfactorily. The only competitors were
Mr. Charles H. Ilelph, in Meteor 11., of Sydney; and
Mr. Cornwall, in Nautilus 11., of Victoria.
Meteor 11. is the new name of the famous hydro-
plane which, when owned by Mr. Tony Hordern, of
Sydney, was known as Kangaroo; and it is a craft
that can travel. The race was to be decided in three
bouts over five miles. The first went to Mr. Relph
and Meteor 11. in llmin. 27sec. When they were
ready for the second bout it was discovered that
Nautilus could not compete, the starting batteries
having run dry r so the Meteor had a walk-over,
there must have been some shocking bad manage-
ment to bring about such a fiasco.
The smashing of Dave Smith by Eddie
McGoortv was an impressive spectacle, even though
it was so brief. Smith was the first to enter the
ring, and bowed to the cheering thousands with easy
confidence. The special lights for photographic pur-
poses were blazing overhead, and the thoughtful Reg
Baker had countered their fervent heat by installing
two electric fans which drove the hot air upwards.
Then came McGoorty, “pride in his port, defiance in
his eye,” and superb self-confidence stamped all over
him. He had promised to knock Smith out in one
round ; he had told a daily newspaper that it would
be done with the left hook with which it was accom-
plished in New York, and had given a minute
account of how the left hook worked. The voice
of the announcer whispered that they had both
weighed list. 41b. that afternoon at 2 p.m. Yet
for some reason McGoorty looked a much bigger
and heavier man than Smith. He also looked a
more determined and dangerous fighter. From the
moment McGoorty entered the ring Smith seemed
worried. IVhen they posed together for the pictures
his face was somewhat drawn ; and when he accepted
the crayon portrait of McGoorty he looked as though
he was thinking of something very different—pro-
bably of how to block that dreaded left hook. And,
unfortunately, it did not occur to him that abso-
lutely the best defence against a Yankee left hook
is a straight left flush on to the nose, repeated
rapidly, and varied by stabs to the point. There
was a terrible lot of delay, and at last the pent-up
impatience of the crowd broke forth in a storm ot
hoots.
This writer’s notes of the fight consist of two
lines: “Smith, black trunks; McGoorty, green
trunks.” There was no time to write more, for just
at that moment Scott said, “Box on!” Smith came
out of his corner hesitatingly. His right glove was
up to cover his jaw, and he had a horrible half-
crouch. McGoorty had no thought of defence. He
came in perfectly open for the quick left stab to
the nose that would have sent all his aims awry—
but Smith had no left stab about him. Smith hardly
left his corner, and waited to be attacked. McGoorty
walked right across the ring, and feinted with his
left hook. Smith guarded and stepped back.
McGoorty bounded in. There was an awful smack,
a resounding thwack that could be heard in the White
City, and Smith shot against the ropes and slipped
down to the floor. The left hook had arrived. It
was obvious that Smith had forgotten for the moment
all he ever knew of how to fight. Instead of staying
down for nine and recovering as much as possible,
he sprang up at five. McGoorty let fly a left and
right that would have lifted Smith’s head right off.
But Smith ducked them both and backed away.
Still no sign of a poke to the nose. McGoorty fol-
lowed up, and again Smith was sent prone. Up
again, and hardly up before he was once more
hurled against the ropes and by them flung right
back to roll on to his side. Up once more and out of
a shower of ferocious wallops, with a split cheek, a
bleeding mouth, and a battered nose, he reeled against
the ropes on the northern side. There he covered
un his face and waited for what was coming. A
pained expression came into McGoorty’s eyes. He
saw his man was done. He sent a few lefts and
rights on to Smith’s gloves, when he could have
uppercut him. Smith reeled and swayed, and once
drove a feeble right swing, so pitifully futile that
the American looked at Scott and said something
about stopping the fight. But Smith was still on
his feet; his towel had not come in. “Box on!”
Muttering something about being sorry, McGoorty
again stepped forward and battered the gloves of
the swaying gladiator. Then Smith broke away
towards mid-ring, and McGoorty, leisurely tripping
after him, with a perfectly-measured right uppercut
sent him to the floor. He fell backwards, and turn-
ing as he fell, came down on his side and then
rolled over on to his back, with his arms spread-
eagled knocked out. Before the count could be
gone through the towel soared in. And even then
it was less than two minutes from the time
McGoorty started to walk across the ring to where
Smith awaited his doom.
Saturday night saw another disturbance
in the Stadium ring. This time it was “Young”
Saylor and Alf Morey, the Westralian boxer. Saylor
is a confident young man who is going to receive a
short, sharp shock before long. He is alleged to
have bet a compatriot a new suit of clothes that he
knocks out his first four opponents. Well, if he
leaves himself as open as he did on Saturday night,
he will see stars. Had Morey fought up to his usual
standard he would have shifted Saylor’s proboscis.
But he didn’t. The wild yells of the Yankee base-
ballers, who were fresh from the banquet at the Hotel
Australia, broke him up, and he fell, like the walls of
Jericho, before a great noise. Saylor is a most re-
markable fighter—it were a joke to call him a boxer.
He has a _ terrific reach, and he swings in blows
which no right-thinking boxer would wait to receive.
Morey did —which was a great mistake. He should
have charged right in and hit Saylor hard on the
nose or the chin immediately that young man began
to issue posters to the effect that he would, in the
near future, swing the right. Morey’s trouble was
that he would attempt to hit only about twice in
each round, and fought a purely defensive fight. Say-
lor hit all the time. Sometimes he hit right round
the other fellow’s head and landed the blow on the
mouth. Sometimes he hit right round his own neck
and shoulders and caught Morey in the same place.
His simian arms were so long he could hit from any
quarter and at any angle, and, being apparently
double-jointed, direction makes no difference to him.
Morey was down for nine seconds in the second round,
and apparently tired rapidly after that. The end
came in the 14th, when Saylor landed a left swing
on the ribs and just clipped the temple with the
right. Morey intended to get up at nine, but the
clock beat him ; he was actually counted out, and
Scott was placing his hand on Saylor’s head when
Morey rose and squared up once more. As he did so,
Saylor, taking no chances, suddenly uppercut him,
and that time Morey went down in grim earnest. It
was a weird happening, but took place so quickly
that the referee had no time to interfere.
Dave Smith is under contract to Baker
for three more fights, the opposite parties being Jeff
Smith, Jimmy Clabby and Georges Carpentier, if the
last-named can be induced to come to Australia. The
chances are that Smith will never fight really well
again, and if he is wise he will get out of the
gaipe. A man can stand only one hammering like
that which he received from McGoorty on New Year’s
night.

ESTABLISHED 30 YEARS.
There s a row over the sculling champion-
ship of the world. Ernest Barry is the holder of the
title, but on October 23 last year William Albany
challenged him to row for it and £2OO a-side,
according to the rules subscribed to by Barry himself
last year. He accompanied his challenge' with a
cheque for £25. So far, all was in order. Barry
replied that lie would be delighted to row Mr.
Albany, but the stake must be £5OO. Albany’s
backers looked up the rules, and found that £2OO is
the minimum for rowers of the same country. Since
then Barry has accented a challenge from ,T. Paddon
(N.S.W.), who, by the same rules, must put up
£5OO. As the rules give the holder three months
in which to accept a challenge, Albany lias notified
the world that at the end of that term he will claim
the title and defend it against all comers. Barry’s
object would appear to be to make matches with a
£5OO stake in preference to those worth only £2OO
—which, after all, is very natural.
Totalisator investments in and near Ade-
laide for last year came to just half a million sterling
for about 30 afternoons’ racing. Of that total the
various racing clubs got £37,500 towards their
stakes, the S.A. Government was “sweetened” by a
trifle of £12,500, and the people who did the
betting cut up among them £450,000 —90 per cent,
of what they put in. The useless, unproductive
parasite whose bag is not allowed to be seen, or his
voice heard, on a South Australian course, got
nothing.
The lion of Australian cricket just now'
is undoubtedly little C. G. Macartney (N.S.W.).
This season he has scored 195 against Queensland,
117 and 52 against South Aus., 142 against • Maori-
land, 53 against Victoria, and 162 in Grade Cricket
—668 runs, at the tall average of 120.1.
* * * *
Bobbie Lewis, who is getting into the
sere and yellow leaf of Australian jockeydom, had
a joyous time on Wcstralian Derby Day. He pushed
Radnor to victory in the Derby, Hathor in the
Karrakatta Plate, and Dollar Dictator in the Visit-
ors’ Handicap—all consecutive winners.
After running second in the A.J.C. and
V.R.C. Derbies at Sydney and Melbourne, Radnor
was bought with a view to annexing the Westralian
event. He justified the deal by snatching the blue
ribbon and £l5OO prize money. Pilbarra, the
odds-on favorite, failed to run into even a place
among the dozen starters.
The Gght between Colin Bell, the Moree
(N.S.W.) heavyweight, and Sam McVea, the negro,
at the Olympia (Sydney), was brief and exciting
while it lasted. Bell started well, and showed excel-
lent defensive work, taking McVea’s famous left
hooks on his glove. Feeling his way cautiously, he
gave the great black a few that hurt. Backing out
of a clinch he held his head too low, and McVea
promptly chopped him across the back of the neck,
sending him to his knee. Springing up, he clinched.
As he broke away McVea hurled in a wicked left
hook, but Bell, evading it, countered with a splendid
right uppercut that sent the black to the boards
with a crash. Thunders of applause. McVea sprang
up, and a scramble ensued. The end came early in
the second round. McVea, unable to break through
Bell’s smother, left an opening. Quick as a flash
Bell uncovered and sent the left to the negro’s face ;
but, following up, he received a right on the cheek
which sent him down. Springing up he charged in
recklessly, and McVea met him with a right cross.
It caught him on the temple. Bell rolled over twice,
went to rise, but could not, and was counted out. He
had twisted his ankle out of joint and was sent to
hospital. McVea's luck was in. He would pro-
bably have won anyhow, but he would have had to
fight for it.
* * * *
Fights between Sam Langford and Joe
Jeannette are recurrent outbursts which can be fore-
casted as accurately as the transits of Venus. The
arrangement seems to be that Langford shall win
two out of three or thereabouts. The latest took
place in Paris, and is described as a marvellous ex-
hibition of pluck by Jeannette, who, though knocked
silly in the 13th, kept going till the 20th round. Balder-
dash ! No fight between these bosom friends is ever
a genuine smashing match. They fool the public,
and the Parisian public seems a dead-easy public to
fool in the matter of scraps. No man on earth who
has been “knocked silly” in the 18th, let alone the
13th, round has a chance of stalling Langford off till
the 20th if the Tar Baby is really fighting.
Packev McFarland, the Yankee, is a some-
what unusual fighter. In his first recorded scrap he
earned a lOdol. note ; now he is worth over £lOO,OOO,
all made since 1904. He has fought 100 fights, and
won 53 on knocks-out and 16 on points. Four were
drawn ; the other 27 were “no decision” combats.
He has never had a decision given against him, has
never been knocked off his feet, has never had a
black eye, and has no sign of cauliflower ear, nor
any other mark of the game left on him. He has
now given up boxing, and is attending a college to
improve his education with a view to becoming a
professional man. How the boxer has changed!
The British Lion has his tail right up as
far as speed- motoring is concerned, practically every
world’s record from 50 miles to 1078 being held by
a British-built car. Just consider this little list: —-

“Leo Lear”: How’s this for bowling? At
a match in Tyrendarra (Vic.) R. Bannam, playing
for Milltown, went on to bowl when the score was
70 for two wickets. He outed five of the locals in
one over for no runs, four with consecutive halls and
the fifth with the last of the over. No ordinary
hump-and-hollow country wicket either, but matting
ort good concrete —a batsman's joy.
The latest development in international
sport is that Germany has sent a hockey team to*
play England. In that sort of tour it is often a
question of who can get away, and this team was all
from Hamburg, barring three Munichs and a Berlin.
Anyway, the English took it seriously, picked their
best team, and licked it by nine goals to one, the
same score England made against France (in Paris)
earlier in the year.
* * * *
“Moana”: A sample of how records are
manufactured in the Land of Wooden ~ Ham.
Recently C. K. G. Billings, the owner of the famous
trotter Uhlan, announced that this fleet-footed prad
would make a final appearance on the track at Lex-
ington (U.S.A.), when an attempt would be made to
seriously damage the previous best for a mile. The
crack trotter was hitched to a four-wheeled waggon
along with a galloper. Charlie Tanner drove, and
alleged experts present contend that for seven-eighths
of the journey Uhlan led the pacemaker and carried
weight. As the mile fled past in lmin. 54i sec., the
trip is hailed as a world’s record for Uhlan, and the
news is cabled that at Lexington (U.S.A.) a horse
has trotted a mile in lmin. 54isec. —a world’s re-
cord! But is it? Is any record made under such
conditions worthy of serious consideration?

iwW'lft u culminated in a libel action. During

one of the most interesting in modern Australasian
press history.
“Ahaswer”: George Ryan, Horsham (Vic ) de-
serves to have his name mentioned in the Inkv’Wav
column for I’ll warrant he has sold more Australian
books than any other bush bookseller. You may nor
find on Ins shelves the latest sloppy best-seller from
the land of the two Georges, but you will find there
almost everything published in Australia. He sold
600 copies of a book of mine 'by telling his cockv
clients that he knew me, and I was a decent sort anil
deserved encouragement, finishing up with, “Anyway
its only a shilling; and the shilling will stav in
Australia ” Every Saturday, when cocky farme?
comes into his shop in dozens, he draws out a book
“Here’s a real good thing! Just out! J ust what
, An n something Australian.
He is the bookseller who is far too rare in Australia.

THE INDOLENTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This poet never has a word to say
When sits he, pen in hand, betimes to write
So has he scarce a thought from morn to night
IN or is ins output worthy, day by day.
These circumstances may not be unique;
There may, perhaps, be many another bard
Who finds poetic composition hard ;
Nor is the explanation far to seek.
High in that mountain cleft, the little springs
Come trickling down from dripping stone to stone;
Higher, upon the topmost peak, alone,
A wind-racked pine-tree to the worn rock clings.
h ar, on the plain, the widening river winds
Till straightened-and controlled by hand of man;
And, over it, the mighty bridges span,
And, on it, ships, the toys of master minds.
H'rn ! Does the explanation seem far sought,
\Vhy poets do not labor as they might—
“Vlby I and others do not reach the height,
Nor fill so full the measure as, we ought?
We sit midway upon an easeful slope,
No harried pine to whip the storm ta song ;
Nor spring nor runnel sounds its silver gong • ’
We sit, midway, and play with idle hope.
We flare not be the greater, lesser thing—
The ordered force, the cultivated power;
We breathe the unbought sunshine ,of the hour
And, scarcely else than as we yawn, we sing. ’
Majorca.

CAN 1 OBTAIN A PATENT?
J lie quickest way to ascertain
this, WITHOUT EXPENSE, is
to write to me describing your invention, enclosing
a small sketch where necessary. Nicety of drawing
is not essential; all that is necessary at first is to
give me YOUR IDEA.
I will at once inform you whether the idea is
patent-able, and, if so, give the necessary instructions
for further procedure.
Persons,desirous of obtaining really reliable advice
or information regarding Patent matters are cordi-
ally invited to consult me personally or by letter. In
all cases a careful consideration of their plans, an
honest opinion, and a prompt reply may be
expected.

John Sands’ N.S.W. Directory is 82 p a °-es
fatter than it was in 1932; and still there is
nothing in the corpulent volume that could
he spared. As usual there are the general
alphabetical directory, and the professional
and trade directory, and a directory of com-
mercial and professional people for each
country town, and a directory of pastoral
people, and every other convenience for
rapid reference which 54 years of experience
has suggested.
IN
BANANA LAND
A coming man in Queensland Liberal
politics is undoubtedly Colonel Rankin, who
represents the sugar farmers and miners of
Howard in the State Parliament. An hon-
orary Minister-ship already dangles before
him—an earnest of the impending full-
blown honors of the near future.
“Albion”: Scalpers are having a giddy
time in the Maranoa district among the
kangaioos. A party of three arrived in
Mitchell recently, after being seven weeks
out with 1100 skins, which averaged 2s 6d!
each, I.h. I’ve been running a newspaper,
but 1 ve sold it and bought a rifle with the
proceeds.
A recent Bulletin forecast as to the
appointment of Gordon Graham to-be Under-
secretary for Lands proved correct. Graham
is an able, courteous oflicial, full of infor-
mation, and—what is more—full also of a
desire to impart it. A successful career as
Under-Sec. looks a safe prediction. He is
tie third youthful Under-Sec. appointed in
Bananaland m the last 12 months, juveni
in of 1 ? 8 ' no ! on « er a bar to promotion in
the State service.

When an aspirant is recommended in
Queensland as a suitable character to be
made a J.P., the local sergeant of police
is usually requested to report on his fitness
- the. nigh honor. On one occasion Ser-
geant Blank, of Dingo Flat, damned the
chances ot Simon Spud, Esq., in these scath-
ing terms, and the record is still in the
Department: “Cannot recommend him for
J.l. Flays the fiddle and behaves in other
ways unbecoming of a gentleman.”
A notable Brisbane identity went over
last week in the person of Capt. William
i o‘» i n i • at tdle age ,d ' 80. Born in Essex in
7, V ns , early llfe was spent on colliers in
the North Sea and as a bargeman on the
J lianies. lb' left England for Australia in
Ibob, and was one of a crew, carrying 800
Chinese coolies to the port of' Callao,
v looked on Brampton Sands, Solomon
Islands. Between 700 and 800 lives were
then lost. Collin escaped in a boat with
31 others. He left Botany Bay in a small
vessel, the Ellen Collin, for Moreton Bay,
ln , .lob 2, and built the commercial house
which is now William Collin and Sons
JLimited. His was the first master’s certi-
ficate issued in Queensland ; and he beaconed
the inner route of the Great Barrier Reef
between Cooktown and Thursday Island,
tor the Queensland Government in 187 S.

Pastor Niemeyer, head of the Queensland
German Apostolic Church, held a Christmas
festival service at Hatton Vale. The dele-
gates in attendance were so numerous that
it is proudly recorded, 18001 b. of sausage
had to lie made daily. A spirituality deep-
rooted in three-quarters of a ton of sausage
has much to commend it.

Government complained that it couldn’t get
ships enough to bring out its nominated
emigrants from Great Britain. To-dav the
complaint is that the Cold Land can’t sup-
ply immigrants enough for the available
vessels, wherefore Queensland advertises in
Germany and Scandinavia for settlers. The
immigration fever is a curiously fitful epi-

“Bildad”: “Billy” Murphy, M.L.A. for
Groydon and Burketown and a few thou-
sand square miles of Gulf country, leads the
Independent party in the Bananalaud Par-
liament, _ and is said to have no difficulty
in securing regular attendance at divisions
or maintaining leadership. This is possiblv
due to the fact that when he puts on his
truly remarkable hat the whole of the partv
is under the one roof. Canvassing a Gulf
electorate is strenuous work, and Murphy
Had a lively experience just before the last
elections, in which several large crocodiles,
a small boat, and two days of hunger and
tlm-st were associated with a visit to Burke-
town The temptation to Gulf crocodiles
must be very considerable when Murphy goes
anoat, as he would provide several meals
lor a whole creekful. Strenuousness does
not cease even with the declaration of the
polls A dissatisfied minority is apt to put-
tlie boot into the successful candidate on
occasion, and Murphy tells a tale of a dance
at the Einasleigh at which he realised from
personal experience that one or two of the
dancers had omitted to change their work-
ing footgear. When at home he runs Croy-
don s only newspaper, and does much to
temper the lot of those who have to live in
that goat-stricken pile of bankrupt galva-
nised iron. s
Magnificent passage from speech by
M.L.A. Luke (Aubigny) : “If the men of the
Labor party would stop pulling the legs of
the worker and take to pulling teats, it
would be a good thing for the State.” Note
the Liberal cocky’s idea of an earthly para-
dise-—a land full of cows and everybody
milking away like mad.
A Brisbane milk-and-water vendor, when
charged with carrying adulterated milk,
sought to excuse himself on the plea that
he carried two cans, one of pure milk for
his paying customers, the other a mixture
tor the bad pays. So the well-to-do, with
the generally-empty cradle, can get the
pure article, while the poor man, with a
houseful of children, to whom the pure milk
is necessary, gets the starvation diet. The 1
trader was quite annoyed when the PM
see his point, and fined him £24
mill his license wasn’t cancelled, so he
is given a chance to sell some more water
at the price ,of milk, and thus get square
with everybody.
I? J ian^an : Barcaldine, which grows some
ut the finest wool in the world, and that on
a minimum of water, was recently congratu-
lating itself on getting a much-needed water
supply. The Government had agreed to
hand out £5OOO for an elevated tank,-etc
and tenders were accepted away back in
April. As dry weather was expected the
Government was asked to hustle. It hustled
in its own way. After a few months, when
most ot the goats of the district were draw-
! n », wader from the bore, it answered the
halt-dozen telegrams to say that the Depart-
ment had searched all over the office for a
suitable engine in vain; and as it would
take three months to indent one from Eng-
land, by which time the dry season would be
over, it had decided to let the matter drop.
Lut Barcaldine, being pernickety, is not so
willing to let the matter drop, as it expects
another dry season to start some day. Also
it does not believe that Queensland cannot
provide the small engine necessary. In fact
the local tinsmith offered to make one at a
pinch.
An Offshore Reverie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, hear the whisper of the steam—the
engines’ steady beat!
Ihe bow wave has a silver fringe, the c-itv
falls astern ; J
The clean sea-wind that lifts the flag knows
neither house nor street.
io learn: once more the truth of life back to
the sea'we turn.
Inshore, beneath the shelt’ring hills the
anchored vessels swing;
Their sails are stowed; their voyage done.
Aloft the ensigns show.
ihe long, lone, golden beaches where the
brave salt breezes sing
Aow hold them willing captives while the
green tides ebb and flow.
Tall ships, with only clouds for sails and
naught but dreams for freight,
o what lost port you bear me now, though
anchored there in peace!
The soothing whisper of the steam—the
engines’ beat sedate—
lave wiped the city fret awav and given
me release.
So, while I travel past your bay, in fancy
I m with you ;
I tread alone your silent decks beneath the
tow ring spars;
Back to the vanished Port of Youth, where
sea and sky are blue,
I sail _ across enchanted seas past Life’s
grim reefs and bars.
Youth’s morning lies somewhere away be-
yond the world’s blue rim *
I cannot glimpse it in the street, so narrow
mean and grey ;
But when the city in its smoke is very far
and dim, J
And from the sea a clean wind blows, I see
the hidden way.
0 anchored ships below the hills and lonelv
sands of gold !
I put aside the foolish task and turn to you
this day! J
I may not stop to taste of it; but still for
me you hold
1 he sweetness of Life’s primal joy that does
not pass away.

Deaconess Buchanan, who has worn
l .nibin m j. eac ! un S' the gospel to discolored
heathen, died this weekm St. Helen’s Hospi-
J T rvl She j^ as been since last September.
J oi 10 years she lived on the island of Moa
(noitli of Northern Territory), where there
was no other white person, and since then
has worked in China and Port Darwin. She
was a frail little bit of a woman, with a
great share ot courage, and a will that kept
her going solus when mere ordinary persons
would have been clamoring for two or three
doctors and an army of nurses.
Our very own aviator, A. IV. .Tones, has
been distinguishing himself by falling 60ft
and not being killed. It is a useful gift,
this, to those who yearn to fly. Provided
always that lie does not lose the knack of
a ing irom dizzy heights and remaining
alive, young Jones ought to help the great
Jones family to the admiring consideration
of posterity. A boy who has lived most of his
life in our Warwick, and makes the first
aeroplane with which lie flies, has something
to the good in that receptacle where the grev
matter should be but too often isn’t.
The races on Saturday were not very
largely attended. Most of the female
patrons wore white gowns, and fanned them-
selves profusely while they did fraction sums
and struggled with the odds. Mrs. Blume
wore another enviable creation, and Mrs
Jack Thomson and Mrs. E. T. Tully were
as usual two of the Best-dressed.
The American baseballers arrived here
early on New Year’s morning, and gave an
exhibition of Uncle Sam’s exhilarating game
before sliding for Southern bases. The party
is about 68 strong, and of ,these"ls are ladies.
A good sprinkling of the remainder are press-
men.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

To produce a full, rounded bust where none
existed before, to grow new flesh in the exact

flat or hanging bust to perfect firmness and
beauty—such is the seeming miracle now
wrought by modern science. No woman
need longer be humiliated and embarrassed
because she is thin, flat-chested and unde-
veloped, for the delightful fulness and re-
freshing firmness of a captivating bust is
easily within her reach. For more than
thirty years Frofessor Muller, the eminent
chemist and scientist, searched and studied
with tireless zeal in an effort to discover a
means for creating new flesh wherever de-
sired. It seemed at times like a hopeless task,
but he steadily kept on with his scientific
experiments, casting aside all the old
theories. At last, after more than a quarter
of century, his labor was crowned with suc-
cess. He has given to the world what is
probably the most important discovery of
modern times. Formerly, there was abso-
lutely nothing known which would actually
enlarge the bust to the size and firmness
desired, but now that a development of from

three weeks’ time, it is not surprising that
the news of this remarkable discovery is
already being heralded over two continents.
No woman is too old; no woman is too

and hygienic in every respect. You can see

your very eyes from the first day you com-
mence its use. It possesses wonderful effi-
cacy for filling out hollows in the neck,
shoulders or arms, or nroducing an enlarge-
ment on any part of the body. Yet its
specific action is upon the bust. AND WE

SCENE IN THE SLUMS.
In a lane off little Burke-st
During a late hour of the night
Two young and ill-famed Women
Like two wild cats did fight.
Blood trickled from their hollow cheeks
As the drunken creatures fought
Prompted by a roughish mob
Whom enjoyed the cruel sport
When a woman from a Chinese den
Wrest the opponents apart.
The poor haggard creature of the slums
Had humanity in her heart.

“Melb”: During Premier Elmslie’s 17
days, Watt and McLeod collogued. McLeod:
“I claim a place in the Ministry for 1 myself
and one follower. Also that Murray and
Attorney-General Brown leave.” Watt: “I
will take you in, but no follower. And I
refuse to drop either Murray or Brown, but
will make McKinnon Attorney-General, and
shunt Brown to something else.” McLeod
declined to take office unless one of his party
was with him. So Watt went ahead with-
out any of them. Obviously McLeod with
his following, and the Ministers discarded
by Watt, will soon join Labor in Opposition.
Watt seems to be working only to keep him-
self in evidence with a view to the flying
trapeze act, a. la the Iceberg, landing into the
Federal Parliament.
Agar Wynne begins to dawn on public
notice in Melbourne as a high-frequency
lunch-expert. In the purlieus of Parliament
House for some months past Australia’s
P.M.G. has been feeding relays of legislators,
Government officials and other persons vari-
ously connected with his new job. The
M’s.P. he “dines” in batches, the Labor lion
sitting quietly beside the Liberal lamb and
never forgetting to use its table-napkin at
the right moment, while Labor sheep and
Liberal goats chew amicably at portions of
the same cabbage. The latest of the Wynne
series was that at which the Federal Han-
sard men were fed, together with a company
of assorted pressmen from all over Australia.
As Wynne is popularly reported to have an
income of “five figures.” it should be pleasant
for those lunched or dined to remember that
they are not nibbling a fellow-man into the
Insolvency Court.

Ear Drums have been giving good hear-

people. Every condition of deafness or

helped and cured, such as
Catarrhal Deafness, Relaxed

Sounds, Perforated or Parti-
ally Destroyed Drums, Drums
wholly Destroyed, Discharge
from Ears. No matter how
long standing the case may
be, there is hope of good hear-
ine- for all the afflicted deaf.
The Common-Sense Ear Drums are made of soft,
sensitised material, comfortable and safe to
wear. They are out of sight when worn, and
easily adjusted by wearer.

sunshine into the life of the lonely deaf. The

Legal Notices.
IN TIIE SUPREME COURT OF
QUEENSLAND.
No. 354 of 1913.
Mr. Justice Chubb.
BETWEEN
ROBERT JOHN SIMPSON, Plaintiff,
AND
WILLIAM SIMPSON, Defendant.
TO WILLIAM SIMPSON, formerly of
Currumbin, in the State of Queensland, in
the Commonwealth of Australia, Farmer, but
believed to be lately residing in the State of
Western Australia.
TAKE NOTICE that Robert John Simp-
son, of Currumbin, aforesaid, Farmer, has
commenced an action against you, William
Simpson, in his Majesty’s Supreme Court at
Queensland, by a Writ of that Court dated
the fourth .day of December, One thousand
nine hundred and thirteen, which Writ is
endorsed follows :
“THE PLAINTIFF’S CLAIM is
against the defendant for partition of all
that piece or parcel of land containing
One hundred and sixty acres more or
less situated in the Parish of Tallebud-
gera, County of Ward, being Farm No.
13, Beenleigh District, Portion 19V, and
being the whole of the land described in
Deed of Grant No. 78463, Volumfe 630,
Folio 223, of which said land the plain-
tiff and the defendant are the registered
proprietors under the Real Property
Acts 1861 and 1877 for an estate in
fee simple as joint tenants, or in the
alternative for the sale of the said
piece or parcel of land in lieu of such
partition as aforesaid and the distribu-
tion of the proceeds of such sale.
“And for costs.”
AND you are required within two months
after the receipt of this notice, inclusive of
the day of such receipt, to defend the said
action by causing an appearance to be
entered for you in the said Court to the said
action and in default of your so doing the
said Robert John Simpson may by leave of
the Court or a Judge proceed therein and
judgment may be given in your absence.
You may appear to the said Writ by
entering .an appearance personally or by
your Solicitor at the Supreme Court Office
at Brisbane.
Dated this Eleventh day of December,
1903.
McCOWAN & LIGPITOLLER,
Solicitors for the Plaintiff, Robert John
Simpson,
New Zealand Chambers,
334 Queen Street,
Brisbane.

Cuticura Soap and Ointment.

“Coo-ee," Chirnside St., Footscray, Vic.
“The affection from which I suffered is,
thanks to Cuticura, quite cured. It first
appeared around my knuckles and finger
joints. I first noticed it by my hands con-
stantly itching. On putting my hands in
water the burning feeling would nearly send
mo distracted. The palms of my hands
were all cracked and the skin completely
peeled off them, and on awakening during
the night tho continual itching would keep
mo awake for hours. I would rub and
scratch them until tho backs of my hands
would look like raw flesh. I determined to
try the Cuticura Remedies, and having Cu-
tipura Ointment in the house, I bathed my
hands with Cuticura Soap and then applied
Cuticura Ointment. I suffered with this
dreadful affliction for six weeks, but perse-
vered with Cuticura Soap and Cuticura
Ointment and am now quite recovered. I
always use Cuticura Soap as a toilet soap
and would not be without the Cuticura
Ointment." (Signed) Miss C. Florence
Daley, October, 1912.
The regular use of Cuticura Soap for toilet
and bath not only tends to preserve, purify
and beautify the skin, scalp, hair and hands,
but assists in preventing inflammation, irri-

“X P D”: After having spent a few years in Aus-
tralian engineering works, I have acquired a solid
contempt for the argument that certain articles
be made by Australian workers, and that the tarill
should accordingly be very merciful to those goods.
For years past, Queensland-built locomotives have
shown themselves at least equal to the English and
Yankee importations in efficiency ; they are superior
in faithful finish of the little parts that count for
long life; arid,, most important, they are abstemious
in regard to running-shed repairs and renewals.
They’re a bit dearer than the engines built by Eng-
lish fitters at 33s. a week; but —neglecting the con
snleration whether it isn’t better for us to distribute
£4000 at Newport or Ipswich than £3500 in Yonc
shire or Philadelphia—I reckon there are ways of
bringing down the cost without lowering wages For
one thing, the railway departments might stock one
set of templates and patterns for each class of loco,
required, and lend them to successful tenderers, in-
stead of obliging each firm to work out its problems
from the drawings. Also let the draughtsmen s
for making unnecessary differences lie tween parts be
firmly suppressed, so that a lot of class X gear can
be duplicated for class Y. Such are the fruits of
my experience now gratefully dropped on to the
plate of my country.
“Moana”: In B. 4/12/’13 “J.D.K.Z.” wails about
artists and singers being rushed, regardless of ex-
pense, to Fogtown to “finish off, and wants to
know why the same thing isn’t done with young
fellows who show brilliant talents as drapers, it is.
The big rag firms of Maoriland, for instance, rush
more young men away to Smokeville in one year to
be finally polished for the trade than are sent in
10 years to learn to spoil good canvas or to make a
pleasant noise. M.L. rag palaces are full of young
men who have been “finished” at home at the firm s
expense. Whether in these days of lightning change
in fashions a young man can gain any experience in
Fogland likely to be worth a red cent, or whether
he can really learn to measure calico there better
than in M.L., is debatable; but there s no denying
the haughty mannah six months in. the Big Smoke
can tack on. Certainly, some firms are now going
slower, having discovered that the finishing p
cess is liable to be disastrous when the Rag Kings
bucolic clients meet the results on the other side of
the counter.
* * * *
“Hermes”: I have just opened a tin of Rhodesian
tobacco mixture, grown in the youngest colony ot the
Empire, manufactured in Capetown, and exported to
the uttermost ends of Empire. Australia can glow
an infinite variety of tobaccos, including weed of
high grade and distinctive flavor. But so far as l
can ascertain there is no Australian mixture on the
market of even Australia. Here the hall-mark is.
“Made of the best American leaf. Why? the
Jews in Capetown more patriotic than the Anglo-
Yankees of N.S.W. and Victoria? When Joe Cook
has grown tired of doing nothing, and doing it very
well, he might turn his attention to this problem,
and ask the Trust in a firm voice when it proposes to
justify itself. A Trust that smothers local tobacco-
growers to encourage those of Old Virginia wants
arguing with —and no nonsense about it.
“T OI”: For something like half a century
Bathurst’(N.S.W.) has been supplied with gas by a
private co. founded by John Newlands Wark, one-time
engineer to the A.G.L. Co. When J. N. Wark died
he left the works to his widow and sons by his first
wife They carried them on until the widow s
death recently, and are now selling to the municipal
council. Under Mark Hammond s Municipal Gas Act
the Bathurst council nearly 30 years ago established
gasworks primarily to supply the city with lights,
and incidentally to sell to private consumers. This
latter was deemed illegal, and a lawsuit ensued,
which ended in favor of the council. The Warks
who were running the business on family with
very little outlay for labor, cut the price to the very
finest, and the municipal works had to do likewise.
Private consumers gave the council and the Warks
month about, so as to keep the competition going
and the price down. Now after a generation has
passed, the council buys out Wark, and thus at last
gets a monopoly of the business.
“J P ” • In the early days of the Maoriland strike
disturbance Auckland couldn’t get sugar away, and
a sugar famine threatened in the South Island. Away
down at the polar end of the Rickety Isles the little
Scotch storekeepers openly cursed the strikers when
they heard the news ; secretly they moved round to
the telegraph office and wired the Employers Federa-
tion at Wellington to stand firm, and then dashed
back to their stores and pushed the price of the
sugar they had on hand up to nearly three times the
usual figure. # *
“Theodore Hogwash”: The directors and manager
of one strict and economical fortress of finance pre-
sented the staff and others with a quaint sort of
Christmas card. Outwardly it was just like the
thousand other inane things that wear out the post-
man at this time of year; but instead of the usual
greetings it was afflicted inwardly with a number of
confused yelps by Elbert Hubbard on the subject of
health. They covered the paper excitedly without
diffusing any useful knowledge or committing Hub-
bard or the directorate to anything definite, and were
calculated to blow up the sympathetic reader and
give him that light feeling in his feet without actu-
ally lifting him anywhere. The final burst was as
follows: “Breathe deeply in the open air, holding the
breath, then expelling it slowly through the nos-
trils ” As soon as possible after receiving this
valuable Christmas bonus, the various members of
the staff breathed through their nostrils—breathed so
hard that they could be heard all over the place.
Some of them haven’t finished yet.
TWO KINDS OF CANNIBALISM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Thousands of the natives on Maiekula Island, w
the New Hebrides, are cannibals, sau ’ .
Gillan, Presbyterian missionary, now visiting: Syd
addS'tbat about 1500 Meg
natives were Christians, but there were still 10,000,
the majority of whom
It was on Christmas Eve I read
A holy man had just come home
With tales of cannibals who fed
Upon their friends across the foam.
At once the plaintive Christmas bells,
The dreary hymns and carols dire
Seemed to be drowned by savage yells
And hiss of “long pig” on the fire.
The cannibal’s white molars shone.
And grisly visions round me swirled ;
How sad to think he sat upon
The round plum-pudding of the World .
I cursed the savage at his feast
And wished him by a cruiser bust,
Until I thought: “The howling beast
Is somewhat milder than a Trust!
“The Trust devours me ev’ry day ;
He, eats me once and it’s Good-night!
The Trust takes all I have away,
Yet leaves me life and appetite!”
I heard the carols and the bells
That old Romance with puddings weave,
And longed to hear the honest yells
Of cannibals on Christmas Eve.
N.B.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.

Miss NANCE
McINERNEY
(From a photo.)
The Champion Surf Siren
tells how she Benefited by
the use of REXONA
SKIN and FACIAL SOAP.
Miss Nance Mclnerney, West’s Pictures
Champion Surf Siren, is one of Sydney s
fairest daughters. Not only did she win the
first heat of the Surf Sirens’ Competition by
a large surplus of votes, hut she was also the
winner of the Challenge Competiton.
Miss Mclnerney is certainly a picture of
health and beauty, and in the accompanying
letter she tells how she has found Rexona
Skin and Facial Soap indispensable for her
toilet.
21 Darling Street,
Glebe Point, Sydney,
29th Dec., 1911.
The Manager,
“Rexona,” Sydney.
Dear Sir, —
I have long found your Rexona Skin and
Facial Soap indispensable for my toilet. I
use it for my face, and it keeps my skin fair
and delicate; also for my hair, which it keeps
in perfectly healthy condition. For my teeth
I find it unsurpassed; its disinfectant quali-
ties keeping my mouth sweet and my teeth
pearly. I would never be without Rexona
Soap.
Yours faithfully,
(Sgd.) NANCE McINERNEY.
THE REXONA HABIT.
A habit formed is hard to break. Bad habits are easily formed, but good habits can be attained
almost as easily. The Rexona habit results in health and beauty, and once formed will never be
broken.
There are two Rexona hab-
its —that of using Rexona
Ointment to heal all injuries
to the skin, and the Rexona
Soap habit, which consists of
keeping oneself clean and pure
•with a delicately-scented and
medicated soap. Rexona Oint-
ment and Rexona Soap mean

The Holiday.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AN idle holiday !
How sleepy all things seemed !
There was no wind at play.
And while the foreland dreamed
A languid, leisured sea
Crawled shoreward sluggishly.
In golden indolence
Stood tree and bush and flower;
All-still and all-intense,
* The sun burned hour by hour ;
\ sea-hawk poised on high
Seemed fixed against the sky.
Men’s thoughts on lazy feet
A tired procession crept;
Through mart and city street
Felt-footed silence stept;
O’er all there hung a sense
Of life held in suspense.
Through noon a,nd afternoon
There rose no trill of bird ;
On harbor and lagoon
No leaping mullet stirred
The waters, soft as oil,
With sudden, white turmoil.
Too tired of journeying
On some lone coastal quest,
Red beak beneath his wing.
The black swan lay at rest;
Again and yet again
The far-flight called in vain.
On green and velvet moss
Awake, yet not awake,
His skin a satin gloss.
Coil, circling coil, the snake
Amid tall flowers and trees,
Bay basking at his ease.
I I hought, “All things are mute-
The sea, the trees, the soil;
The spell is absolute,
Surcease from care and toil;
Too tired to work or play
All things keep holiday.”
Then pondering mute and still,
A shadow seemed to fall —
What moved on yonder hill?
A dark-plumed funeral!
Death knows no easeful breath ;
There is no rest for Death.
N.S.W.
RODERCI QUINN.
Father Christmas.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN my days of adolescence anything in
the shape of a “permanency” I de-
tested. Bike a bee, from flower to
flower, I flew from billets. Now the billets
fly from me. In fact, of late, I can never
overtake one. But of all the jobs in which
I have engaged, my impersonation of Santa
Claus was the most rotten.
It was late in December when I awoke
one morn with an extra distaste for life.
Something seemed hanging over me —had
haunted my dreams overnight, and for the
moment I recollected not what it was.
Then memory rushed over me. I had a
job. I was going to Work —to Earn Money.
My hash proprietress knocked at my
door at that moment. She is, so she tells
me, a Wuss-leyan, and my irreligious de-
cline towards death and the “wooden ulster”
had given her conscience pause.
So she had taken advantage of me some
days before, when I was hysterically weak
after my last jag, and had called in her
“parson.”
He was a fine, bluff person, full of words
and vim, and he must have hypnotised me,
for at the end of his oratorical outburst I
found that I had placed my signature (look-
ing like a mangled centipede) on a Total
Abstinence card, and the lapel of my coat
was adorned with a fragment of blue ribbon.
Next day, the parson used his influence
for me with a city firm of drapers, with the
result that, taking me at my card’s valua-
tion, the firm had engaged me as Father
Christmas for its annual window display.
Descending that morning to the dining-
room I almost died of repulsion at sight of
breakfast. A pick-me-up would have con-
verted it into a dipsomaniac’s feast, but,
alas ! from my coat there shyly peeped the
“blue” of the abstainer, therefore
By 10 o’clock I was feeling my feet in
Toyland. Doll-land was in the same estate,
also a sanded sea-front block inhabited by
some glassy-eyed, dummy bathers in cheap
sea-wear, while some live waterfowl dese-
crated the foreground.
There was a shop-walker—unctuous to the
public, to me peremptory. He coached
me in the r6le of Santa before I made my
bow, and folded me snugly into the red
robe which historical repute has handed
down as the correct wear of the old fraud.
He hooked into position the Niagara of
white goats’ hair which foams over my
chest, having first of all caged me behind a
stifling mask. The modeller had created
this with a movable smile. It was a fear-
some thing, this mask, fit to rank for sheer
cruelty with the iron hood of the Dark Ages.
It was suffocating—it stank —being a med-
ley of goats’ hair and glue, pasteboard and
wax. It was shaped with genial knobs and
senile hollows which, jammed against the
natural protuberances of my phizog, bruised
me sore.
The venerable furrows of its brow jagged
me on the under side like glass filings. Then
it was furnished with a hidden string, which
being dragged allowed my movable grin to
broaden into laughter. Two tweaks pro-
duced loud laughter, three, uproarious merri-
ment. What four produced was never
revealed.
It took some brain-work, and a lot of the
shopwalker’s time to make me mask-per-
fect, but I was declared fit at last, and
made a triumphant arrival in a railed-off
enclosure in the very heart of Toyland, with,
overhead, a banner, announcing Christmas
novelties given away for, next to nothing.
Perhaps there is some of the natural
actor in me. At any rate.. I soon found
mvself doing before the public, some infern-
ally silly posturing, which for sheer asiniuity
was without compeer, and which was well
calculated to lower the status of Santa
Claus iu the minds of all beholders.
Armed with a bundle of tickets and aided
by an elderly little girl got up as a fairy
I worked hard in my new environment, try-
ing to live up to the “new leaf” which the
parson had turned over for me.
Pulling the hidden string and cackling
accordingly 1 soon won golden opinions on
every side. I was all aglow, partly honest
endeavor, partly rush of blood to the head,
while beads of perspiration as big as grapes
coursed over my hidden features.
It was a scene of wild excitement. Being
a free show, we were deuced popular. Sway-
ing mobs of bargain-hunters clung tenaci-
ously to the trails of my enclosure. They
watched my tickets, and hung upon my
foolish smile, my lightest beck, with colossal
interest. Then, at the hint of the fairy, who
seemed .to have the ear of the management,
I scattered free tickets, entitling the good
catches of the crowd to a dip off the “three-
penny table.” Mob rule took the field. Bike
an angry sea the women rolled and surged
round me, whirling and circling to secure a
ticket, grovelling among the public hooves
for them. While these were on all-fours,
others fought for a footing near the rail-
ings. Matrons of repute clawed like cats.
Hatbrims clashed and were knocked awry,
and hatpins fought duels on their own
account.
After one of these scrums I saw one
decorated with a wad of human hair—the
scalped, for aught I heard, being blissfully
unconscious of her loss.
Children screamed. Girls fainted. The
shopwalker, beaten out of bounds, looked
anxious and gave orders to close the front,
doors. As for me, I felt gloriously elated.
1 was the draw, the magnet that had. cor-
nered the greedy emotions of this crowd. It
was assuredly “my hour,” and I grew in
popularity. One glimpse of my red hood
and goats’-liair beard brought more tumul-
tuous souls rushing headlong from the dull
departments of the “Manchester” and “Tail-
oring” to greet me, and with one pull of my
laughter string and its resultant cackle I
made them mine for aye.
For every ticket given away we sold 100.
We were doing good business, and the
fairy’s takings were enormous. But upon
me, with my fatal dislike of a “permanency,”
the situation soon began to pall, and it was
cruelly hot in the red robe.
My “silly ass” grin was still joy to the
crowd, but to me, prisoned behind it, life
had become a ghastly, breathless affair, satu-
rated with the odor of goats—long dead —
and I longed—ye gods liow I longed-for
liquor.
Once I cornered the supercilious shop-
walking cad and demanded the right to
“wet my whistle.”
He was not altogether inhuman. He sent
warm tank water, but there’s many a slip
’twixt the mask and the lip, and.
owing to Santa’s mouth being protuberant
and mine flat and leather-lipped, but little
of the liquor reached my interior.
Thus, prisoned behind my movable smile,
dying slowly of thirst, helpless, defenceless,
I prayed that the end might come quickly.
The gaudy panorama of Christmas gifts
(there was one silly toy there which mad-
dened me—a wooden man holding a bottle
of lager), 'the heat, the crowd, the adula-
tion ! It told cruelly upon an abstainer still
in the infancy of his abstinence and far
from strong. My vows began to weaken. I
was sick of my job. I hated the fairy and
refused to take her cues —result, cessation
of free-ticket scrambles and consequent loss
of Popularity. Business began to fall off.
The shop-walker, too, kept nagging. Under
cover of pretended Christmas banalities he
would growl in undertones. “Book alive,
man. Hustle! You’re losing grip losing
trade.” „„ _ ~
“Well, what d’ you expect?” I would re-
tort. “No sale? Bot o’ stingy jays ; can t
force ’em to buy; they want free tickets all
the bally time.” , .
“Be careful, sir, be careful, he advised.
“Can’t you look pleasant? Pull the string
and give them a laugh or two.” I did so,
and, as the cackling mask once more tell
silent, he started jawing again.
“Crack a few jokes,” he said ; ‘ the crowd s
thinning.” ,
“No go, ole man,” I replied. Never made
a joke in my natural. Bife is real, life is
earnest; life is but”— —
“Don’t argue,” he cussed ; you re a frost,
sir, a Jack Frost.”
“I wish you meant it, I replied.
“Shut up, you silly fool,” he hissed.
“You’re here to hold trade, and you’d better
be careful or I’ll complain to the manage-
ment and you’ll lose your engagement.”
tie thrust his ugly face out at me. Joke
with ’em, can’t you,” he ordered.
“Not at the price.” I answered insolently.
“I’m sweated enough as it is. I’ll strike, in
a minute. Go away and get me something
to drink, or I’ll strangle you with my
rotten beard.”
“Oh, go to !”
A clear, high drawl called him from me.
It was a serpentine sylph from “the Corsets.”
He went to settle an incipient strike there
also, and in his absence, in accord with
orders from his understudy, the fairy and I
left the enclosure for a short and brilliant
season in the shop window.
The window was my stage, the fairy my
satellite. Behind me, as drop scene, a
Christmas tree; before me, the man in the
street.
Would that I had been more tractable,
less thirsty, the better to improve that
shining hour. The fairy tripped prett.il>
round me. With a wand she drew the pub-
lic’s eye to suspended advertisements:
“Santa Claus Gives Away
Christmas Novelties.”
This was but a figure of speech. We
gave nothing, save the lie to the annoum e
ment. . , , . „„
Within the shop window it was hot as
blazes. My throat felt like burning flannel,
and I muttered imprecations in my goats-
hair beard. “Laughter” was turned on ess
and less frequently. , “Boud and uproarious
had gone, mutinied long since, and at last I
gave up entirely and just stood and cussed.
The fairy turned queen’s evidence, and up
came the shopwalker. »
“Smarten up.” he hissed, under cover , of
a jest. “The crowd’s drifting—you re losing
’em —they’re going next door ; there s a clown
there, look pleasant. Baugh, man . laugh.

WHEN HEALTH
BEGINS TO GO.

From birth to death the blood is continually streaming
through the kidneys to be filtered. But when the kidneys are
weak or diseased, they begin to lose their power of filtering
the blood. Then the watery waste left in the blood begins to
spread disease to all parts of the body.
The patient feels unnaturally drowsy and heavy, and
grows irritable and miserable. The uric acid and urea begin
to settle in the muscles and joints, causing the stiffness and
pain of rheumatism. The swellings of dropsy appear; the
heart is affected ; there are pains in the back, and the hands
and feet are always cold.
Kidney disease is hard to cure because it so often reaches
a serious stage before it is found out. But if you take your
trouble in time, before the kidneys get too seriously involved
the disease may be permanently cured. At the first appear-
ance of any of the above symptoms, the patient should com-
mence with a thorough course of Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills, which act upon the kidneys and bladder like laxative
medicines act upon the bowels. The pills should be persevered
with until every sign of kidney weakness has disappeared, for
as long as the least trace remains a relapse is always to be feared.

Two Lasting Cures .

was quite a lad, and ever since I have
suffered greatly from backache. I tried
all kinds of remedies, but only with tem-
porary relief. My work used to be very
laborious, and I have had to leave it at
times on account of the intense pain.
However, Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills
cured the backache and strengthened
my back. I always tell friends who
suffer from bad backs to try Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills; they are certain to
give relief. It is two years since I used
the pills, so my cure has been well
tested.”
Mr. James confirms his previous state-
ment two years later and says: “I am
still free of the torture of backache, and
have been since Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills cured me four years ago.”
I Mrs. S. May, 48 Newland Street, Wav-

13 years since I told you that Doan’s

‘lso go, ole fellar,” I answered. “Can’t
laugh, can’t hold ’em, too blazing hot—want
a — on^y to wa *k the part.”
You’re not paid for that yet,” he mut-
tered.
“What?” I said. “Do yop threaten cpe?
■ft s rank intimidation.” Like a wolf I
glared at him through my eyelets. He came
nearer. I sniffed at him. He had’ had a
drink. A drink, and he dared preach abstin-
ence to me, an unwilling knight of the blue
ribbon.
Just then a motor shot up to the kerb.
At sight of it the shopwalker dropped his
bounce and fell to shrill pleading. “They’re
the Best People,” he squealed. “For Gor’s
sake, put a spurt on, old man. Don’t let ’em
go next door. Yes? Yes! Beer afterwards
it you like, only hold' ’em till they’re in.”
He stole swiftly away' to rub his fat hands
and bow obsequiously in the doorway. Motor-
less, common folk made way obediently, and
the party advanced with some circumstance.
First a Juno, encased in motor wraps,
holding up a pierrot child. “Daddy
Christmas, darling,” she warbled, with
supreme _ condescension. “Clap hands at
him; which of his tickets will darling have?”
Closer she came. For the first time I
was thankful for the safe if stinking cover
of my mask.
It was She. She who was once my love.
She who threw me over for a brewery. Be-
hind her waddled Avoirdupois, the brewer.
Meclianicallv as they smiled condescendingly
at my grotesqueries I pulled my string and
the mask broke into indecent mirth. The
pierrot prattled with joy. She too laughed,
sympathetic soul. Little she recked of the
thirsty ruin behind the pasteboard and the
glue. I ground my teeth and cussed, shook
my fist at the brewer; but he, silly bounder,
took it as festive by-play. Ah, gods, thus to
torture a man! Love flown. Liquor lost,
for the blue ribbon.
In my agony I trod upon the fairy and
broke a jingling “wind-bell.” Then she
sailed past me. Final insult, she was leav-
ing the window, she was going to the clown
next door. Her barrel of a husband, his
pockets stuffed with notes, rolled on in her
wake.
The shopwalker blamed me. He started
to “rouse.” I clutched him. “Gimme a
drink,” I implored; “a sawft, water, cold
tea, anything.”
He looked at me. He smelt my goats’-
hair beard. Pity was born in his slanty
eyes, but born too late. He had indeed
tried me too far. I took him by the throat.
He lost his dignity and was sore afraid.
The cheap-show public watched us in amaze.
Was this indeed pantomime, it asked, rub-
bing its eyes and jamming its nose against
the window. My enemy pleaded for mercy,
but I heeded him not. For down the street
came a lorry, laden with crates of Christmas
beer. I knew the lorry. Mick Macdonald
was driving the horse.
“Mac,” I bawled, throwing the shop-
walker into a lot of Japanese umbrellas.
“Mac, Mac.”
He heard not my anguished bellow. His
brake was on, and its screeching drowned
all other sounds.
Serene, content, all unconscious of a
friend in dire need, Mac and his jingling
cargo passed by.
Then I lost control of myself. Turning,
with one flourish I demolished the Christmas
tree.
I leaped from the window into the shop,
blazed a track through Toyland. The dummy
in the cheap bathing-suit barred the way.
I gave him what he had been looking for all
day; one between his glassy eyes, and down
he went.
I rushed through the shop. Startled girl
assistants scuttled behind counters, while
heads of departments put up their eyeglasses.
A crowd opening before my onrush, closing
in after me. followed me hotly to the
manager’s office.
The manager was there with some share-
holders, drinking iced claret. On a desk
stood a glass of liquor. I seized it. “Put
that down, sir,” thundered an over-collared
man. I needed no second invitation. I did.
Then I 'tore off my red robe and jammed the
mask on tc the manager’s ears. I made a
present of the goats’-hair beard to a duke in
spats.
Then I fled through “the boots” and “the
ready-mades” to the rear of the shop—to
Little Bourke-street and freedom.. Thence
to the little pub at the corner where, all
invitingly, stood Mick Macdonald and his
lolTy.
After my flight up Little Bourke-street a
scrap of blue ribbon rose languidly upon the
wind, soared a moment, then dropped
pathetically into the dingy gutterway.
WEEROONA.
Our Post Office.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
are dull in our post office this
morning. But, then, things are always
dull in our post office. There is a
staff of four, and the daily mail averages
10 letters. You can’t get much work or
excitement out of 10 letters, and the result
is the day goes so slowly that you would
think the clock had been dipped in treacle.
Some days things are so quiet that I think
I must be dead. My favorite roost when
I’m doing nothing (I’m always doing
nothing) is next to the empty safe, and op-
posite the file where we keep the exciting
notices about mails we never help to make
up for places we never hope to see. The
Deputy P.M.G.’s name at the foot of num-
berless notices fascinates me, and I have
often thought of sending him an insulting
letter about nothing, and so getting myself
gradually thrown out of the Department
and out of this hole. (Perhaps, though,
they would only pigeonhole it, or appoint
a Royal Commission to inquire into me!)
There is no particular reason why I should
stay here, for I am not married and don’t
wish to be. My salary is absurd, and I
hate everybody in the place—including, of
course, both messengers and the Hag who
is supposed to attend to the counter. (She
isn’t really a Hag, but she will be if she
stays. And the way she does her hair an-
noys me.) They are decent enough in their
way, and not ruder to me than they have a
right to be. (Did I mention that I was the
postmaster? Well, X am! I am also—if
it interests you—43 years old, deaf in one
ear, and going bald on top. But X don’t
suppose such personal details will interest
you. They hardly interest me, now.)
The trouble with this office is that nobody
has anything to do. We are sick of the
sight of one another. I don’t know exactly
how sick the other three are of me, but I
am sick enough of them to hate their names
on pay vouchers. If the Government could
only see us four loafers rotting in idleness
we might get healthily sacked, and so save
our souls by doing real work in some place
that isn t dead. I have thought of writing
an anonymous letter to the Department in-
forming the official head that neither I nor
my assistants have anything to do—in fact,
that the strains of a mouth-organ played
by the IJ.M.1 J .M. can be heard issuing from the
office at any hour of the day.. (By the way,
1 commandeered that mouth-organ from
young Jim, the junior messenger, and a
d —d fine fuss he made about it. You’d
never think I was the postmaster, the way
that kid talks to me.) But I’ve never cor-
nered sufficient energy to write that letter.
It seems to have fallen through, like my
scheme for starting a night-school and edu-
cating the grandfathers of the hamlet, so
that they could write letters for me to
stamp and bag. That was the Grand Idea
1 had when 1 came here first, full of en-
thusiasm—comparatively full, that is. I
suppose I must have been slow and depart-
mentally d —d, or I would never have been
sent here (Out, you brute! Where’s
that ledger? Bang! ! Oh, well, it
can stay there till that kid comes in. I
told him to leave some stones handy for
throwing at the hens! Why that woman
doesn’t keep the rotten things in her own
house ! .)
Two o’clock still! D—n it, it was two
o clock half an hour ago!. .. .Young Jim
should be back by now. Of course there’s
no use in coming a dashed thing
to do, and the sight of him cutting the
counter drives me mad. I hate, too, the
way his ears stick out. And he has a
cursed habit of whistling through his teeth.
I can’t play the mouth-organ while anyone
is whistling. But that kid should put in
an appearance now and again, if only out
of respect to me. (After all, I am the
postmaster!) Suppose someone got an-
noyed with the office and wrote the De-
partment that the messenger helped people
to kill pigs in office hours? Why, the boy
might be sacked. It would be the making
of him, of course, but look at the disgrace
of it. And I would have to write pages of
reports. And I hate writing reports—
especially since that blessed calf chewed up
all the dictionary between C and M. I
can’t trust my spelling since I’ve been here,
and with two-thirds of the dictionary eaten,
report-writing is too dangerous. They might
say down there that I wasn’t educated!
Yes, I’ll have to have a word with that kid.
He’s getting too cheeky, anyhow. It’s an
understood thing that the telegrams are to be
delivered in person by me. There is—or
there ought to be —some excitement hanging
to telegrams; and I guess, as P.M., I am
entitled to first call on any fun that’s going.
Yet that kid has been sneaking them under
my nose and rushing off with them. You
know, that’s too hot, altogether!
If there was a railway station here, we
could go down and meet the train, and 1
would have some inducement to put on my
boots at least once a day. (I think a P.M.
should put on his boots once in a while, if
only as an example to his staff.) But there
isn’t any line as yet. It’s just promised.
I don’t know how many politicians and Gov-
ernments have lied about it and given their
solemn assurances that they will get the
Transcontinental diverted, so as to link up
the place with Europe and the Old World
generally. (If I should want to go to
Europe to-morrow I would have to go all
the way to Sydney. Think of that! I’ve
tried to get indignant about it, but can’t
manage it, somehow.) On Sundays the
oldest inhabitants get up on the crags like
goats and indicate exactly to one another
where the overland express will come
whistling out of Bodger’s Gully and swoop
on the town; then how it will skate out
again in a sort of screaming curve, and rush
up Death Adder Hill straight at Europe
and South America and other places. Yet
these same old men got hysterical when, the
other day, a partly-burst motor of the cheap-
est sort came crawling down the road that,
for some mysterious reason, leads to this
blot on the map. It would never have
come, only the goggled incompetent in charge
had lost his way. But, busted as he was,
he was so annoyed at arriving at all that
he tried to insult me by saying that it was
the deadest hole he had ever struck in his
life. When I asked him if he didn’t want
to telegraph home, or something of that
sort, he thought I was trying to insult him
back, and told me to go to H—l. (I didn’t
have my boots on at the time, so I guess
he didn’t know I was the postmaster.) He
got his motor going soon afterwards, and
puffed out of the town in the opposite direc-
tion, amidst the cheers of the grateful popu-
lace. I used to walk miles down that road
when I first came here. But the drawback
about walking out of this place is that, un-
less you can think of something else,'you’ve
got to turn round and walk back. There
is no reason, of course, why people should
come back, but they do. Those who move
out, that is. After a while, you find it
easier to stay still and go without boots.
(To tell you the honest truth, I don’t know
exactly where my boots are. I have an
idea I lent them to Harry, the senior mes-
senger, last week. When the horse-faced
galoot comes back from helping the Hag to
make jam I’ll ask him. Next time he wants
to go and see a girl he can borrow somebody
else’s boots.)
But, slack as we are here, I manage to
keep this office from going to the dogs alto-
gether. When young McCocky came in yes-
terday and wanted me to take a kangaroo-
rat in exchange for three stamps, I said
• N ° !’ , J don,t care if there ** a row about
it. While I m here I’ll see that the place
keeps up to a certain standard.
Ah, well! We’ll be all right when we get
the Transcontinental!... .D—n it! it must
be more than five past two! Where’s
that mouth-organ!
N. & E. O’FEBRALL.
